The Red Page
July
Despite the war 30 sonneteers took part
in our Australian July competition. Rimes
seem to exasperate R. G, Henderson: —
I’m tired of sonnets on our dozen months.
What do I know of sonnets t That some are quite as
Hot as tiie “Answers to Correspondents’’ column.
Rime cannot do them justice, and I have no
Time to acquire their fit vocabulary,
Not so the rest. They freeze my marrow solid.
Cotton for some—Australian wool for others.
Limejuice for February—June wants something
warmer.
That’s my poetic calendar ! So —July !
Hail, thou midpoint of Winter, months of coughs,
Colds, influenza, football in all the codes,
Hats that can stand the rain, dark clothes, stout
boots
(Sale bargains mostly), tangoes and wliate’er else
Folds in thy gloomy bosom —Hail! July 1
R. G. Henderson.
And “Mick Much” excels himself in his
final simile; —
Jooly’s orlriglit, the days are jist the thing
Fur solid yakker till yer day. is done ;
Uz fur the nights, there’s allez whips o’ fun
In ’ops wif righto heifers ut yer wing.
Then sittin’ tight w’en damcin’s ud its fling,
En kiddin’ to yerself yer jist the one—
Until some screwin’, crook son uv a gun
The diner grabs, en’ show yer’ve ’ad a string.
The ’Urdles now, en' Steeple’s all the go
Ter give the blokes a charnce ter do their dough.
Then fetch a skite their forchin jist got pipped!
It ain’t too dusty livenin’ aroun’
These early morn's wif Jack Frost on the groun’,
Uz if the moonlight froze w’ere it wuz tipped.
Mick Much.
Our sympathy, too, goes out to “Phillip
Phrozeout”: —
Oh, ’oly wars! Ain’t ole July dead tough!
Me pot er mizree’s full; I’m close up blind
Wiv smoke frum soddin wood; I’ve arfer mind
Ter crawl rite up er ’oiler log en’ snuff!
Thet’s ’ow I feel. I’ve got th’ flamin’ ’uff!
It seems like years since good ole Solly shined.
It’s pelted rain ier days, en’, struth! th’ wind
Is blowin’ strate orf liozzie’s frozen scruff!
I’m short er nap, too —thet’s ernuther cow!
’Bout 2 a.m. w’en Jolino’s nippin keen,
Phil’s got ter rise en’ lite er fire er die.
I ain’t er bloke ter make er silly row,
But, struth, sum gems uv purest ray obscene
Er fired frum roun’ me camp-fire et July.
Phillip Phrozeout.
Good average sonnets were submitted by
Charles P. Pettinger, H.W.C., R. Blantyre
and Leslie Lee (2). The sonnets of a higher
class are noticeable this month for their
unevenness. Few are good all through. Some
start off happily, but get chilblains on their
sestettes; others take an octette before they
thaw. In the frozen sestette class are: A.
Gore-. Tones and S. Elliott-Napier, with a
purple alliterative chilblain on his last line;
while Duncan Brown and Emo are frost-
bitten in their octettes. “Aeneas Hayseed”
supplies a straightforward sonnet that looks
too much like a seedsman’s catalogue. Son-
nets by Lilia Gormhuille McKay, Syd. C.,
Nina Murdoch, Mary A. K. Robb and “Jul-
ian” remain for final adjudication. Mary
A. K. Robb begins with a fine image, but
descends to prose in her sestette. There is
more poetry, despite the space taken to
develop his first theme, in Syd. C.’s simple
effort, with its lilting promise of spring:—
A quivering veil of silver rain is thrown
O’er boughs that lift their gold against the grey.
And clings in tremulously crystal spray
To wraith-like blossoms magically blown
In leafless orchards where the earth is strown
With the pale emerald of her new array.
And hark! the river plunging on its way
Thunders in deep, insistent monotone.
But, cold and keen, the wind uprising brings
Snatches of warbled joy that stir the blood.
The bleat of lambs, the breath of flowers new—
And back the cloudy pall impetuous flings,
Baring the wet earth to a golden flood,
Poured from a soft infinity of blue.
Syd. C.
“Julian” just fails by bis carelessness
about the caesura in lines 12 and 13 and his
banal last line :— ■
Now icy spears assail the patient Earth,
Hurtling in shotvers, yet her gracious heart
Bids hut a hundred lovely braveries start.
The sodden fields see the white sundew's
birth,
The pinlc-tipped saplings press to nobler
girth, -
Lichen and fungi ply their fairy art,
And passionate heaths that love and live
apart,
Fling their last challenge to the wintry
dearth.
White mists surge up at dawn, and all the
heights,
Like gleaming isles, float in the silver flood,
Shifted o'er purple gulfs by arrowy lights.
Earth heaves, soft riot parts the brooding
air,
Buds stir, there is a quickening of bright
blood,
And August comes, the fairest of the fair.
Julian.
Of the two sonnets left that 6f Lilia
McKay is more sincere than the somewhat
•elaborate simile of Nina Murdoch. Had the
prize not been offered for an Australian son-
net, it would have gone to Lilia McKay, an
Auckland competitor. She exquisitely pic-
tures a July, but it is a Maoriland July.
JULY.
By T.H.I.A GORMHUILLE Mc KAY.
By fragrant tokens on the wintry way
I know she has been here, timid and fleet,
Too frail to linger yet and boldly meet
Rough Winter's jealous wrath. Saw you
her, pray?
A primrose wreath upon her brow, and gay
Red wind-flowers at her breast, all dewy-
sweet? . .
Heard you the tread of light, wind-driven
feet f
Did such a maid delight your eyes to-day?
Ay! on the highway in the windy morn
\Ve saw her pass, in gown of green arrayed,
Wit a tossing hair and sheaves of daffodils;
But as we spied her Winter's clam’rous horn
Mustered the storm-clouds. Suddenly dis-
mayed.
She fled. The grey rain hid the misty hills.
The best Australian, as distinguished from
Australasian, sonnet is that of Nina Mur-
doch, 77 Ben Boyd-road, Neutral Bay,
Sydney:—
JULY.
By NINA MURDOCH.
The fig-leaves fall before the wind in
sh owers —
A cutting wind that rushes from the west —•
The snow lies thick on Kosciusko’s breast,
And on the coast the twisted ti-tree cowers
Before the rain! Lot in an instant, bowers
Are filled with sunshine as at Spring's behest,
And in a vision, August, golden-tressed,
Passes with joyous arms weighed do ion with
flowers.
July is like an adolescent youth
That wakes the echoes iv-ith his boisterous
shouts,
And then falls dreaming for a sudden space
Lost in a maze ’twixt wonder and the truth
Because of joy and strange desires and
doubts
That move, him at a passing maiden’s face.
Dawning of a horrid doubt as to the Divine
Right.
(A Cartoon by Max Beerbohm.)
Russia
The Mainsprings of Russia, by Maurice
Baring (Nelson, per Dymock’s Library), is
not Red Russia , The Real Russia, Russia as
She Is, or Russia as She Isn't. It is merely
a truthful account of Russia by a. man who
knows the Russians. The Slav is a white
man; he has nothing in common with any-
thing Tartar, Mongol or Semitic, though
from the 13th to the 16th century Russia
was under the dominion of the Mongols.
During the period from the first Mongol
invasion till their expulsion by the Slavs in
the 16th century, Russia acted as a buffer
between Europe and the Oriental barbarians :
it was Russia that saved Europe from the
Mongols.
The most important factor of Russian life
is the Russian peasant. He owns the
greater part of the arable land, owns and
tills it. This fact is practically unknown
outside Russia. The peasant was, indeed, a
serf; but serfdom in Russia was not a
slavery resulting from conquest or difference
in race: it was merely due to economic con-
ditions.
“When the legislation which ultimately
produced serfdom was framed, it was not
regarded by those who framed it as a per-
manent solution of the relations between
landowner and peasant, but only as a tem-
porary makeshift. The result —slavery—
was unforeseen. The peasants never, through
nearly two centuries of slavery, lost sight
of this fact. Moreover, they kept fast hold
of the idea that the land was theirs, that the
land belonged to the. people w r ho tilled it.”
The emancipation of the serfs, in 1861,
meant chiefly that a portion of the land
which the peasant considered to be his by
right was restored to him. Over 350,000,000
acres passed from the hands of the land-
owners into the hands of the peasants for
ever. On an average each peasant received
from eight to 11 acres. The procedure
seems to Australian minds like a gigantic
resumption of estates for closer settlement.
The landowners were paid for the land by
the Government: the peasants had to pay
back to the State in instalments, over a
period of more than 50 years. The State
acted as banker to both parties, paying the
landowners ready money and advancing the
money to the peasants. In 1907 the pay-
ments by the peasants were cancelled. After
the emancipation the land was owned by
the peasants in common, as they had always
held it. All the land that was fit for culti-
vation was divided among the peasants,
according to the number of taxed members in
each household. In 1904 agrarian riots all
over Russia led to every landowner selling
a certain amount of his remaining land to
the peasants. Now the peasant can leave
the Commune, can become the permanent
owngr of his individual share of the land, or
can. exchange his strips of Commune land for
an allotment and start a farm. It is evi-
dent, therefore, that the peasant is the most
important unit in the nation. In the long
run the peasant will become the owner of all
the arable land in Russia.
Jt
Anybody can become a noble in Russia.
He has simply to reach a certain definite
step in the army or the civil service; and
the service is open to anybody who can pass
a certificate examination at the end of his
school time. The two consequences of this
arrangement are that the nobility in Euro-
pean Russia number about 600,000, and there
is nothing aristocratic about such a nobility.
The Russian noble is a State servant.
Originally the servant of the State was
rewarded by the possession of land, and soon
the corollary followed that the landowners
must serve the State. Their land was merely
their pay. Thus no landed or territorial
aristocracy came into existence. “In Russia
there are no feudal castles, no families tak-
ing their names from places, and compara-
tively few stone houses in the country.”
The Russian landowner is an absentee land-
owner ; and in the end the nobility as a caste
of landowners will disappear.
But with such a democratic arrangement,
why is it that Russia is not democratic '! In
practice there is a gigantic machine worked
by a governing class of officials which is
absolutely uncontrolled by public opinion.
“Though the system is theoretically favorable
to the advancement of merit, it is a thousand
times more favorable to mediocrity, routine,
office-hunting, officialdom, red-tape, to the
stifling of all individual initiative, and the
shirking of all moral responsibility. The
chief evil result of the system was the
uncontrolled arbitrary character of the cen-
tral government and the local administration
as carried on by the provincial governors and
other Government officials.”
There is a similar answer to the question :
“Why are the Russian people discontented V”
The reason is that in 1005 explicit promises
were made to the Russian people, which, if
carried out, would insure their complete
political liberty and full rights of citizenship.
These promises have in some cases not been
carried out at all, and in other cases they
have been canned out partially, or according
to the letter and not according to the spirit.
The Government’s attitude toward the Duma
is to do everything to prevent the Duma
becoming a vital factor in the State, and to
try to convert it into a passive annex of
the Government. And the local administra-
tion has the effect of exasperating the people.
The secret police, too, must live; and if they
did not represent the discontent as more for-
midable than it is they might lose their jobs.
And not even a secret policeman likes losing
his job.
£
A word of warning is given by M. Baring
to those who judge the Russians by the works
of their novelists of genius. The characters
of Dostoievsky are Russians; but they are
not average Russians, any more than Lear
is a portrait of the average Englishman, or
Faust the image of the ordinary German
bourgeois, even when he is not laying float-
ing mines. The average Russian is well-
educated, cheerful, sociable, intensely gregari-
ous, hospitable, talkative, expansive, good-
humored and good-natured. He is pre-
eminentlv indulgent, the most tolerant of
human beings, and as a juryman addicted to
verdicts of acquittal.
“Ultimately, it is_ safe to say that the
achievement of political liberty in Russia
will depend not on dynamite and the death
of revolutionaries, nor on the measures of a
statesman, however far-seeing and however
wise, but on the will and desire of the average
man. On the day the average man really
desires political liberty he will get it.”
Of the intelligentsia Baring remarks:
“They are extremely well educated;
infinitely, incomparably better educated than
the average Englishman. They are some-
times over-educated. .. .The average Russian
of the educated middle class is so much
better educated than the average educated
Englishman that comparison would be
5i11y....1f you took an average Russian
schoolboy of 13, and put him at an English
public school, he would find the work given
to an average English schoolboy of 13 not
only easy, but childish In Russia, the
average reading public and the novel-reading
public is on a much higher level than the
average English-reading and novel-reading
public, and the average literature food sup-
plied to it is higher also; the average
Russian novel or story never descends
to the level of silliness which you
find in the great majority of English
magazines The Russian stage is on
a far higher intellectual level than the
English stage, and the Russian theatre-going
public is incomparably more intelligent than
the English theatre-going public.”
“Russians often single out laziness and
the want of practical energy as a national
failing. But the defence of Sevastopol, the
creation of the Trans-Siberian Railway, and
the transport of troops over a single line
during war time, are examples of abnormal
energy in the domain of achievement; and in
the persons of Peter the Great, Suvorov and
Skobeliev Russia has given to the world
examples of terrific and explosive energy*
Stern stuff must exist somewhere in the
Russian character, or else the Russian Em-
pire would not be there to testify to the
fact. The Russian Empire is the result of
something, and it is there.”
Ultimately even Germany will have to
admit this fact.
Poems Translated from the Irish
I SHALL NOT DIE FOR YOU.
0 woman, shapely as the siban,
On your account / shall not die,
The men you've slain. —a trivial clan —■
Were less than I.
I ask me shall I die for these,
For blossom-teeth and scurlet lips?
And shall that delicate swan-shape
Bring me eclipse?
Well shaped the breasts and smooth the skin,
The cheeks are fair, the tresses free;
And yet I shall not suffer death,
Cod over me!
Those even brows, that hair like gold.
Those languorous tones, that virgin way;
The flowing limbs, the rounded heel
Slight men betray.
Thy spirit keen through radiant mien,
Thy shining throat and smiling eye,
The little palm, thy side like foam —*
I cannot die!
0 woman, shapely as the swan,
In a cunning house hard-reared teas I;
O bosom white, O well-shaped palm,
I shall not die.
■—Padraic Colura.
THE STARS STAND UP.
The stars stand up in-the air,
The sun and the moon arc set,
'The sea that ebbed dry of its tide
Leaves no single pebble wet;
The cuckoo keeps saying each hour,
That she, my Storeen, is fled—
O Girl of the brave, free tresses,
Far better had you struck me dead!
Three things have I learned through love,
Sorrow, and death, and pain.
My mind reminding me daily
I never shall see you again;
You left me no cure for my sickness,
Yet I pray, though my night be long—
My sharp grief! and my heart is broken —
That Cod may forgive your wrong.
She was sweeter than fiddle and lute,
Or the shining of grass through the dew,
She was soft as the blackbird’s flute
When the light of the day is new;
From, her feet on the lone hilltop
I have heard the honey dropping;
Why, Girl, did you come to my door:
Or why could you not be stopping?
From “The Poem Book of the Gael.”
You pay your subscription to a circulating
library, and find before you an enormous
number of novels (chiefly) from which to
make your selection. But these novels are not
all the latest novels published. In the latest
issue of The Athenaeum, the London
weekly that notices every London publica-
tion, 25 novels are listed ; and these include
but a small proportion of the novels pub-
lished in the United States and Australia.
But these 25 new novels are not imported
by the Australian subscription library. Only
a selection is bought for your selection. Who
makes that selection of the sort of novels you
will like, and on what principles is that
selection made? The manager of the library
is responsible. In the case of known authors
his judgment is easy. He knows the demand
for a particular popular author, and orders
the dozen or the hundred copies necessary.
In the case of an unknown author the library
manager has had a proof copy long before
the book is published. He cannot be ex-
pected to read every one of these new novels:
he is compelled to dip. The manager of one
Sydney library confesses his method. He
reads, carefully, the first four pages. If that
instalment interests him, he dips further
ahead: if still interested, he orders a copy
or copies. But the main test is the first
four pages. They must interest —or the
new novel is omitted from his list. A hint,
here, to our young authors: make your first
four pages interesting, stimulate your reader
within that limited space, lure her on....
You see the same method of selection in the
average library subscriber. She takes up
one of the latest novels, skims the first para-
graph. the first few pages. “I’ll take this
one, please....” A low basis of judgment,
but the subject-matter is the popular novel
of commerce suitable for vague feminine
reading.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Retnrn of the Lost Legions.
Anglo-Australiiius are hurrying from England in
great numbers. Eight hundred left for Australia
tliis week in the outward-bound I*. & <k steamer,
and another 800 are hooked to follow in the next
Orient ship. Il'ttr rahli'.
Titov are coming back in shiploads
To the place that gave them birth;
They are heading for their home-land
Front the confines of the earth
Which suggests the gloomy maxim
That a super-tax is far
Less effective in its action
Than a European war.
They are pouring out of England
In an ecstasy of haste;
Folks who formerly were leisured
Now have not an hour to waste.
Unto ev’ry soul amongst them
Home lias grown extremely sweet —
Not to mention the discomfort
Which arises front cold feet.
Oh ! The English towns are pleasant,
And the English fields are fair.
But. the absentee abhors them
When the battle-bugles blare.
And a patriotic fervor,
Which has long been dumb or drunk,
Is aroused once more within hint
By the workings of Bint* Funk.
N.S.W.
SINGEMOINE.
of Babs, Her Dimple.
Let sages prate of the perfect grace
Of a life well ordered, a mind at rest:
Let spinsters boast of neat virtues placed
Out of Hie dust in a chaste, chill breast.
What can such noodles have known or
guessed
( What had they gained, had their hearts
been bolder!)
Of a kiss of worship devoutly pressed
In the dimple coy on a rounded shoulder?
Priests who stalk at a solemn pace,
Still there is time to be happy and blest!
I’rtides who cry that delight is base.
While you’ve a chance be your sins con-
fessed !
Up and be joyful! For joy is the best
(lift of the gods till we tumble and moulder,
Down with the roots. This truth you can
test.
In the dimple coy on a rounded shoulder !
There’s fun enough for the human race,
Gold enough in the glad, warm nest.
Kick your blue devils out into space!
Laugh, and you’ll shake the grey load off
your chest!
I know a nook where the Graces nest,
Frying to men as their blood runs colder:
You’ll find it (delay is a curse and a pest)
In the dimple coy on a rounded shoulder.
Envoy.
Ilabs! by the duns and the deuce obsessed,
Hear your lover I who's growing older),
Swear that Love lurks with unfaltering zest
In the dimple coy on a rounded shoulder!

FRANK MORTON.
The Roving Kilchen
As the result of experiments carried out at camps
of training', the military authorities have decided
that travelling kitchens are a practical advantage,
and it has been decided that a number of them shall
be purchased for distribution throughout Australia.
—News Hem.
And now a-down the rural roads the travel-
ling chef will go :
He’ll eat the bread live miles ahead though
ten back it was dough :
lie’ll boil the beef at Rillybong, the spuds
at Overflow.
'Phe creaking wheels will be attuned to some
old kettle song.
What time they turn, and ever turn, the
winding road along.
Retween the Army’s city base and Kangaroo-
-1 a.gong.
The roadside kids will gather from the
humpies where they dwell.
And watch tin l kitchen pass as though be-
neath some magic spell—
For where a kid and kitchen meet the world
goes wondrous well!
The lonely triulger in the dust will sniff from
out the breeze
The fragrance of the juicy roast, the glorious
scent of cheese.
And all the perfumes wafted out from boiling
pork and peas.
lie’ll breathe great mouthfuls of the air. and
lick his lips, and start
I’ursuing. like old Nemesis, that quaint old
jolting cart.
With emptiness behind his belt, but fullness
in his heart.
For in that travelling kitchen will his hopes
be set a-stack :
Y\ here all the roads lead out from home, but
seldom one leads back—
lie’ll even glimpse a wandering pub along
tin' Sunset Track !
And so in fancy I can see where rural road-
ways wind.
And where the wheels eternally the gleam-
ing gravel grind,
A joyful welcome far ahead—a hunger march
behind !
Ret s hope that where the ways are long, and
where the plains are wide.
That roving kitchen’s chef will keen l:is>
larder well supplied—
And we who plod the pinch-gut path be asked
to step inside!

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.

Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.

all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
, The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
dales or Sketches (those dealing until Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say. 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. Put
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmissioji of
MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 20, 1914.
THE TRUCE OF GOD AND THE
ABSURDITY OF MAN.
In days long gone by, when the arm of the
King was short, and his writ ran stiff, and
men often fended for themselves and slew
their enemies as occasion offered, the Roman
Catholic Church devised the Truce of God.
Private enmity was suspended from the
middle of Saturday to the middle of Mon-
day—just as if the rich city merchant was
taking the barmaiden to Manly for a week-
end. Later, the Truce of God was extended,
and private murder wasn’t allowed, under
penalty of the ban of the Church and the
reprobation of all decent people, between
Wednesday morning and Monday morning.
The Church knew that it couldn’t wholly
obliterate private warfare. The attempt
would have overset its authority. But it
endeavored to establish a restful interval.
In like fashion the embai-rassed Irish land-
lord of the period of George IV., William
IV. and the early days of Victoria, was a
“Sunday man.” He could go out on Sun-
day, that day representing his Truce of
God, when he was exempt from arrest for
debt. Now, by reason of the great war, a
Truce of God is proposed among politicians
in Australia. The idea is that parties in
the State and Federal Parliaments should
coalesce in a limited sort of way; and that,
while the military trouble lasts, political
differences should be drowned amicably in
the pot of patriotism. Matters which are
liable to lead to dissension, such as the
tariff, the loan problem, the route of the
Port Darwin railway, leasehold or freehold
distribution of public lands, Federal or State
ownership of railways, uniform railway
gauge, public or private steamship owner-
ship, the establishment of State-owned cities
on our neglected ports, and many other
matters which are liable to lead to quarrels
are to be set asid.e.
The question arises whether there may
not be too much Truce. Patriotism, in an
emergency, doesn’t wholly consist in a coali-
tion of parties, who will vote money for the
support of an expeditionary force, and sit
down hard to watch its departure, and cheer
when it leaves, and then do nothing save
offer the Empire their best wishes. Things
have to ’se done if the country is to be
kept in a state to carry its share of the wax-
burden, and to maintain its people during
the period of disorganisation which war
creates, even though the field of battle may
be a long way off. And if strife comes
right to our shores —which may happen, for
strife is an uncertain creature —a great
many things will have to be done. One
of the first griefs which struck Australia
when the guns began to go off was the
closing down of a number of mines which
have been in the habit of selling their
raw or partially refined products to Ger-
man manufacturers. There is no cause
why these products should not be dealt with
in Australia, or why this country should
depend on Germany, but through a fail-
ure in the tariff, or by reason of something
about which political parties have failed to
agree, this country depends largely on Ger-
many. And because Germany is our enemy
hundreds of good Australian citizens have
become unemployed and have to rely on
such assistance as the rest of the commu-
nity can render them. In a few more
weeks thousands of Australian citizens may
become unemployed for the same reason,
and be compelled to lie back against public
charity at a time when public charity isn’t
feeling very good itself because its divi-
dends have ceased. If the war lasts fox-
years the situation will become serious.
Supposing the Truce of God continues, and
Freetraders and Protectionists decide to
sink their differences about the tariff until
Peace reigns supreme, and devote them-
selves wholly in the meantime to watching
the . departure of troops, and refuse to
inquire, in profound respect for the Truce,
why so many Australians are out of work,
it may be a very fine and pious and patriotic
business. There is something beautiful in
the spectacle of political parties embracing
each other on the shore when the soldiers
set forth. But the pai-ties shouldn’t become
so friendly that they must cease arguing
about how to raise the money to pay for the
soldiers.
* * * *
The first days of the war have shown
that there is something rotten about Aus-
tralia’s tariff, or its loan nolicy, or its
manufacturing. policy, or its general
methods. It is obviously a public matter
of some sort. And if our Australian
political . patriots refuse to find out
how it is that the Germans can bring
so many Australian citizens —who are
also British citizens —to want by a brief
suspension of business, they are liable to
appear futile and wholly absurd.
* * * *
Australia is about as far away from the
sound of the guns as any other part of the
British Empire. It hasn’t seen a foreign
soldier on the horizon. It hasn’t heard the
report of a hostile rifle. The British terri-
tories haven’t been invaded, and the Mother
Country has been less heavily involved in
the war than any of its Allies. Yet Aus-
tralia’s loans can’t be floated at the old
address on anything like reasonable terms
because Europe wants its loose cash to
finance its armies. The unfinished railways
which are dependent on the loans may
presently be suspended, and that will
greatly ci-ipple the community. Even the
inrush of chiffons and picture hats and
giddy underwear is diminishing. Drapery
girls and such-like picturesque creatures are
being thrown out of work because we can
no longer sell goods to our enemies in order
that we may buy or borrow from our friends.
If Australia was a country which smelted
and refined its own ores, made its own
Woollens and cottons and silks, built its own
clothes and hats and boots, constructed its
own rails and locomotives and ships, paid its
way out of its own revenue (or at least
floated its own loans), and did what trade
was essential with its own vessels, this
remote scuffle iu Europe Wouldn’t have
begun to affect it yet. Australia Would
have at least 30,000,000 people instead of
less than 5.000,000. It would be able to
supply 100,000 trained soldiers for the
defence of the Empire instead of 20,000
volunteers. And it wouldn’t look iu the
eye of public insolvency because of the
debts that are falling due.
The whole matter of imperial patriotism
consists in building up Australia so that
it will be a strong, solvent, self-dependent
section of the British Empire. If it is to
go to pieces whenever Germany can’t buy
its ores and Britain can’t lent it money
with which to buy foreign underclothing,
and France and Belgium can no longer pur-
chase its wools because the men employed
in their woollen factories have gone to the
war, then Australia is a mere pauper and
parasite. If peace between local parties
means the abandonment of any attempt
to find out why this country is of such
small value, it might be better to go back
to the old party strife and call the Truce
of God a failure.
The present state of things might be
expected to lead to a great increase in
Australian manufactures, a great decrease
in Australia’s dependence on German ore-
buyers and Hebraic money-lenders, and a
vast advance in the country’s population.
But no one can be expected to put up huge
factories in which to manufacture the goods
which we can’t at present get from Europe,
or to treat the raw products which we
can’t at present sell to Europe, if the coun-
try is to go back to the old, slovenly, ineffi-
cient order of things as soon as the war
is over. Then the new factories, would be
a bad debt and the new industries would be
dead. Our chance of being a really for-
midable section of the Empix-e would again
depend on our luck in selling crude ores
abroad and our chance of borrowing money
in foreign parts. Also the Sydney Tele-
graph and the Herald and the Bruce
Smith party would probably go back to
their old attitude, and preach the old doc-
trine that we should devote ourselves to the
production of raw material, such as hides ,
and timbpr, and depend on more “advanced”
nations like Kaiser William’s country for
the products of a higher civilisation.
* * * *
The situation, So far as The Bulletin
can see it, is embodied in these few proposi-
tions :
(1) Out of an intense regard for the Empire which
is holding up a good cause against Germany there is
to be a Parliamentary peace, and all the ordinary
controversies, of which the fiscal controversy is the
chief, are to be hung up.
(2) Therefore no effort is to be made to find out
why such a large proportion of the Australian mining
population depends on a hostile race to boil down
its products, ond is faced with hunger as soon as the
hostile race becomes unusually hostile.
(3) Or to find o.'t why it must simply sit down
and keep on hungering till the foe is pleased to leave
off being- hostile.
(4) Or to discover why the greatest wool-produc-
ing country in the world is to have its wool left
on hand as unsaleable stock, and (if the war proves
to be a matter of long duration) to clothe itself in
old bags.
(5) Or to ascertain why a great gold-producing
country is liable to become a crippled member of the
British power because it can no longer borrow gold
from places which don’t prod’'" l -Id.
(6) Or to inspect the reasons why its railway
system, which is a very conspicuous feature of its
share in defence, is rendered so inefficient by reason
of diverse gauges and divided control.
(7) Or to look into a multitude of other things.
Australia doesn’t want, at this crisis, to
have Ministries shoved out to-day and
shoved in to-morrow by reason of the old
political controversies. But there was
never a time when it could less afford to
drop these controversies, because, unless
the tariff que,- tion and the borrowing ques-
tion, and the question of State or Federal
railway ownership and the problem of
the route of the Port Darwin railway
and a number of other matters are
decided in haste, Australia may, before
the conflict ends, be wholly incapaci-
tated from doing its duty. Therefore this
paper again puts in its plea for the Elective
Ministry on the Swiss pattern. That sort
of Ministry would be fixed in office for
three years, whether its measures were
rejected or not. Therefore it could deal
with every question which bears, however
indirectly, on the efficiency of Australia,
and get a Parliamentary decision upon it,
without the risk that sudden changes of
portfolio-holders would create trouble at a
Ume when trouble should be avoided. The
Truce of God is a great idea, provided it
is carried out logically. Let us, for once, be
logical. There is nothing in the written
law. to prevent every Legislature in Aus-
tralia electing a Ministry with a proviso that
it will last as long as the Legislature lasts.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.)
THE ALLEGED NEW POLAND.
Russia’s political methods are often
regarded as coarse and heavy and bear-like,
and plantigrade, yet the Big Cold Country
strikes the right moment about as often as
anybody else. It was announced the other
day, in a quiet sort of fashion, that Home
Rule would be granted to Russian Poland.
Whereupon Russian Poland sat up and sang
the local National Anthem, and became more
enthusiastic about the supply of men to the
Russian army that it ever was before. Inci-
dentally Austrian and Prussian' Poland
were necessarily seized with the idea that it
might be a good thing to be attached to
Russia, and to form a part of a resurrected
and autonomous Polish State, with its own
army and a large measure of its own Govern-
ment, and with the Czar as sovereign.
Poland has no old royal family of its own,
like the Stuarts or the Bourbons or the
Orleans crowd, or the that
it wants to restore. The Jagellons and
the Sobieskis are long since extinct. They
represented, in a sort of fashion, hereditary
ruling races, but they were an exception.
Most of the later Polish kings were mere
elected shadows. Often, like Stephen
BaThorY, and WENceslas of Bohemia, and
Plenry of Valois, and Sigismund of Sweden,
and Augustus of Saxony, they were for-
eigners. No one in Poland wants nowadays
to hunt up a Poniatowskl and restore the
traditions of the last monarch, for if there is
any Poniatowskl left he is probably a
decayed person living in a boarding-house.
With Uo old kings to fight for, the Polish
pride will probably be fairly well satisfied
with a local legislature and a local defence.
The offer of these concessions will almost
certainly put a charge of dynamite under a
corner of the house in Austria and Germany.
* * * *
Two countries which have suffered greatly
through the ignorance of people whose infor-
mation is 50 years old are Russia and Aus-
tralia. There are still folk who fancy that
almost everybody in Russia is sent to Siberia,
and that almost everybody in Australia is a
convict sent out from England, -and duly
flogged on arrival. The information is less
accurate than it might be. The Polish
masses, despite all their wrongs and hard-
ships have always had some traces of kindly
feeling towards Russia. When Frederick
the Great, of Prussia, proposed the First
Partition it was Russia which tried to keep
together the miserably decayed State—a
Slate which had made the payment of taxes
a voluntary matter, and allowed its army
and police and courts of justice to disband
for lack of salaries, and left its king with
no means of living save what he could borrow
from foreign ambassadors, and established
complete liberty—even the right of insurrec-
tion, for its 200,000 “nobles.” and complete
slavery for almost everybody else. Russia
'tried in a high-handed fashion to prop up
and reform this amazing wreck of anarchy
and beggary and lawlessness, so that it might
be a buffer against Frederick. Having failed,
it stole all of Poland that it could get, on
the simple and obvious plea that if Prussia
secured the lot its guns would almost com-
mand St. Petersburg, and the need to keep
the Prussian guns a long way off was plain
to the humblest understanding. If the
Polish carcase couldn’t defend itself, mainly
because of the treasons and dissensions of
its innumerable “nobles,” somebody had to
bury it.
Russian Poland was allowed, for a number
of years after the conquest, to have its own
'Parliament of a sort —-composed, unfortu-
nately, of the same old “nobles” —and its
own army, and a considerable measure of
Home Rule. In this respect it received
better treatment than the other divisions of
the unfortunate country. The methods
might be crude and uncouth, but they were the
methods of the time, and were the methods best
known at the time. It was only when the
amazing horde of “nobles” proved utterly
unmanageable that Russia fell on them like
an avalanche. Some of them went to
Siberia. Some went to Paris and London
and became music-teachers and dancing-
masters. This gang of dispossessed slave-
owners was so vast that many people mis-
took it for the whole Polish nation gone into
exile. Meanwhile the lands of the exiles
were to a great extent divided among their
serfs, and the real Pole —the unlettered, in-
articulate toiler, who couldn’t write patriotic
verse or deliver heroic speeches, and who
had mostly lived in a hole underground—
began to get a show. He hated the Russian
because he was of a foreign race and a
s trahge religion, and because liis policeman
was arrogant and his soldier not too kind.
Vet,. by way of a sort of under-current, he
realised that the Russian had released him
from slavery to some extent, and put him in
the way of having three square meals per
day—or at least two. Also he occasionally
had a drink, thouglj the liquor wasn’t of the
best.
* * * •
The habit of lamenting about the wrongs
of the expatriated Polish noble,' 1 in his capa-
city of Polish patriot, gradually declined.
The nations of Western Europe grew tired
of trying to spell his name, which was mostly
16 consonants and one vowel; and sometimes
he had sense enough to call himself Brown
or Smith or Boleslas McDougal, and to
sink liis -lost dignity and his ancient unin-
telligibleness. Even the British Government
became weary of paying allowances, however
small, to the outcasts, and giving them
house-room in barracks, such as Walter
Besant described the situation when he
wrote “By Celia’s Arbor.” So the outcasts
began to get work. They grew less and less
sanguine over their policy of rushing occa-
sionally back to Poland and calling the people
to arms, because the people became less and
less inclined to take up the cudgel and the
scythe, and follow their incompetent
decayed old proprietors in hopeless in-
surrections. . The Polish provinces developed,
in time, into the richest and most
densely-peopled section of the Russian
Empire. Their position was far better, so
far as material comfort went, than in the
days of the old squalid independence. Still,
there always remained the Russian police-
man and the feeling of conquest and subor-
dination.
* * * *
If a new Polish Legislature is established,
based on a wide suffrage, instead of the old
Diet of “nobles,” it will probably shake up
Central Europe in a surprising fashion. The
great ambition of the Poles is to get as much
as possible of their old kingdom together
again, and Russian Poland may become the
rallying point. This isn’t a time when either
Austria or Germany wants any section of
its subjects to find a rallying-point under a
hostile flag. Poland may prove another Ire-
land, with this difference —that the north-
eastern section of Ulster may be fighting to
get into the Home Rule movement , rather
than fighting to stay out of it. Therein lies
no small difference, and when Europe is full
of differences it must annoy our enemies to
strike a fresh one.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Spirit that Makes Revolutions.
The Sydney Telegraph was responsible for
the following indisputable bit of horse-sense
last week
A crisis like the present will always discover a
type... .anxious to discover how they may selfishly
turn the public difficulty to their own advantage. In
a neighboring State the carcase butchers announce
that they will have to raise the price of meat....
It is they and their like who in every country make
the Socialists and inflame the mad passions of
anarchy.
In the same issue appeared a report of the
annual meeting of the shareholders of the
Australian Gaslight Co., at which it was
announced that though dividends of 9 per
cent, were to be paid on the A and B
shares, and of 7 per cent, on the C shares;
though the said dividends included income
tax charges, and so freed individual share-
holders from further liability in this direc-
tion ; and though there was the tidy balance
of £11,545 IDs. 7d. to be carried forward,
after the paying of the above-quoted divi-
dends, yet the price of gas was to be in-
creased to 3s. lOd. per 1000 . feet. The
A. G. Co. is in a far more favorable position
than the Melbourne carcase butchers, from
many points of .view. Its monopoly is prac-
tically complete. People can do without
fresh meat for weeks, and suffer no serious
inconvenience. Gas the majority of them
simply must have. The butcher who sup-
plies bad meat is shunned; if it is very bad
he is prosecuted. If he over-charges, his
customers deal elsewhere. Debtors let 'him
in, and legal redress is liable to be more pro-
fitless, so far as he is concerned, than bitter,
though futile, blasphemy followed by in-
action. The A. G. Co. is in a position to
supply whatever sort of gas it likes; and
some of its goods are very, very bad. The
variety, for example, that the writer has
been paying for during this winter failed
miserably, most of the time, to warm the
heater of his humble bathroom. If there is
any delay in settling up for this absurd ser-
vice a “collector,” without the courtesy, bul
with roost of the other characteristics of a
professional blackmailer, transpires and
makes things unpleasant. If there is further
delay the gas is cut off and the payment of
an impost is required before it is turned on
again. So about the only bad debts the A. G.
Co. ever incurs are in connection with those
who are prepared to go back to wood-stoves
and lamps. The country is faced, at the
present time, as the Telegraph notes, with
a crisis. The latter is inevitably going to
become more acute as time goes on. Sydney
—which is to say the region where the A. G.
Co. operates—is bound in the nature of
things to feel the trouble worse than most
other centres, if only because, being the seat
of Government, it must be the bourne of the
State’s unemployed. And in this place and
at this time one of the most prosperous
monopolies in the Commonwealth considers
that it is a fair thing to hoist out of all
reason the price of the necessary which it
purveys. The fact that the Telegraph
chides the carcase butchers of a “neighbor-
ing State” but has not a word of blame for
the A. G. Co. of its own city is characteristic
of the local Liberal mind. The oppressive-
ness of the meat-sellers is new, and hence
dreadful; that of the gas-dealers is justified,
not to say sanctified, by yeai;s of usage. As
a matter of fact, there is a certain amount
of justification for the action of the carcase-
butchers. The war in Europe has occasioned


an extraordinary demand for meat, and the
carcase men have to pay more for the stuff
which they retail, in proportion as an un-
usual quantity of it is being shipped abroad.
But there is no export of gas. The gas in-
dustry is one of those which is in no way
prejudiced by the trouble on the other side
of the world. The N. S. Wales Government
is here offered an opportunity to do the com-
munity a useful service. Prices of neces-
saries. are being legislatively fixed in all
directions. What is wrong with Parliament
fixing the price of gas in Sydney at a reason-
able figure—one that would give the A. G.
Co. a fair return (say, an average of 7 per
cent, for all classes of shareholders), and
at the same time free the country from the
possible inflammation of “the mad passions
of anarchy”?
A Tariff Side-Issue and Some Sundries.
In time-off trouble 'Broken Hill lias' a habit
of getting in early with its own concerns.
Lately the local A.M.A. demanded that the
directors of those mines which have ceased
operations should be compelled to resume.
Not a word was said about the fact that the
fiscal-sinking policy, or semi-Freetrade policy,
for which Barrier Laborites have done their
full share,, has prevented Australia becom-
ing the great metal-refining country it should
be, and has compelled many of the mines to
depend on the German market —now closed —
for the sale of their concentrates and for the
money to pay wages with. If the directors
don’t resume work it was requested that the
Government should take over the mines and
work them. But if the Government does
that it must face one of two alternatives. It
may stack a vast quantity of unsaleable pro-
ducts till the war is over, and meanwhile
pay wages out of extra taxation at a time
when extra taxation is a difficult problem.
Or it may establish ore-refining works "of its
own, which promise to be something very like
a bad debt when the war is finished imless
the tariff is radically altered, and these
Broken Hill folk say nothing about an inten-
tion to abandon fiscal sinking and to support
tariff reform. As a third alternative it was
proposed that the Holman Ministry should
provide work for the Broken Hill toilers and
moilers by immediately commencing to build
the Menindie-Barrier railway. Unfortunately
the Holman Government, with abundant
support from Broken Hill, has turned dog oh
its old Labor pledge that it would reduce
borrowing, and learn how to do the great
bulk of its public works out of revenue. It
has become —with the support of Broken
Hill—a foreign loan Government pure and
simple. It doesn’t know how to be anything
else, and now that foreign loans are scarce
it is little use for Broken Hill to demand
that it should make bricks without straw.
Then, in addition to demanding that work
should be as abundant and as well-paid as
usual, the local A.M.A. urged that the
price of provisions should not be raised,
even if they were imported provisions and
the price was raised at the other end. And
it suggested that everybody on the Barrier,
including single men who occupy lodgings,
should cease to pay rent, and that time-
payment furniture instalments should leave
off till the scuffle at the far end of the globe
is over. If the concessions are granted, then
the A.M.A. folk at Broken Hill will make a
solid dividend out of their country’s troubles
and the Empire’s troubles. They will be the
unpaid rent and unpaid furniture instalments
to the good. No doubt the Hill is a dry,
lone, weary, dusty, depressing place where it
is difficult to cultivate the finer emotions,
yet the lack of these emotions may be carried
too far. The female —she is generally a
female, and sometimes possesses a baby—who
lets rooms to bachelor miners will have a
dismal time if her rent is stopped indefinitely,
and the furniture man’s clerk and rouseabout
will have very little dinner on Sunday should
the instalments leave off. Just here it may
be noted that nothing was said by the A.M.A.
about making any special effort on behalf of
Australia if, as demanded, it got plenty of
work and a stoppage of rent. The proposi-
tion lias a one-sided aspect somehow.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]

War and Business.
“10-2-4” — his sentiments :
Someone wrote to the S. M. Herald last week to
ask if the upheaval in Germany was “a capitalists’
war.” The answer was as follows: —
To talk of “a capitalists’ war” at this juncture is
merely to utter sounds without meaning. The capi-
talist has always feared this particular war more than
anything else in the world, except, perhaps, Social-
ism If anything is clear in the world, it is that
this war is the outcome of racial differences, which
have nothing whatever to do with wealth or the
absence of it.
The sounds without meaning appear to the writer to
emanate from the Herald. Most wars are business
wars, and if ever there was a “capitalists’ ” or
“trade war” this is surely one. The Servians have
loathed Austria for generations, not so much on
racial grounds (millions of Franz Joseph’s subjects
are their fellow-Slavs) as for the sordid reason that
the Servian capitalists, big and little, in the pig
industry have been financially harassed by the Dual
Monarchy’s tariff, and by its refusal to let them have
a free path for their products to the world outside
via an Adriatic port. The Servians who engineered
the slaughter of the Archduke Ferdinand played a
subtle part from their country’s viewpoint. They
not only robbed Austria of the one possible ruler
who could have held the two kingdoms together—
the man who had created, single-handed almost, the
Empire’s present Army and Navy—but they also
brought the enemy of their nation’s chief business
into conflict with the rest of Europe. The motive
power at the back of the general conflict, like that
at the back of the particular row between Austria
and Servia, was, again, not racialism, but trade,
business, capitalism or whatever else one may choose
to call it. Who thinks of the Pan-Slav issue now?
It is Germany against the world, Slav and otherwise.
Germany’s amazing Emperor is up to nis neck in
one of the bloodiest gambles that was ever known,
not on a point of racial sentiment, but in a frantic
reach after trade. That his hand has been forced,
that he is reaching months, perhaps years, before he
intended to, is plain from various indications. For
example, the rate in the German State Banks has been
extraordinarily low—often as low as 3i per cent. —
during the past 12 months; and cheap money, and a
resulting depletion of the national gold reserve, is
the last thing Berlin would have allowed had war
seemed imminent. But there is no doubt that when-
ever the position appeared favorable Germany meant
to fight—for the colonies which it hasn’t got; in
bri. ) for trade or capital. Germany has notori-
r =ly been prepared for years to fight Britain for
Africa, and France for the latter’s colonies, and for
security of tenure as regards Alsace-Lorraine. (Aus-
tralia it doesn’t want.) The position wasn’t particu-
larly favorable to Germany when it butted furiously
into the present dispute—one with which it had, on
the surface, no connection whatever. But it was
more favorable than it was likely to be for decades
to come.
Consider the circumstances. The Archduke
Ferdinand —a War Lord of sorts, a naval and mili-
tary enthusiast, one who might have made a great
cohesive ruler, and who would certainly have been
an impressive figurehead—had been killed. The old
Emperor may die any day, and Ferdinand being
dead, his successor will be a boy who has not been
even heard of by most of the nation. It is agreed
by all the experts that the last Balkan War caused
a 50 per cent, depreciation in the value of Austria-
Hungary as an ally. With Franz Joseph gone and
a feeble youth at the head of things, the troubled
empire might well have depreciated the other 50
per cent. Germany’s only hope of colonial expansion,
within the lifetime of the present Kaiser, was plainly
to strike while Austria was still of some value. And
so Germany struck. Its position is the burglarious
one of wanting some valuable property which a neigh-
bor possesses, and being determined to seize the
same, whatever the risk, while the chances seem
brightest. Had the Archduke’s death not precipi-
tated the necessity for the great steal, Germany’s
intention was to greatly strengthen both its navy
and its army during the coming two years—a pur-
pose which was indicated by the recent elevation of
the financier Kuhn to the Secretaryship of the
Imperial Treasury. The business of Kuhn was to
advise as to the best way of raising the necessary
millions, and financing the armament expansion
scheme generally.
To sum up, the present war is essentially a busi-
ness one, as regards the nation which is really to
blame for it. It will unquestionably ruin many
capitalists of all nationalities. All wars do. But,
on the other hand, it will make fortunes for other
capitalists—as every war does. If Germany loses,
the British Empire will keep its existing trade, and
probably get the trade of various German colonies
into the bargain. Also, the other members of the
Entente will benefit, in a financial sense, and Servia
will be able to export its pigs free of tariff inter-
ference from Austria, and in other respects the
winners will be richer than they were. If Germany
wins, it will be immeasurably richer, which prospect
is the sole reason, \Vhen all is said an'd dbnle, for its
entry into the present appalling struggle. Nations
don’t differ much, in the essentials, from individuals.
National pride is largely a matter df national wealth.
The same sentiment which inspires a man to amass
millions impels his country to amass colonies. If
Germany is reduced to the rank of a third-rate
Power, as a result of the present war, the truest
epitaph that could be inscribed on the stone ’at the
head of its dead greatness will be: “Another Victim
of the Get-rich-quick Habit.”

Our Daily Lies.
The language of “O. C. Cabot” :
Consider the rumors of the war, how they grow.
They cease not, night or day. The papers cannot
longer manufacture or cultivate them, but the
blitherer known as the Man in the Pub circulates
“cables” and “special messages” which leave pro-
bability comatose and possibility gasping for breath
(or beer).
The most pretentious, absurd and circum-
stantial lie of the last week or 10 days I give
from my' own experience in the reverberating and
crepitating city of Sydney. It started on August 6
with the account of a battle in the North Sea
between the British and German fleets. My infor-
mant (the first of 17 dittoes) gave the figures of
loss as 13 British Dreadnoughts and 13,000 men;
Germany, 21 Dreadnoughts and 23,000 men. No
small craft referred to. This rumor did heavy
work until about 11 a.m. on the next day, when it
shrank a little. I was then informed by 251 persons
(not all at once, but before five o’clock in the
afternoon) that the British had lost eight Dread-
noughts and the Germans 13 ! The loss of personnel
was kindly omitted. On Sunday night we were all
dragged out into the cold (I live at an altitude of
3400 ft., and in August the Sunday nights are dashed
chilly), and told that “special word” had come from
the lying old city of Sydney to say that the
“British loss was definitely known” as 14 Dread-
noughts and “about” 17,000 men; while the miser-
able Germans had been sent Below to the tune of
23,000 in 32 ships—presumably Dreadnoughts! By
this time I had got hardened, and simply asked our
informant whether he was sure it was only 32 Ger-
man ships. He said he thought so. On the follow-
ing morning 2761 people in Pitt- and George-streets
told me (during the day) that 11 British battleships
had “gone” ; also that the German Navy had been
“entirely wiped out.” On Tuesday a total eclipse
of the lie occurred ; 17,880 people from the Central
Railway Station down to the Quay agitatedly re-
marked that “a disaster” had struck J. Bull’s big
fleet right on the Dogger Bank. The news was so
bad that the Admiralty, the authorities, Garden
Island and the Salv’army Headquarters had unani-
mously refused to give the least publicity to the
facts. I had two drinks on the strength of this
awful defeat, to put myself right with the world,
and got a friend to pinch me hard in two places.
(I am sorry to say he pinched me in three places—-
one for half a crown.)
The news steadily got worse for the next
two days. The fact (?) that the Australia
was sunk off Norfolk Island, on Wednesday,
added nothing of real importance to the information
which had got Sydney (and I assume the rest of the
Commonwealth) down, and when I returned to the
village on Port Jackson I was quite prepared for the
worst. In fact (or fiction), I positively apprehended
the “authoritative announcement” that the German
Pacific fleet was even then shelling Manly or Pfah-
lert’s Hotel —two spots, though occasionally lost to
sight, to memory always dear. But, cher Monsieur
(that’s French), picture my relief- —my transports—
on learning from 28,991 people—lingering in the
vicinity of the pubs, tramsheds, newspaper offices
and churches of the metropolis—that “good news had
come!” It seemed (this would be last Thursday)
that the Governor-General and the Minister for
Loans (I mean Works) had received “an official
cable” from London, or Lowestoft, or Lourdes, or
some place commencing with “L,” to the effect that
“there had been a great battle in the North Sea.”
Hastily dashing out, I purchased seven different
“war” editions of the most religious papers in sight.
Violently dashing in, I searched them hard for
details —much as an old woman searches her blanket
for the 'nimble flea. All that the most rigorous
scrutiny could detect was a few lines which said
that the “German fleet was bottled up, somewhere
in the North Sea.” Putting up a prayer that Some-
body would keep his thumb on the cork of the
bottle, I retired to the mountains for the night. But
even there the war and rumor of war ceaseth not
from the uprising of the Sun even unto the going
down of the S. M. ’Erald. On Friday (to-day —
this very moment that, tremblingly I write you,
cher Monsieur (Vive La France!), 156,927
persons have, are, or will be told, telling
or informing me that it is true that
“there has been a great battle in the North Sea.”
The last report is that there “has been a victory” ;
Admiral Jellicoe and Prince Albert, the King’s
young son, the most prominent (and improbable)
among the “dead” who number 12,000; also, “we
have lost six ships, including two super-Dog-
boxes.” The Germans are out; nothing less; their
“loss” is 33 ships, and “ALL their Dreadnoughts!”
Well, thank Heaven, the “truth” is out at last.

“H.”: Last week a whisper went around
the saloon bars that in the near future
w-bisky and soda would cost ninepence per
nip. Pray Joss it may be so! A fortnight
previously John M. Joshua, of Joshua
Brothers, appeared before the Inter-State
Commission with a request that the duty
on proof whisky be increased from 14s. to
17s. per gallon. He explained that the
protection for blended whisky, under the
preference clause, was only 2s. per gallon.
Pure malt whisky and pot-still whisky had
a protective margin of 4s. per gallon, but
as no one drank them it made no real differ-
ence. Popular taste, these days, runs to
blended whisky. Given a duty of 4s. per
gallon on “blended whisky.” and the Aus-
tralian-made goods would boom. As it is,
the firm , distills only once in three years,
and has never succeeded in selling within 20
per cent, of its capacity, which is 400.000
gallons per year. So every good Australian
will fervently pray (in fact, he will pray
twice) :
(1) That Joshua Bros, have on hand a very large
stock of Australian whisky.
(2) That the price of imported whisky rises to
ninepence per nobbier.
Incidentally, Norman Shelley, wine and
spirit merchant, declared that it would cost
a heap" of money to break down the preju-
dice against Australian whisky. Maybe;
yet there was just as strong a prejudice
against Australian brandy, until the doctors
ordered the South Australian spirit to be
used in the hospitals, on account of its
purity. Then the prejudice began to dis-
appear and went on disappearing. Inter
alia, Shelley remarked that if a higher
duty were imposed on blended whisky, he
would consider the advisability of starting
it whisky-mill. On that admission alone it
looks as though Australia couldn’t do better
than give the higher duty a chance.
[C. A. Jeffries, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
«►_
In view of the fact that this is a day of
stress; considering the circumstance that
the gold reserve of the country should be
economised if Australia is to be in a posi-
tion to do its duty to the empire: keeping
an eye on the obvious proposition that
bankers are loyal and devoted citizens; not
forgetting the plain and naked and unvar-
nished event that this isn’t a time to throw
dirt at the solvency of the Australian
Government, especially when it is doing its
best to hold up the British flag—viewing all
these matters dispassionately, will the man-
agers of Australian banks lay in a big supply
of handy Australian notes (£5 and £1 and
10s.) and offer them, also press them, in
payment of all demands, only supplying gold
when gold is absolutely asked for? That
would do more to help this country through
the crisis than the delivery of 1000 speeches
and the waving of 20,000 belltoppers. There
are too many speeches and the waving of
belltoppers becomes a weariness. No bell-
topper ever killed a German soldier. If
every man will do what littlte he can in
his own line df industry, instead of drawing
diagrams in beer on the bar-counter of a
pub, showing the position of the Belgian
army, he will be a better patriot. Bank
managers who are prepared to attempt
their little bit in support of the Common-
wealth and the Empire are invited to roll
up.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]

The Melbourne Argus, being apparently
unable, for the moment, to secure an
imported match with which to light its
imported cigar, has wailed thuswise: —
The safety-match position is only another of those
unfortunate shortages which have upset business in
that article so often during the past five years.
Importers have always been careful to have as moder-
ate stocks of imported matches as possible, realising
that it was only a matter of time when with the
assistance of the duty the Australian article would
destroy the necessity of importing matches. The
extraordinary thing is that each time an interruption
to supplies of imported matches has occurred our
factories have failed to cope with the position. Some
contracts for Australian matches are already behind
as it is.
The position appears simple. The tariff
being inefficient it has been “a matter of
time” till Australia would be able to supply
itself with matches. This country was a
man sitting on two stools, or an ass hovering
between two stacks of hay. The local sup-
plies were insufficient because the tariff
wasn’t quite good enough, and the imports
were insufficient because the tariff wasn't quite
bad enough. When the imports were parti-
ally cut off it was held as a grievance against
Protection that our insufficiently-protected
match manufacturers didn’t, inside a Week,
put up works capable of supplying ali
tralia, with the chance that these costly
buildings would be a bad debt as soon as the
rush of cheap-labor imports got into full
swing again. There is much weariness and
exhaustion about this habit of regarding the
Australian worker as a person who must
stand around idle until, perhaps once in 20
years, the importing business strikes a snag,
and. then rush into the breach, and retire
again as soon as the blighted importer is able
to resume duty.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney,]
♦
This is a time when, by reason of the war,
it is especially important to conserve the
gold reserves of the country. Therefore The
Bulletin asks once more whether there is
any Australian bank manager who is so pro-
German that he still refuses to stock the
Australian 10s. notes, and fails to instruct
his tellers that they must always offer Aus-
tralian Government notes, and only supply
the yellow metal when it is demanded? This
paper only knows three banks in Sydney,
apart from the Commonwealth institution,
which profess to keep the ten-bob note on
hand. It doesn’t know one which makes a
habit of offering payment in notes. There-
fore it has an idea of making out a list of
Sydney bank managers, and sending the same
to Kaiser William, with an intimation that
they are his good friends and allies. And it pro-
poses to get its Melbourne and Adelaide and
Brisbane representatives to make up similar
lists in their cities, and forward them for the
joy of Kaiser William. There was a time
in Scotland when the people resolved to
stand by their country by demanding notes
and thus saving the gold reserve, and they
did it so strenuously that the sovereign fell
to a discount as compared with the £1 bit
of paper. In other words, McDougall
McPherson and McTavisii decided that,
even if they couldn’t go personally to the
front, they would do their little bit in their
own little sphere, to help the nation. Aus-
tralia might do as much without injury.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Gregor McGregor, the Labor leader for
whom the Senate had a genuine affection,
died last week; and in South Aus. the
Senate election will be disturbed in conse-
quence, for no other Labor nominee can
now be put forward in his place. The
rough-hewn old man commanded universal
respeft because of his unfailing cheerfulness
and good temper under conditions which
would have broken most people. He had
been almost blind for over 20 years ; yet he
continued to be one of the best informed
members of the party. Every moment of
spare time he had in his little home on the
Adelaide foothills was filled in by some one
or other of his family reading to him —read-
ing anything, everything; reading and per-
haps re-reading till the patient listener had
stowed the facts away in his mind. In
that same mind he also kept a most amazing
collection of anecdotes, which made him a
popular speaker almost anywhere. Long
before the first Federal election, at which
he was successful, he had been one of the
Labor group in the local Leg. Council; in
fact, for 20 years he had continuously re-
presented Labor, and had never repre-
sented it anywhere but in an “Upper”
House. Though he was unable to take a
portfolio through his partial blindness —to
which was added in recent years increasing
deafness —he throughout led his party in
the Senate. There his wise old head kept
his followers out of many difficulties, while
his unfailing good humor and personal popu-
larity got them out of most of the others
that they fell or were pushed into.
Non Credo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I rise from perusing the papers
Which sell at a penny a time,
(Our good old opinion-shapers
Which feature sensations and crime),
My sense of credulity scattered,
My faith in veracity fled,
My idols of rectitude shattered
And left in the gutter for dead.
(And the whole regrettable business is the
natural, in fact inevitable, outcome of study-
ing the war-neivs.)
Which moves me to cry to the mountains,
and likewise the plains and the hollows,
My newly-constructed “Non Credo,” the
substance of which is as follows -
I do not believe for a minute
That ninety of Germany’s craft
Invaded the Channel, and in it
Were challenged and raked fore and aft,
And finally captured and taken
To London as prizes of war.
My faith in such thrills has been shaken.
I now —though with grief—go so far
As even to question the story
That nineteen of Germany’s ships
Were blown, without mercy, to glory
When they and our fleet came to grips.
I do not believe that those over
From any great maritime scrap
Vre landing at Calais or Dover,
Or anywhere else on the map.
As a fact, to be brief.
If you want my belief,
It is this: There has been no such scrap.
I cast an eye of cold distrust upon the weird
narration
Of how a Gallic medico, while poisoning a
well,
With cholera bacilli, was—to everyone’s
elation —
Discovered by the other side, and promptly
given Hell.
I view with equal disbelief the circumstantial
stories
Of Germans shooting little girls and execut-
ing priests.
However dread an alchemist a European
war is,
It cannot, in a week, transform kind folks
to savage beasts.
I have my doubts that any son of Marshal
H. von Bieberstein
Remarked : “My country, ’tis for thee,” and
then fell down and died ;
And also that a Belgian lancer hurried from
his battle-line
And killed and captured Uhlans, single-
handed, far and wide.
The' Matin boasts that France does not
contain one Teuton warrior;
The Anzeiger that Paris will have fallen in
in a week ;
And both are lying hard. I know, though
which one is the sorrier
In this particular respect is somewhat hard
to seek.
I question if the Goeben exists,
And if the Breslau ever was afloat;
I doubt the so-called flight from Swakopund,
And that our foes left Windhuks in a
boat.
(In passing. I may mention, I am quite
prepared to find
That Swak and Windhuks both are myths,
or something of the kind.)
I class as liars all those sheets which claim
That prices have sone up in Berlin town.
I likewise give the lie direct to those
Who talk of prices being “fixed” or
“down.”
I even, in my frenzy, am nrepared
To prove, beyond a shadow of a doubt,
That Belgium’s not the Cock-pit of the
North! !
And so lay all the leader-writers out.
And, finally , if the press-lying which has
characterised the present trouble goes on
much longer, I am ready to support, with
cogent argument, the proposition that the
whole story about there being a European
war in progress is a fabrication, concocted
with the object of booming the circulation of
the newspapers.
N.s.w.
10-2-4.
