One of the blights of genius which Muriel
Starr occasionally touches is her tendency
to gabble. Her voice has an extraordinary
intensity of appeal, but she does not inflect
it sufficiently for the sake of dramatic effect.
On Saturday evening, at the revival of
“Within the Law” at Sydney Criterion, this
defect was occasionally too apparent. Her
rapid delivery became monotonous. But she
is a great emotional actress, and Bayard
Veiller’s melodrama was an admirable
vehicle for her undoubted powers. Mary
Worth, in the part of a demure confi-
dence woman (all women are confidence
women), had the opportunities for character
acting cruelly denied her in “Madame X.”
Her work was delightfully finished. Gwen
Burroughs, however, proved a decided weak-
ness to the cast. Her impersonation of the
guilty saleswoman was on conventional,
colorless lines. In the scene where she is
supposed to be fainting with hunger it would
have required the production of two doctors’
certificates before the audit nee believed her.
E. W. Morrison repeated his faultless imper-
sonation of the forger Carson, achieving the
full pathos of his last scene with the most
delicate touches of artistry. Eric Maxon, who
failed to be as youthful as the author meant
him to be, showed again an unexpected
power in his emotional moments. His socks
were of the thinnest silk of the most
decollete, tendencies. Boyd Irwin remains
the one convincing detective on the contem-
porary stage; and if the New York police-
officers are not like Lincoln Plumer this
critic confesses himself disappointed with
the United States. Gerald Ilarcourt, as the
“stool pigeon,” was not an improvement on
the previous cast. But, on the whole, the
melodrama was disappointing, owing to the
failure to rehearse the final scene. This
scene occurs after the company has received
the flowers from the audience. The curtain
f°ll and rose, to disclose the star bowing on
the empty stage. The curtain fell again
and rose, invariably catching the star in an
attitude of modest surprise. It was evident
that she had not, for one moment, imagined
that the curtain-raiser would lift the rag
again. This little scene was repeated with
variations during the next six times the
curtain was raised. Either the curtain-
raiser sluuild be discharged for incompetency
or the actress should be sufficiently re-
hearsed.
War’s alarms have had little effect on “The
Girl in the Taxi” at Her Majesty’s, Sydney.
The vehicle has now been running for 10
consecutive nights, and the mileage is ex-
pected to be something tremendous before
the differential drops out or the carburetter
falls to pieces.
Le Roy, Talma and Bosco are still bright-
ening things up at the Palace (Sydney),
particularly since the new illusion and tab-
leaux', “To the Glory of France.” were pro-
duced. Naturally, this depicts the result of
Armageddon (not R. McGeddon, The
Bulletin verse-builder) in a manner suit-
able to local patriotism and the show cen-
sor’s idea of things. Fresh turns by the
supporting entertainers induce this paper
to retain its previous good opinion of them.
It was only visions of Fat and the Church
stampeding and tearing out their hair with
rage that made The Bulletin give up the
idea of coming out a day late this week in
order to include a sledge-hammer notice.
Therefore, since Wednesday was the first
night, Sydney Adelphi show is ordered to
come up for sentence next Thursday, and,
meantime, is bound over to keep the peace
without the option.
The Sydney Tivoli’s latest acquisition,
Harry Taft, who is billed as “America’s
famous whistling comedian,” has a whistle
that is more suited to a sheep station than
anvthing else, and a supply of talk that isn’t
suited to anything that this paper cares
about.. The Bulletin advises him to sit
down and listen to himself for half an hour
and then think it over. Meanwhile Cinque-
valli, boss juggler, is due on Saturday.
** * *
The daily afternoon performances at the
National Amphitheatre (Sydney) have
proved a winner for the Fuller-Brennan
firm. From the start the scheme caught on,
and nothing is likely to lessen its popu-
larity, except war depression. And, of
course, as there is no war depression for any-
body but Bill of Berlin, the present arrange-
ments will continue as usual.
All’s well at the Imperial (Sydney). The
tangoist threads his (and her) mazy way
and the ragger leapetli like the mountain
goat. Also, the box office keepeth its tail up.
Sydney Glaciarium’s annual corroboree,
“The Palace of the Golden Domes,” corrobo-
rates to-night.
Concerts and meetings “in aid of the War
Relief Fund” are the main interest of
Adelaide juft now. Evemthe visiting Science
men had to listen to a variety of patriotic
songs and speeches during their brief stay.
The massive, Salisbury-like Oliver Lodge
gave a fine (and quite intelligible-to-thc-
crowd) discourse on ether, but most of the
visitors were bent more on acquiring local
information than giving any. At the Tivoli is
Beth Tate, the American girl with a manner
that is demure and songs that are moder-
ately demure. Josh Dixon works hard at
his trade of comedian, and amuses many—
but not all. The Omega Three and Thornley
Dodge are just coming in. At the King’s
are a clever lot, singers and dancers mostly,
and Deaves’s “mannikins” from the U.S.A.
This little theatre has caught the public
fancy strongly. “Her Luck in London”
(Anderson Co.) has also nothing to com-
plain of in the nublic patronage at the Royal,
but a good military drama would go much
better just now. Branscombe’s “pink” co.
of Dandies has started a short season at
the Town Hall, and army and navy films
continue to do good service at Wondergraph,
West’s, Pav. and Empire.
The last-but-one-week of the Gil-Sul sea-
son at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) has brought
the sweet, familiar melodies of “Patience”
back to local hearing, and the fact that
even a British dook can get tired of toffee
is once more in evidence. “Patience” calls
more attention than some of the other operas
to the want of personal attractiveness in
the chorus. The 20 love-sick maidens (who
number only IS in this production) are
sufficiently unbeautiful to justify Bunthorne
and Grosvenor in the habit of walking ahead
of the ladies, and steadfastly resisting their
importunities. Also the 18 dragoon guards-
men suggest that the standard of the House-
hold Brigades is not what it used to be.
But the choral singing is quite satisfac-
tory, the orchestra admirable, and the well-
worn scenery blends with the music. As
for the principals, they were at least equal
oil Saturday to most of the opportunities
for a double-encore which a first-night per-
formance offers. Kavanagh, with his loud
military voice and swelling lower chest, gave
importance to the character of Colonel Cal-
verly. Frank. Wilson, who failed to look a
blonde and handsome Grosvenor with the
assistance of a brick-red wig, sang his songs
incisively. Arnold was a droll little Major,
especially in the famous posturing trio.
LEROY , TALMA AND BOSCO AT
SYDNEY BALACE.
Bosco, the human rabbit hutch .
McKeown did nicely as the toffeed Duke, and
Walenn’s sardonic little Bunthorne, being
rather insignificant, gave the other charac-
ters more apparent value than they had
when Howard Vernon’s tall impersonation
of the fleshly poet used to overshadow them.
In the name part, which needs clever acting
as well as singing, Miss Pearl Ladd lacked
nothing but a strain of essential melody in
her warble. Miss Ethel Morrison did fairly
well as Lady Jane, and the three leading,
rapturous maidens were not unduly bril-
liant. This is a company wherein nobody
ever casts a searchlight on the proceedings.
“Pinafore” is announced for the last five
nights of the season, beginning next Satur-
day. A week of panto, revival will follow.
“Bunty” struck an unsuitable time for
pulling the strings at Melbourne King’s.
The Scottish character comedy with the art-
lessly melodramatic plot would seem more
at home in piping times of peace, when the
haggis and the pibroch lie down together and
business is guid all round. Just now
“Bunty” is a very unseasoned dish to set
before the King’s, and although some of the
ingredients have virtues of their own, the
first-night feeders didn’t speak of the re-
freshment in a warm, advertising spirit.
“Bunty” came, however, with the intention
of staying six weeks, which is lots of time
for sincere admirers of the Graham Moffat
show to drag in their friends and persuade
them to acquire the taste. There are many
points of honest humor in the dialogue, and
minor incidents.
At Melbourne Royal the demand for “A
Royal Divorce” is unabated, and the roar of
the stage cannon will continue until further
notice. Julius Bonanarte’s reference to
the British bulldogs who “fight on, fight
on, fight on” is largely responsible for the
turning away of money on Saturday nights.
If he were to drop in a few remarks about
“brave Belgians” and German swank the
management might have six Saturdays and
three crowded matinees a week.
“The Glad Eye” will be looking for
patronage at Melbourne Princess’s on Satur-
day (22nd). Ten gentle strangers have
arrived from England to present “the funni-
est play within memory” to Australian
notice, and Willoughby, Ltd., in conjunction
with Beaumont Smith and Louis Meyer.
Leroy becomes enthusiastic over his
Entente Tableau.
will produce them on the date aforesaid.
Meanwhile Alien Doone is closing his season
with “Sweet County Kerry.”
The Melbourne Tivoli bill has already lost
Cinquevalli, if the advt. spoke truly, and
another principal item is already on the spot,
or may be expected immediately. Bar the
graceful old daddy of modern jugglery there
was nobody of particular note at the Tiv.
last Saturday. The Carpatii Bros, are one
clever gymnast in tights and another in
baggy pants, who labors to be funny. Welch
and Carbasse have a feeble sketch; the
Four Vags are singing no better than before;
and the Baroness continues to serve out
obsolete coon songs with the help of a well-
nourished tenor. Also, Dusty Rhodes
devotes a fine, fruity voice to witless ditties,
and Eugene Damond plays admirably on
his violin. None of these turns are excit-
ing.
West (Melbourne) is still publishing inci-
dents in the career of the late Queen Vic-
toria, omitting naught that will help to a
complete revelation of the lady’s stedginess
and respectability. The historical incidents
are generally well produced, but numerous
domestic pictures are rather spoilt by the
presence of an Albert the Good, played as a
languid burlesque of a Ouida guardsman
with most of his character in his feet.
Melbourne Bijou is now haunted by the
comfortable presence of Edward Blondell,
the much-advertised “Lost Boy” from Noo
Yark City. Edward’s turn is mostly a long
conversation with an unimpressive lady
accomplice, admittedly funny in spots, but
generally insufficiently droll to warrant its
discursiveness. The less-boomed Ray and
Ray are more laughable and more naturally
humorous, and even the rough, homespun
work of the Waldrons seems striking by
comparison. The two best acts on the
present Bijou bill are furnished by Carlton
Max, an accomplished ventriloquist, and
Juno Salmo, a singular contortionist got up
like a Mephistopheles at a fancy-dress ball.

BALLADE DRAMATIQUE A DOUBLE REFRAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In London actors are doing duty as special con-
stables between midnight and 4 a.m.— Cable item.
Scene: The Strand by Night. Time: The Present.
Actor: Halt, sirrah! Stay thine onward march.
You gaze on one who feels the parch
Of midnight thirst within his mouth.
Intolerable is my drouth.
This tongue is like some fire-dried hob.
Could you oblige me with a bob?
Citizen: Mine is an ill-requited job.
I simply cannot spare the bob.
Actor (aside): Angels and ministers of grace!
He dares to utter to my face
A lie as base as it is wild,
One that would not deceive a child.
But I am no true British pro.
If I allow him thus to go
With money that would buy me beer
And other alcoholic cheer.
(Aloud, with the suavity of the expert borrower):
1 fear that you mistake me, friend.
I did not ask that you should lend
All that you carry in your fob.
The sum I mentioned was a bob.
Citizen (coldly): And I repeat that I decline
To be a sort of walking mine,
Or cash dispensary, or bank,
For ev’ry stray dramatic tank
Who happens to require a beer.
Let me be absolutely clear.
You may entreat, rave, rant or sob,
You shall not touch me for a bob.
Actor (anyered, aside): Now is the wintry discontent
Which was within my bosom pent
Unleashed like all the dogs of war.
Insult and greed have gone so far
That only legal action may .
Avail to wipe their stain away.
(To Citizen, fiercely):
Know, parsimonious ruffian, who
Has been in colloquy with you?
(Opens coat and displays special constable’s badge):
It is impossible to flee;
You’ll kindly come along with me,
And in the Bow-street station lie
Till morning, as a German spy—
(Suddenly remembering that thirst is stronger than
revenge): Unless, that is, you forthwith rob
Your trousers pocket of that bob.
Citizen: Here’s for your predatory nob;
I’m d —d if you shall have the bob.
(Strikes Actor on the nose, and Exit, j
Actor (raising himself weakly from the pavement):
Thus do our fairest visions fly:
A gleam of hope, and then they die.
And Life's again a place accurst,
A vast, dry wilderness of thirst—
One where it is a Giant’s job
To even raise a paltry bob.
Curtain.
N.S.YV.
H. SINGEMOINE.

“C. Ockney”: Pinero’s latest play was
produced in London last month —a tragedy
called “The Bulkeley Peerage.” It proved
that every peerage is a tragedy. The chief
characters were Ponsonby Sweet (played by
Gerald du Maurier) and his wife, their little
boy, a detective and Lord Bulkeley—this
part falling to Australia’s old friend,
Edmund Gwenn. • The plot? It was all plot.
The Sweets plot to take Lord B.’s title, and
assure Sweet junior that some day he will
be a peer, at which the innocent youngster
asks, “Shall I have a pavilion at the other
end of me?” Lord B. is murdered by the
couple, asphyxiated with the help of a patent
stove. The detective then appears and in-
forms the guilty pair that, the night before,
Bulkeley had, with all his brother peers,
renounced his title and become a mere com-
moner ! Curtain. This giddy little piece
provided one of the sideshows at a theatrical
garden party, given in aid of the Actors’
Orphanage fund, and was the biggest success
that Pinero has scored of late.
“Harrison O.”: “C. Ockney” suggests
(B. 13/8/T4) that my yarn concerning the
improvisation of an opera by Teddy Solomon
and Sydney Grundy “is not quite correctly
given.” I don’t wish to be uncivil to “Ock.,”
blit I am impelled to state that he lies. I
A WARNING.
A war cable states that German spies have been
arrested disguised as Boy Scouts.
Australians are advised to keep an eye on
Boy Scouts, and if any look like the above to
report the matter to the authorities at once.
am prepared to make an affidavit on this
point, or even, if necessary, to bet about it.
Still, preserving my temper and keeping
well within the frontiers of strict courtesy, I
furthermore mention that the insinuation
that Grundy would have required to be “hard
up” before collaborating with the clever
Yankee Yid is asinine. At this time Solomon
was trying to rival Sullivan, and many
thought, myself included, that he was going
to succeed. And whether “hard up” or not,
Grundy did collaborate with the Hebrew
musician in several operas, including “The
Vicar of Bray” and “Pocahontas,” which
was produced at the Empire in 1895. For
the latter piece Hayden Coffin was cast as
Captain John Smith, but, on the alleged
ground that he had had no operatic train-
ing, the part was wrenched from Coffin
almost at the last moment and handed to
Frank Celli. A few weeks later Coffin was
given his chance in “The Lady of the Locket”
(also by Solomon), and scored a sensational
success. These things have nothing to do
with the controversy, but they may help to
cow “Ockney” by showing him that he is
assailing me on a familiar topic.
Died the other week, Carlotta Addison,
who in her day was one of the sweetest and
best of comedy actresses in England. Her
young day was that of the Robertson and
H. J. Byron comedy, and she played the
sentimental heroine, as a rule, after which
she developed her art on graceful matron
lines. Her last appearance was in Bernard
Shaw’s “Pygmalion” at His Majesty’s
(London). When the old favorite actress
fell ill her place was filled at short notice
by the much-travelled Australian Nellie
Mortyne. But when, 10 days later, Miss
Addison died and another actress had to be
engaged for the rest of the season, manager
Tree chose Alma Murray for the vacancy.
The merits of the emergency lady didn’t
count. All that was the matter with her
was that her name wasn’t big enough.
“Allegretto”: The late Alice Charbonnet
Ivellerman took the musical world in Mel-
bourne and Sydney by storm with her musi-
cal fireworks. Even the dour musical critics
who suspected her of superficiality weren’t
proof against her youthful beauty, vivacity
and charm. Soon after arriving in Sydney
she married (why no one ever knew) a
N.S.W. public servant in a moderate posi-
tion. The more remarkable thing was that
she turned him into a high-class music
teacher. Their school in Darlinghurst
was, 30 years ago, Sydney’s leading
musical centre, and flourished for nearly 20
years. If the lady’s splendid energy hadn’t
wasted itself in amateur theatricals (for
which she fancied she had a genius) the
place might have lasted longer. As
it was the family struck hard times until it
shifted camp to Paris.
“C. Ockney”: Providing Europe quietens
down sufficiently to enable the mummers’
voices to be heard, London is in September to
see a revival of Barrie’s “Little Minister”
at the Duke of York’s. This is the piece
that gave Harry Rickards an opportunity
for an excursion into the “legitimate” some
16 years ago. Pattie Browne was the lead-
ing lady, playing the part that Winifred
Emery created in London —Lady Babbie.
Two of the original London cast are at pre-
sent in Australia —Eardley Turner and
Clarence Blakiston. The latter has yet to
make his first appearance here. He is
Nellie Stewart’s newest leading gent.
Eardley Turner has been to the front for
quite a while, and when he has nothing
better to do he writes verses for The
Bulletin. Incidentally, Tiie Bulletin
has never seen any reason to object to the
verses.
According to a just-issued British blue
book there were 9076 persons calling them-
selves actors in the Cold Country in 1911,
compared with 6044 in 1901; and in the
same period “actresses” increased from 6443
to 9179. The vaudeville profesli is stated
now -to give employment to 3213 male and
2445 female grease-painters.
H. B. Irving has got back to the
stage in London, in a play by
Stephen Phillips on the David and
Uriah theme. The idea is followed
closely, though the characters are
placed in Cromwell’s army. Colonel
David gives Captain Uriah a need-
lessly dangerous post—and marries
the widow. At the end, where the
child dies, Colonel David, recognis-
ing his sin, vows to “live ascetic-
ajiy” for the future —a thing which
the original David would never
have undertaken. The part does
not seem to have suited Irving.
The papers all say he was splendid,
of course; but one tells the truth
in this delicate fashion —“albeit his
great talents are not attuned to
the emotions of a passionate lover.”
There is no Borneo strain in any
Irving’s composition, somehow.
“Harrison O.”: Attended Melbourne Her
Majesty’s on the opening night of “Patience”
and lost mine because the principals seemed
to imagine that it was their place to be
seen and not heard. The words of the aver-
age musical comedy song do not matter
tuppence, but with a Gilbertian “lyric” the
words are of as much, and sometimes more,
importance than the music. Both the
heavenly twins —the one with his galloping
tiifcq, and the other with his long lines, diffi-
cult proper names and words of many syl-
lables —were apt to treat with scant con-
sideration those who had their songs to
sing-oh. A clear, crisp enunciation should
be insisted on in a Gilbertian comedian, and
it is what Walenn, the present frivoller-in-
chief, most conspicuously lacks. Frank
Wilson, in this respect, is the most satis-
factory member of the co., for he takes the
audience into his confidence from the jump,
and warbles like a man who has nothing to
conceal.
“Sr ”: A Melbourne show that to my
recollection lias never seen the “P.P.” column
is the Aquarium. Sole lessees and managers
are the Exhibition Trustees. The perform-
ing seals and dogs have always been with it,
and likewise a small supply of moving pic-
tures. Interspersed with these is a mild
sensation in the form of the Salome dance by
a nicely-shaped young lady, undressed beau-
tifully to the character. Whenever I get
down to Melbourne the Aquarium is my
first Sunday-show visit, and for the past
three years I have regularly met this particu-
larly nice Salome with the lights turned
down low. May her shadow never grow less
definite*
“Binnacle” : An unusual case was heard
in N.S.W. Vice-Admiralty Court this term,
when the crew of the N.S.W. Government
pilot steamer sued the' Chinese owners of
the barquentine Alexa for £2OOO and the
skipper handed up a claim for £lOOO on
account of salvage. One bad night in Jan-
nary last the Alexa broke away from a tug
right under North Head while entering Port
Jackson. The pilot vessel Captain Cook
answered its signals of distress and extri-
cated it from a dangerous position just
PROFIT AND LOSS.
EXCITED PATRIOT: “Bravo! Hooray! Germany loses 8000 men. Hooray!”
(And Jingo loses his watch.)
when hope had been abandoned. All this
was practically admitted, but the case raised
an interesting point as to the liability of a
merchant vessel to pay salvage to a Govern-
ment vessel whose upkeep is the affair of the
taxpayers. The N.S.W. Government aban-
doned any claim it might have had against
the Alexa and its valuable cargo of copra,
but granted permission to the officers and
crew to sue on their own account. The case
was heard before Justice Sly, who found
that valuable services had been rendered by
plaintiffs; that, in fact, they had rescued
the Alexa. But he dismissed the case on
two points: (1) That the regulations under
the Navigation Act require the crew of the
pilot steamer to render aid to vessels in
distress; (2) That no King’s ship can sue
for salvage without the consent of the
Briitish Admiralty. Had the case (the
first of its kind in Australia) gone the other
way, and the crew of the Captain Cook
established the right to claim salvage in re-
spect of vessels assisted, the rush for billets
in the pilot service (already big) would
have become a regular stampede.

Time is a strange old creature and can wait
for his revenge. He has all Space and Eter-
nity before him. A few centuries, or even a
few eras, don’t matter. When, not very
long ago, Persia sent an embassy to Greece,
after more than 2000 years’ suspension of
diplomatic i-elations, the last Persian Minis-
ter having been thrown down a well by the
Greeks away back in the tangled wilderness
of 8.c., Time was hurrying. _ But the other
day he got on a devil of a sprint. In 1870 a
dispirited and ill-conducted French army
crossed into German territory and fought the
first skirmish of Napoleon lll.’s war at
Saarbruck. Then the tide of battle turned,
and France never looked up again. Saar-
bruck was where the Prince Imperial, who
was afterwards killed by an unimportant
nigger in Africa, received his “baptism of
fire.” Now, after waiting 44 years, a much
greater French army, full of fight, is pouring
over the same frontier, and the battle which
was interrupted in 1870 is going to be
finished. One way or another the arrears
are to be cleaned up.
The late Gregor McGregor took about the
biggest jump on record on entering Parlia-
ment, going into South Australia’s Upper
House straight from pick-and-shovel work,
However, before he came to Australia, aged
30, he had been prominent in Labor move-
ments in Glasgow dockyards, and he waited
17 years before becoming M.L.C. He filled,
meanwhile, most of the higher posts in the
State’s Labor party. In 1901 S.A. sent
most of its best men of either side to the
first Federal Parliament, and McGregor
became Senator almost as a matter of course.
He was only 66 when the end came.
There seems to be —as Hughes said last
week —a “dreadful fatality” attending the
South Aus. batch of Federal politicians.
“Stadium”: Germany, to me, seems to be
in the position of a fight-thirsty pugilist
who has gone down to the stoush depQt to
have a cut at an old opponent and struck a
whirlwind of opposition on the way to the
ring, where the old opponent is smilingly
awaiting him, reinforced by two tremen-
dously powerful friends, one of whom in-
tends to be always behind him. Meanwhile,
one of the confident lad’s supposed friends
is scuffling ingloriously in a corner with the
hostile small boy he tried to bully,
and the other is taking a front seat
and preparing to watch the contest
with an impartial eye. The only ques-
tion seems to be: How far will it go?
As there is no referee to score the points, it
is bound to be a knock-out. And the unfor-
tunate person who is just trying to get to
the ropes has only one notable victory to his
credit! A fight promoter who staged such a
contest would deserve all the hard things
that could be said about him. But then the
fight-hungry one has been his own promoter.
The permanent and reproductive railway
that the Australian always builds out of
loan money is the first asset to be torn up
by the roots when war really arrives.
Sometimes the enemy (who doesn’t own
it or owe for it) does the job in a care-free
manner, and sometimes the groaning bor-
rower has to wreck his own security to
save worse trouble. But neither of the
belligerents can utterly destroy and efface
the bill. A skilled and earnest gang of
wreckers, working at high pressure, can
make the most permanent and reproduc-
tive railway look just like a heap of old iron
in a few hours. But, providing the borrower
survives, it remains on Cohen’s books as a
beautiful, complete railway in good working
order. The really permanent, eternal and
imperishable railway hasn’t yet been in-
vented. Nothing is permanent and im-
perishable but death.

“Hot Room”: One of my pet vices is
Turkish bathing. Once a week I rest my
.jaded mind and body by retiring to a place
of heat and silence and getting myself par-
tially cooked. It isn’t so much the cooking
that refreshes me as the silence and nudity.
The washing and kneading are secondary
considerations, and I believe I could
do without them altogether. However, when
I arrive at the baths on Saturday afternoon
I am too tired to say so to the manager, and
the odds are he wouldn’t understand me if I
did. So I submit to the usual washings and
take my money’s worth in peace on the slab.
But when I arrived last Saturday the
usually peaceful baths were filled with hot
unclothed men of various shapes who were
shouting alleged war news at one another.
They had collected a mass of scare intelli-
gence and were hurling it backwards and
forwards like a caye-full of barbarians. All
they wanted to complete their make-up
was a bundle of stone axes and a bearskin
or two. Before I could draw back, they
had demanded my opinion on the situation,
and straightway I found myself engaged by
a total stranger of globular shape who wore
a small towel round Iris shoulders and a
gleam of frenzy in his eye. When I had
fought myself clear of him I was tackled
by a tali person like an armored cruiser
gone to ruin. He had no intellectual guns
worth mentioning, but his mind was full
of poisonous gas, and he kept at me till I
rose and steamed at top speed for my
clothes. I finally escaped from the place
with my mind shot to rags. When the war
is over I will resume the Turkish bath
habit. There is nothing in it just now,
* * * *
“Fiddlestick”: This is the tale of a
patriotic boardinghouse proprietress, and a
string band. Madame Hash is in the habit
of hiring an orchestra of sorts and giving
her guests a “musical dinner” once a week.
The night on which the news came that J.
Bull was at war, the band was just turning
up preparatory to murdering the overture,
and the boarders were on the point of
attacking the soup, when the lady_ rushed
into the dining-room in great excitement.
“Stop!” she commanded; and the soup-
eaters and the fiddlers alike ceased their
noises. Approaching the leader of _ the
orchestra the true-blue patriot asked in a
voice trembling with emotion: “Are there
any Germans among you?” “Nein!” came
the instant reply. “Ye vos all, eferyone,
Oustralien* 1”

Some military .terms explained : —-
A convention is a ■meeting' to arrange rules of war
for your enemy to obserae during peace.
A censor is an officer who gives the newspapers a
chance to pretend that they know more than they
tell.
An enemy is one who can’t keep up the pretence
of being your bosom friend any longer.
A peace society is a society which is at peace
•during war.
An ambassador is a gentleman employed at a large
salary to write history in advance.
Patriotism is a quality
in man nearest akin
to virtue in woman;
and the virtuous
woman takes her vir-
tue for granted.
An armistice is an
opportunity given to
war correspondents to
renew their imagina-
tions.
War news is the hot
poker with which the
papers warm the pot
of public enthusiasm.
AVhen it has done its
job it is withdrawn.
Alliances are things
you quarrel about
when no other pre-
texts are available.
An army is the most
you can get for your
money.
Tactics are the series
of accidents which hap-
pen to you when you get into a row. Strategy is
the list you draw up for your enemy beforehand.
Armageddon is the general term applied to the
next great war.
“0.K.”: It should be dead-easy for the
Pankhurst militants to (get Woman Suffrage
granted by the next British Parliament. All
they have to do is to enlist as field-hospital
nurses, organise a system of relief-work in
Britain, and otherwise make themselves con-
spicuously useful. After thus rising to the
occasion they can march to the House of
Commons, and the vote will be brought to
the heroines on a silver salver. Militancy
has failed hitherto, because it wasn’t admir-
able. The time was Not Yet Ripe. But the
blood-red sun has ripened it. . “Votes for
Women,” placarded in every military hospi-
tal, and pinned to the heaving breast of
every nursemaid when Tommy and Jack
come back with woioden legs, will convert
the Army and Navy to the rebel cause; and
what the Army and Navy wants it has only
to ask for, and (for a time, at least) it will
get.
A practical joker, whose methods belong
to the pre-booby Trap, or Stone Age period
of humor, is operating just now at Carlton
(Melb.). He sent by telephone to one
house last, week a doctor, a couple ot par-
sons, two undertakers, and finally a nunibei
of policemen, to remove the alleged victim
of an accident to the morgue. . The last
time this class of joke was played in London
the victim sent the wag, by way of a return
jest, a retired pugilist with instructions to
take it out of the humor-
ist’s hide. In the re-
sulting court proceedings
the magistrate said that
the one joke seemed to
him as justifiable as the
other.

Some remarkable testimony is adduced
against compulsory training in “The Mili-
tary Menace in Australia,” a tract in sup-
port of the Australian Freedom League and
its noble propaganda : —•
The ideals of manhood and morality set before
armed Europe may be summed-up in the words of
Kaiser William to his conscripts: “I may call upon
you to shoot down or bayonet your own relatives—-
father and mother, sisters and brothers. My orders
in that respect must be executed cheerfully and with-
out grumbling.” Hear him again: “The Gospels must
he introduced with a fist of iron.” Again: “The
army in China must take r.o prisoners, but kill all.”
Wherefore, asks the quaint Freedom League:
“What is the Australian citizen’s duty—to
foment and hasten strife by training our
young in the principles of war?” Nay, nay
indeed! Rather let us offer no resistance to
Bill Hohenzollern when his “gospel” arrives
in these parts. After putting sister and
wife in the forefront of the slaughter, mother
with them, and grandmamma in between,
nothing will remain for ourselves, of course,
but to tear hastily for the tall timber, with
Freedom still our watchword and peace
reigning over all!
* * * *
“Hermes”: I suspect that this European
turmoil will be decided by the plain Jenny
Wren type of officer who runs the commis-
sariat. Any fool with enough cash and
men can raise an army 1,200,000 strong, as
Kaiser W. has done. But it requires a
genius to collect food and general supplies,
and have the lot always where it is wanted.
The French, Belgian and British forces are
acting chiefly on the defensive, so their food,
problem, although big enough, is not so big'
as that of the Germans, who are the attack-
ing party. Imagine about twice the popu-
lation of Sydney shifting its hungry mouth
every day, and you get some idea of this
German horde and what the feeding of it
means. The difficulties of transport will in-
crease as the army advances, and if there
should be a big battle and an unutterable
smash it will be about impossible to feed
such a mass of men on a retreat. It looks
as though the modern system of hurling all
the able-bodied of a nation at the throat of
another, and leaving the feeding of them to
the old, the feeble and military unfit, will
bi’eak down under its own weight.
Australia does not realise what an unusual
place it is until it sees the astonishment and
delight of a trained observer such as any of
the big Science Congress men. According to
their various bent, they go into ecstasies
over their first experience of a blackfellow,
or a gold mine, or a kookaburra or iguana,
or an acacia-wattle in bloom, or even over
the sun persistently in the north. South
Australia showed them all these things, bar
the gold-mine; also it took them to see its
Hallett’s Cove rock grooved by (alleged!
glaciers, and led them into a copper-mine and
the big winery at Seppeltsfield, and got up a
corroboree, and remarked upon its ideally
laid-out city. Only when they congratu-
lated it on its perfect midwinter climate, it
smiled sadly, and tried to look as if cloudless
skies were not steadily assailing its most
important and vulnerable spot, the wheat-
field, where a worse disaster is threatened
than any the State has known for years and
years.
TERRIBLE THOUGHT.
SCHOOL SPINSTER (to Art spinster): “If the
enemy [?]ome they might seize us girls to be
their Hives.”
The war discovered the existence of a.
woman with an undue amount of good luck.
When the dreadful news arrived that Britain
was involved she fled, in a state of acute
financial panic, to the post office, and wntn-
drew the' whole of her savings, viz., *5/,*
Of this sum £3O was in Commonwealth
notes. She lost the notes on the way home,
and notified the police. The latter adver-
tised iind next day a “Mr. Walker, a
laborer,” called and handed over the
“flimsies,” receiving the lady’s thanks in
return. When you remember the uninteln-
gence of Melbourne’s police and add to it
the odds against the notes having been
found by a citizen sufficiently honest not to
steal them, you arrive at the vast sum ot
this foolish female’s good fortune.
BOG IN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Belgian named Boggarts dashed from his o\ Vn
ranks, killed four Germans, and regained his coiu-
rades without a scratch. He made these salli es
several times.- — War cable.
The Uhlans do not want to fight, their hearts are f ar
away;
They do not hate the Belgians, and they do not want
to slay ;
But, since the war-drunk Caesar drives them on their
bloody track,
We must whack them very swiftly; we must break
and drive them back.
We must thrash them till the Kaiser has his thirst
for fame a-slako,
And our German brother we must wallop for that
brother’s sake;
We must kill a-many Germans, that the German V ve
may win—-
So, bog in, Boggarts ! Bog in.
A mighty people dragged to death behind a Kaiser’s
car;
The Rhine grapes rot because the press treads out
the grapes of war ;
Down by Treves, and up at Namur and from Liege
east to Cologne,
We must make the reaping swiftly of the death the
Kaiser’s sown ;
With some quick, decisive battles, that their hearts
may fail them soon,
And that peace come ere the wounded faint beneath
a wintry moon;
’Tis sacrifice of guiltless blood to purge the war-
lord’s sin!
So bog in, Boggarts! Bog in!
Give our brothers not a minute’s rest to steel their
hearts anew.
So that next harvest may be peace, let battle’s ghastlv
dew
Splash the fields of corn and poppies ; let the furrows
be a grave
For the few who are the luckless, that the others W e
may save;
Strike hard and strike them quickly; deal swift
penance for their sin !
Bog in, Boggarts i Bog in!
N.s.w.

The story of one patriot and another: —
l, George Gordon, Gas Manager, Port Fairy, Vic-
toria, solemnly and sincerely declare the contents of
tin's letter to be true. —Geo. Gordon. Sworn before
me, J. C. Holden, J.P., Port Fairy, Aug. 10., 1914.
(1) I am a member of the Port Fairy Rifle Club,
and at a special meeting of the club on August, 6 1,
with six others, volunteered to serve my country
during the war.
(2) On Saturday, August 8, my landlord informed
my wife and;myself that he had heard 1 had volun-
teered, and that the allowance to my wife was only
3s. per day. He said she could not -keep herself
and family and pay his rent on this amount, and in
the event of the rent not being paid it would be his
duty to put her in the street. He said people would
condemn him for doing this, and, .to make himself
right, I must take written notice to quit in 30 days.
The notice to take effect immediately the rent
became in arrear. 1-Ie said I should have hung back
until I was forced to go.
(3) As I expect to be called any day, and as there
is no other house available here in any way suitable,
1 have had to make arrangements for the sale of
my furniture and the transport of my family- to
Melbourne immediately on receiving orders to report
myself.
(4) My said landlord is a Justice of the Peace.
(5) My rent has never been in arrear since" I
became the said landlord’s tenant.
(6) I am one of the-picked spots of the club.
(7) The said landlord. is a‘ man of substance and
a considerable property, owner.
(8) l have had the extreme pleasure of kicking
him off my premises; and 1 have reported the facts
of the case to the Hon. the Minister for Defence and
the Hon. the State Premier.
Dear Bulletin,— The war ended almost
as soon as it began, and Britain is obli-
terated, and its coat of arms is defaced, and
its sun has gone down with ignominy and
contempt into the ocean wave. Never again
will it look up. This was how it all hap-
pened. Two Hebrews, who used to give me
reasonable credit, became afraid that I would
lose my job through the downfall of our
civilisation or some other beastly old animal.
They mobilised themselves —nowadays every-
body is mobilised save the people of the
Falkland Islands—-and advanced on me in
close order. Their artillery, in the shape
of a writ, was pushed in front. Their light
cavalry, consisting of a time-payment furni-
ture agreement, on which six instalments
were overdue, covered the country and cut
off supplies. As Tennyson or Macaulay or
Shelley put it —I forget which of them put
it, and I recite from memory:—
Their light-armed ships blockade the ports,
Their archers hold the mountain forts,
Their horsemen tread the harvest down.
The river, black with many a mast,
On twelve proud bridges have they passed,
And pitched their mighty camp at last
Before Gain’s imperial town.
The quotation may be wrong in places, but
in the hurry and scuffle of war no one can
be wliollv accurate. I faced the enemy in

“Moana”: A year or two ago Bertie
Wright, as Timothy Gibbs, was creating a
roar of laughter nightly in “Our Miss Gibbs”
by holding a lost umbrella up to the receiver
of a telephone and asking the lady at the
other end of the wire if it was hers. A few
years earlier, when the telephone was new,
a coipic paper pub-
lished a drawing-
depicting a dude
dressed in a suit of
pyjamas speaking into
a telephone in the
early morning and
saying: “Excuse me
for speaking to you in
this rig.” Both of
those alleged jokes
have come true re-
cently, it now being
possible to see by wire.
The device is fitted
into a box that can
be attached to any
telephone, and with it
the persons using the
instrument can see
each other while they
are speaking. It can
be used in conjunc-
tion with wireless,
and so scientists are
predicting that shortly
Australasians at home
will be able to appre-
ciate Uncle Cohen’s
bright smile when he,
at Loandon, collects
his dividends on their
pawned assets. Now,
will somebody please
invent a machine that
will enable the bor-
row and bust crowd
to look upon the face
of Posterity and feel ashamed of themselves.

One awkward outcome of the war,, fiom
the point of view of certain folks, is the
automatic disappearance of the rights at
civil law of “alien enemies.” Thus when
Carl Tauber, a German subject, of long
Australian standing, recently asked Vic.
Insolvency Judge Moule for a certificate of
discharge from his debts, and argued that
the Crown’s right to confiscate an alien’s
property extended only to corporal goods
and not to debts, his Honor replied that the
Crown didn’t want Tauber’s debts at all,
whether he was an enemy or not. The posi-
tion was that at present he couldn’t recover
debts, and the Court wasn’t going to wipe
out his debts either. The _ Judge’s point
was that so far as the civil law went
Tauber simply didn’t exist. Legally he was
a nameless corpse.
“Sawmill Charlie”: If the Croatians at
home are anything like the Croatians in
Maoriland, Francis Joseph isn’t likely to get
much solid help from them. They’re Slavs,
nearly all these so-called Austrian gum-
diggers, and would make poor soldiers on
the side of any piratical old Emperor in a
campaign against others of the same blood.
In fact, I’m inclined to think they’d make
mighty poor fighting stuff in any scrap last-
ing over five minutes in a tight place. It is
true they dig sometimes as if pursued by 17
devils. They can live on the smell of next
week’s dinner, and are not addicted to con-
siderations of personal comfort. But, physi-
cally and mentally, they are poor stuff. About
most of them there’s some indefinable quality
of pathos; they appear to bear the centuries-
old brand of an iron heel. They work, as a
rule, with the resigned, plodding persistency
one notices among Chinamen. They live in
shacks and hovels. They are given to wild
bursts of Booze, to running amok and kindred
frenzies. But if one could turn loose an army
in the frame of mind I once saw an Austrian
digger occupy at Dargaville, they’d clean up
all Europe. This wild cat, full of cheap
spirit, wrecked two pubs and three shops,
shot a couple of citizens and knifed four or
five others. It was only when the firebell
rang and the whole population fell, on the
alien en masse that he hoisted the white
flag. Then he relapsed into a wooden silence,
which had not been broken 12 months later
when I heard of him in a lunatic asylum.
The war supplies a whole essay on the
necessity for providing every ship, which is
something better than a ferry-boat, with
wireless telegraphy. The economical Ger-
mans send many of their big vessels to sea
without wireless. One after another of
these has plodded, honestly and full of good
faith, into a hostile port, because the cap-
tain didn’t know that strife had commenced.
Australia has arrested some and _ hopes to
get more. Probably a few British and
French ships have struck the same sort of
disaster. The maddest man on the seas
just now is the skipper who takes a ship
worth £lOO,OOO up to the old, familiar wharf,
and finds himself and his vessel arrested by
an official who earns only £3 a week. A
mere trifle spent on a wireless installation
would have enabled him to turn back and
sprint for home, or else flee for neutral
waters. Last Saturday all the lager beer
on earth wouldn’t have cooled the feelings
of the captain of the Lothringen, because
he steamed into Melbourne harbor, and Mel-
bourne spread out its arms and gathered him
in. He was almost wild enough to speak
disrespectfully of the Yarra.

“Non-Com.”: That Kaiser Bill never ex-
pected Russia to call his bluff is made evi-
dent by his lack of preparation for the
rush on France. For years it has been
known that the German plan of campaign
was to demoralise France with a sudden
dash on Paris, and then throw a million men
back across to meet the Bear as he came
in over the frontier in his slow, deliberate
way. When the Morocco affair threatened
to end in war it leaked out that the German
preparations for the dash were hideously
complete. German agents were in all the
frontier .towns to seize the available auto-
mobiles, to cut telegraphs and to blow up
railway bridges and viaducts. On this
occasion, it seems, none of these prepara-
tions were made; when the Germans
rushed into Belgium there was no commis-
sariat even, and the unfortunate troops were
fed on lead and steel instead of meat and
bread. The Prussians at the rear were so
busy rushing men to the front that- those
who got there first were two days without
food, except what they could scratch up
for themselves. But they were mostly
Bavarian and Wurtemburg soldiers —
people, in the eyes of the swanking Prussians,
fit only to be food for cannon. The whole
conduct of the dash suggests that William
was the Unready. Meantime an army corps
has been wiped out and a week of most
valuable time lost; and no doubt across the
track to Paris are, by this time, whole series
of trenches waiting for the defending force
to snugly step into.

“Suppressed” : Allow me to raise my voice
—anonymously, of course. This tremen-
dous fight for freedom has inspired me to
raise the standard of revolt and (without
mentioning my own name) to denounce the
so-called sanctity of the home. I have a
home —at least my wife has. lam the
honorary parcels express and week-end
gardener. lam also the doer-of-odd-jobs
and the night watchman. I pay an indem-
nity of two-thirds of my weekly salary, in
consideration of which she is supposed to
refrain from invading my peace of mind and
bombarding me with questions. Needless
to say she doesn’t respect her solemn treaty
obligations any more than a certain Power,
now very much on the job. I am in a con-
tinual state of war, but no Power or group
of Powers will come to my assistance. It
seems horribly unjust, so I am putting in
my claim while there is assistance avail-
able. lam not a nation or a. people —but
I am one people, and I consider that I
have a small claim for protection. Any
benevolent Power with time to spare will
be supplied with my name and address on
production of a reference from an ordained
clergyman or other responsible person.

You’d never think we were at war ;
All round the church is still;
Doves coo, and o’er the vestry door
A sparrow whets his bill.
I used to love this place of peace,
But now my bosom swells;
I’d like to boil the choir in grease
And madly ring the bells.
I yearn to Are the infant school,
And push the verger round,
And dash stones in the goldfish pool
And make some vulgar sound.
I was quite short with two old maids
Who called just now! Who cares ■
For gifts of dozens of new-laids
And silly jumble fairs!
Who wants to visit dear old dames
And hold cats on one’s knee?
I want to see some blood and flames!
Oh, dash their cups of tea !
At last!! The vicar picks a flow’r!
He’s stooping down! TAKE CARE ! !
Too late 1 this is my e-vil hour !
I’ve GOT to kick him ! THERE !! !

HOW I RUBBED AWAY A STONE OF
FAT FROM MY HIPS AND ABDOMEN
IN TWO WEEKS’ TIME.
For years I tried every possible means to
bide the excessive fat on my hips and
abdomen, it seemed as if it had accumulated
on just the two places that it would be most
noticeable. Everything that I tried failed to
make me any thinner. But thanks to a lady
friend who had studied herboristerie, I
learned the secret of a harmless plan which
enabled me to rub away with ease a stone
of useless fat in only two weeks.
The plan is so simple and some of my
friends seem so glad to know of it that I
think there may be sister readers of the
Sydney Bulletin who have perhaps been
longing for this very advice. First, get
from any good chemist 1 dram of quassia
chips and 3 ounces of cirola bark extract.
Take them home and, after putting the
quassia chips in a pan, pour over it a tea-
cupful of boiling water. When this has
stood for about a minute, strain through a
cloth and add the cirola bark extract. Then
pour the mixture into a bottle and apply
it every night and morning with’ your hands
for about ten minutes, using a circular
movement. Each time you can almost see
some of the fat melt away. At first I
thought it must be a dream, but. when I
measured there was no longer any doubt. I
soon grew too slender for my clothes and had
to have them taken in, but it was a pleasure
to do it. and I shall feel eternally grateful
to Ihe lady who showed me how easy it was
to regain my youthful figure. —E. E. A. "

PERSONAL
ITEMS
JOHN DOWNER, K. C., ex-Premier, ex-
Senator and Father of S.A. Far.
It is just possible that the poor showing
which the German host has made in Bel-
gium is owing to it being 10 months too
previous. Next June will see the 100th
anniversary of the victory—largely a Ger-
man victory—at Waterloo. Now if it hap-
pens that Bluchers ghost arranged to rise
from the grave on the day of the Waterloo
centenary (and no one knows what arrange-
ments a ghost may make) it is the Kaiser’s
previousness which has deprived his country
of the services of a magnificent old spectre.
Even the dry bones of “Marshal Forwards”
might be an inspiriting asset these times.
But if Germany has been too soon in one
sense it has been too late in another. The
war has been postponed until the German
population has outgrown the local food
supply. It depends to some extent, though
not to anything like the same extent, as
Britain, on imported victuals. Austria also
has just got over the line at which there is
surplus food for export. With land sup-
plies cut off by the anger of Russia, and
sea supplies stopped by the French and
British fleets, Germany may, for the first
time in history, have to fight with nothing
save old Rhine wine in its stomach.
UNDOUBTED LOYALTY TO THE
BRITISH FLAG.
THE HOSTESS: “How did your friend Oscar
express himself on the war with his
country?”
THE GUEST: “Very displeased , I assure you.
He absolutely refused to drink any more
German beer.”
The law is wholly unable to see a good
thing when the good thing comes along. Last
week a German resident of Sydney, who
had been insolvent for no less than 21 years,
but had now paid his creditors in full, asked
the Bankruptcy Court for a discharge. This
being a time when Germany is at war with
us the Court should have given three cheers,
because one of the hostile race had gone
broke. Then it should have, given three
more cheers because he had paid up, and no
British citizen had lost by him. Three more
cheers might not have been out of place in
granting the discharge, so that the bankrupt
might go away instead of staying here to
inspect the fortifications or otherwise serve
Kaiser William. And if an odd cheer had
been thrown in by way of discount it
wouldn’t have been amiss. That would have
made 10 cheers altogether. Instead of which
applause the case of Emil Julius Silberberg
was adjourned till next month on the ground
that he was some sort of a public enemy.
Now, how much adjournment will make any
difference to his position as a public enemy?
“Budda”: Died recently, on the rough
track between Groggin and Corryong (Vic.),
John Riley, said in those parts to be the
original of Paterson’s “Man from Snowy
River.” For 30 years Riley had charge of
Groggin, a cattle station at the foot of
Kosciusko, 50 miles, from Corryong. It is
accessible only by bridle or pack tracks. The
main track keeps to the Victorian side all
the way, and in crossing the precipitous Indi
Gorge much rough country is met with.
Anyone unacquainted with the Upper
Murray could scarcely conceive the difficul-
ties which beset the bushmen who, under
the Kosciusko guide, Finlayson, undertook to
carry a dying mate through gorge and ravine
to medical attention. The first obstruction
was the Hermit Hill (2000 ft.)., five miles
from Groggin. Snow was falling and the
sides of the precipice became so slippery and
the cold so intense that the bearers had to
abandon the stretcher. A mountain pony
was introduced, and Riley placed' in the
saddle. Butler, the lightest of the party,
mounted behind and supported the dying
man till Surveyor’s Creek Junction was
reached. Death met them there. The body
was, however, conveyed per stretcher, and
buggy to Corryong, where it was buried.
Riley was an open-hearted busliman, a
superb horseman, and a fearless stockhand
with the homing instinct of a dingo.

“Kola”: Among the many notices of the
late O. W. Palmer, of Terang, I have not
seen any reference to an ill-starred expen-
ment made by him in the ’Bo’s. Palmer had
a dairy farm at Tooram (Vic.), a few miles
east of Warrnambool. Having some diffi-
culty in securing hands, he conceived the
brilliant idea of importing colored labor.
So he brought out a shipment of Hindoos
and set them at the cows. But the Ram
Chunders didn’t take kindly to the work,
and Palmer had great trouble with them.
They were dirty, also; but that was a sec-
ondary consideration. They started light-
ing, and one turbaned shirt-tail nearly slew
another. The climax came when they joined
forces and threatened to smash up Palmer
arid defile liis bones. The Law then stepped
in. and Gunga and his compatriots were
fired home to Delhi’s coral strand, where the
waters go down at Lahore.
Old Alfred Allen, who. 25 years ago, was
the laurel-crowned harpist _of Bulletin
political cartoons, is very seriously ill. Just
before he was elected for Paddington (1887)
lie published a book of poems, “Australian
Verse Drift”; hence the laurel and the harp.
In his early days he was known as the
Quaker noet; in the House he was a faint
echo of Henry Parkes. He was the useful
author of the Early Closing Bill and the
kindly founder of the Sydney Night Refuge
and Soup Kitchen in Kent-street. The
building in which he established this charity
was the first home. in Sydney of James
Dowling, Chief Justice, who occupied it in
1828. It still stands. Mr. Allen’s father
was a soap-maker, who set himself up in
that business at Blackwattle Swamp three-
quarters of a century ago. In those days,
before tinned meat and freezing chambers
were invented, soap-making included sheep
and cattle-boiling, the surplus stock (when
there was any) being thus got rid of for
their tallow.
“10-2-4”: Let me make amends. About a
month ago I cited young Quinlan, who
killed a woman in a Sydney hotel under
peculiar circumstances, as an instance of
a dangerous psychopath. I went on the
reports of his trial, which made the crime
appear motiveless to the point of insanity.
A friend of the youngster’s blameless and
altogether estimable family assures me that
the reports fail to do justice to the actual
situation. It appears that he had particular
need of money, that he went to his victim’s
room to get it, and that he attacked her in
a sudden panic of-being discovered. If this
is so it puts him in a much less dangerous
class than the Sadistic lunatic. The mur-
derer-for-gain may be cured of his tendency
by a steady and decently requited job, where-
as the other sort of offender remains a public
peril till he dies.

Now that Britain is involved in a big war
one wonders how Joshua Ratcliff, the Mel-
bourne “conscientious objector” to compul-
sory training, feels on the subject. After
selling all that he had—at a sacrifice, no
doubt —and wiping the dust of “military-
ridden” Australia off his shoes, Joshua de-
parted with his lads for Europe—and peace !
A line from Joshua, explaining how he likes
peace, would be very interesting.
Died at Glen Innes last week, aged 87,
Sophie, widow of William Gunsell Burgis.
She was a Hawkesbury native, a daughter
of Thomas Dargin, a free man, and one of
546 Hawkesbury settlers who signed an ad-
dress of welcome to Governor Bligh in 1807.
Bligh was good to the Hawkesbury, and
put the curb on the military “trader” who
fleeced the settlers of his produce, giving
him rum in return. Even rum has its
limitations.

Maoriland’s Legislative Council, which is
busy reforming itself into an elective instead
of a nominee* body, has seven life members
left in its voting strength of 39. These are
W. D. 11. Baillie (appointed 1861), 11. J.

Mrs. Katherine Dendy, widow of Henry
Dendy, of Brighton and Walhalla (Vic.),
died in Sydney a few days ago. Henry
Dendy was an old Port Phillip colonist who
took advantage of a land regulation of 1841
whereby a person paying £5120 into the
Treasury might fix upon any portion of
unreserved territory and require the authori-
ties to survey for him eight square miles.
On June 8, 1841, Governor Gipps notified
that eight of these special surveys had been
applied for. One of the eight applicants
was Henry Dendy, who took his kingdom on
Port Phillip Bay, including the whole of
what is now Brighton. Besides getting the
land at £1 per acre, Dendy and the others
had the right to nominate “one of the labor-
ing class” for a free passage to the colony
for every £2O paid.
“Chasuble”: Monsignor Cerretti, the Apos-
tolic Delegate who is to be located in Sydney,
was to be consecrated at St. Peter’s, Borne,
on July 19, the officiating prelate being Car-
dinal Del Val, Secretary of State. To be
sure, this would be in the chapel space at
one side of the capacious floor, yet it is a
mighty honor. Putting this and that to-
gether, I don’t think the Australian arch-
bishops and bishops relish Cerretti. Cer-
tainly I do not find any of them gushing
about him. Nor does the. religious press
gush, and it takes its cue from the leaders.
Meanwhile, sundry Catholic Australians have
been interviewing Cerretti in Borne. But
not a syllable can be extracted from him as
to what he is going to do, or what are his
powers. At the same time, he is most loqua-
cious. He is only about 40. and therefore
younger than almost any other magnate of
the Church here. Thus we await the bland
Sphinx. On appearance, he might be a
nephew of Archbishop Carr.
The London papers lately got a story
from the Pie Commissioner’s office about: a
remarkable discovery. In November, 1849,
Margaret Cameron wrote from Fort YV il-
liam, in Scotland, telling brother Donald
about a legacy. The yarn runs that Donald
dropped* the letter on the road between
Castlemaine and Taradale (Vic.), and for
34 years it lay there, till Edward Taylor, of
Taradale, found it. Edward kept the letter
which had been lying on the road for 31
years, and 31 years later, when somebody
advertised in Melbourne for the missing heir,
Taylor rooted out the ancient document and
sent it on spec, to rhe advertiser. That
more or less fixed i Km, aid. who was
earthed in Queensland aged 90. Tiie
Bulletin hates to disbelieve a word of it;
still it can’t help remembering that the name
of the man who is alleged to have left the
money unclaimed for 65 years was Cameron,
and Donald Cameron at that. But perhaps
that’s really the remarkable discovery.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
The conference between the Federal Gov-
ernment and State Premiers# adopted the
proposals made here last week for enabling
vhe gold resources of the country to do the
utmost of which they mre capable in the
present world crisis. The idea was to turn
some of the credit of the country into cash
when cash may be badly needed. The
method suggested for carrying out that idea
was that the Commonwealth should issue
notes against deposits of gold with the
Federal Treasury—a £J note against ss. or
Us. Sd. worth of metal. The scheme actually
adopted was to authorise the issue of 20s.
worth of paper against ss. worth of gold in
the case of State Governments, and 6s. Bd. of
gold in the case of private banks. A State
Treasury with £500,000 of gold can there-
fore command £2,000,000 of legal tender, and
in the same circumstances a bank can com-
mand £1,500,000 worth. Besides the gold.
I he State or the bank will deposit its promise
to pay; in short, the Commonwealth will
allow it an overdraft at the rate of 4 p.c.
This is very good business for the Common-
wealth, which will thus be making 4 p.c. out
of its own credit —a very handy source of
revenue, at a time when revenue is badly
wanted on account of the shrinkage in cus-
toms receipts. It is equally good business
for the States and the banks, which will get
money at 4 p.c. at a time when money is
very hard to get no matter what inducement
is offered. And it is also good business for
the individual citizens, who are thus enabled
to draw against their own resources at a time
when, without some such scheme, their
resources would be of little value to them,
and might be seriously jeopardised for want
of a trifle to carry on with. If any legisla-
tion is needed to give effect to the scheme,
there is no doubt that it will be promptly
passed.
The whole plan, of course, depends upon
the fact that Commonwealth notes are legal
tender. To issue them without any backing
of gold at all would be dangerous, seeing that
the Commonwealth undertakes to pay them
in gold ; but a reserve running from 25 p.c.
upwards is ample for the purpose. The other
75 p.c. is as good as gold. Of that there
can be no shadow of doubt whatever. It
will be absolutely impossible for the Com-
monwealth to issue on the terms above-
mentioned more notes than it can pay. If
every 7 ounce of gold in Australia were dumped
into the Commonwealth Treasury and paper-
currency of four times the gold value were
issued against it, the issue would still be
well protected. The plain fact of the matter
is that the Commonwealth is mortgaging all
the taxable wealth of the country, and that
wealth isn’t a mere four times more than
the gold reserves of the country—it is
upwards of 20 times more. Behind every
note that is issued stands Australia, and all
its gold mines and its coal and silver and
copper mines, and its sheep and its cattle,
and its wool and its wheat, and its timber,
and its grazing lands, and its farming lands
and its orchards, and its shipping and
its stocks of merchandise, and the labor and
the earning capacity of all its people. If
anybody feels a doubt about a Commonwealth
note with that backing, he is a fool who is
beyond arguing with. And if, under pre-
sent circumstances, he pushes his doubt to
the extent of depreciating the Commonwealth
note issue, lie is an unpatriotic fool to boot.

The dividend on the old shares comes down to
0 p.c., which is 1 p.c. under the standard:
hence the directors notify that they have
“decided to raise the price of gas to 3s. lOd.
per 1000 ft., of which due notice will be
given.” The standard price fixed by the Act
of 1012 is 3s. 6d.; and for every Id. charged
beyond that the dividend on the old (“A”
and “B”) shares must he reduced i p.c.—
unless a Judge will certify that there has
been an increase in the' cost of production
sufficient to warrant the increase in price.
Thus far the co. has not been able to satisfy
the proper authority on this point. Some-
thing, certainly, has produced a slump in
profits, and it doesn’t appear to be a fall in
consumption. For the first half of 1913 the
revenue was £322.661; for the past half-
year £343.112. The directors refer to “the
partial dislocation of business and abnormal
conditions existing during the period of tran-
sition at: Mortlake,” and this has, no doubt,
been responsible for excessive costs and con-
sequent shrinkage of profits. In the half-
year to December 31 £311,064 was collected
from private gas-consumers. For manufac-
ture (which is kept apart from distribution)
the cost in wages and gratuities was £45,535.
In the manufacture of gas which, so far as
private consumers are concerned, brought in
£200,398 in the past half-year, the wages
cost was up to £49,337. On account of the
“partial dislocation” before mentioned, some
or all of the higher costs and lower profits
are, however, only temporary, and the half-
year’s figures are nothing to go upon. There
may be justification, apart from these figures,
for an increase in the price; for, in
view of the compromise embodied in the
Act, it would not be a fair thing to require
the co. to pay such higher wages as would
necessitate a reduction of the standard divi-
dend. But. if the higher wages cost is due to
a temporary disturbance (the result of chang-
ing-over from Darling Harbor to Mortlake,
the result of which will be to reduce costs
and increase protiis presently) the argument
for an advance does not apply. Presumably
the co. gets something for disturbance as
well as for the actual Darling Harbor pro-
perty taken over by the Government. If it
gets paid for disturbance in a lump sum, it
can’t reasonably ask to be paid for the same
disturbance in (lie shape of a higher price
for gas. Still, so far as one can see, the law
allows it. The dividend having been reduced
1 p.c., no matter how, the price can, it seems,
be automatically raised 4d. It looks as if there
is a flaw in this Act—as in most others.
During the half-year there has been a
heavy expenditure on capital account
amounting to £400,788; and as temporary
loans raised during this period were £263,462
(making the total £662,113) the directors
propose a debenture issue to fix the debt. At
present, of £2,522,616 charged to capital,
£1,275,000 is represented by “A” and “B”
shares (limited to 10 p.c., unless the price
of gas is put below 3s. 6d.), £255.000 by “C”
shares (limited to 7 p.c.), and £300,000 by
debentures; the balance (£692.616) has so
far been raised by overdrafts, etc.

This was one of the litter of the Queensland
boom of the late ’ciggies, and, like the rest
of them, it soon < id for the surgeon’s
knife. There were £1 shares and 15s. shares;
the former, on a revaluation of assets, were
written down to 14s. 6d. and the latter
to 9d. That left the co. with no capital to
speak of; and it had a business to watch.
Nevertheless a few live people with money
saw that there might be the foundations
for a profitable trade; so they took the thing
in hand, suppressed dividends for a time,
put every penny of profit back into the busi-
ness, called up more capital until there was
£60,000 of it, and gradually got the shares
out among free-house owners and others who
could do the co. most good. Meantime
Queensland was feeling entitled to extra
drinks on the strength of fine wool clips,
good meat prices and record sugar harvests.
This co. also saw a chance to specialise in
bottled stuff, and jumped at it, all the more
eagerly because it reckoned that, if the
Wowser should prevail and pubs were shut,
it was the bottle trade that would get the
most advantage. When its competition and
that of another smallish concern began to be
worth taking notice of, a beer war started,
and there was a check in the growth of pro-
fits! Then things were arranged in the usual
manner, and, with improved premises and
new plant supplied partly out of the new
capital and partly out of the co.’s own
savings, trade moved on rapidly. The next
table shows some details: —
1905. 1912. 1913. 1914.
Capital .. . . £16,025 £45,000 £60,000 £60,000
Liabilities .. 21,819 26,794 28,139 26,709
Land, plant, goodwill
and properties . . 31,000 44,975 51,267 50,636
Stock .. .. 7,124 14,125 19,551 16,145
Book debts .. 5,826 22,612 24,654 32,347
Cash, etc. .. 417 773 916 7,630
Gross profits .. 14,022 31,370 32,023 41,907
Management . . 11,715 20,065 22,313 26,502
For many years, as shown above, the bulk
of the profits were written off, and the money
used to make replacements, etc. Apparently
the management reckons that assets are
worth at least book value; wherefore a con-
tingency fund of £7OOO has shown up among
the reserves, and £3870 is now added to it.
The arrival of this visible contingency re-
serve seems, in short, to be a hint that the
directors find that £7OOO more than was
necessary had been written off assets; and
that being so it looks as though the £7OOO
plus the £3780 now appropriated for that
fund might properly be added to the re-
serves. But even if the £3780 wasn’t true
profit, the year’s financial results would be
splendid; there would still be over £6OOO in
excess of the 9 p.c. div. Of course Brisbane
has had more money to burn than it can
hope to have generally, and the co.’s profits
may not keep up where they are; neverthe-
less" the concern has unquestionably worked
up a good, solid connection, and lias estab-
lished a popularity which it could hardly
have expected 10 years ago.

Included with the profit in the last line is
£2389 written off; and as the bulk at this
is against wear and tear of P l^’ c( |“ e rT t
profit is considerably less than £4989. How-
ever, it is substantial, and there is no visible
reason why it shouldn’t continue so. As '
matter of "fact, having most of the biggest
co-operative butter factories behind it, anc
therefore starting out with a ready-made
connection, it ought to have shown good pio-
fits from the beginning. Unfortunately it
tried to climb over difficulties which were
put in its way by getting a large stock ot
timber from Japan, and that was a disas-
trous move. Gradually that .stock was got
rid of—at a heavy loss —and no trouble is
found now in securing regular supplies ot
M.L. white pine. While the £30,000 worth
of Japanese timber was being worked pit,
interest and insurance had a big appetite.
To June, 1913, they cost the co. £3253 tor
the 12 months. In the past year the out-
goings under these heads were got down to
£2009. And they should go still lower when
more share capital is got in. Presumably
this will be done. At present only £lo,oio
is paid, and that isn’t enough to cover even
the stocks of timber in hand. Nor is it
likely that those stocks can be much reduced;
even with a timber-drying plant the wood
must have some seasoning. M.L. white pine
needs probably as little as any in the world,
because it is practically tasteless and odor-
less to begin with; but two months is none
too little to keep even this stuff, in view of
the use to which it is to be put. For the
other £25,000 or thereabouts required to
finance the business, and represented by a
freehold (which the co. got at the bargain
counter), book debts (£7228) and machinery,
the co. has to depend on the bank (£11,783),
an advance from its foster-parent, the
Coastal Farmers’ Co-op. Society (£4229),
and sundry creditors (£8180). Altogether,
the accounts show that the co. has made an
excellent recovery; and. besides promising a
future good return on the co-operators’ capi-
tal, it is even now unquestionably rendering
them fine service by providing them with
boxes at a price which they couldn’t have
hoped for had the co. not been born. In fact,
it was only born when the price was gradu-
ally pushed up from Is. Id. to Is. Bd. At
that time costs were much lower than they
are now; yet the present price of boxes runs
no higher than Is. 6|d.

Capital (paid) £10,000; reserves £12,795;
unpaid, but subscribed, capital £40,000.
Tinder ordinary circumstances that would
be quite sufficient security, provided the
risks extended no further than the estates
in hand. But the shareholders’ funds have
to back £16,637 of “sundry debtors” as
well. If these are advances made to un-
realised trust estates, mainly for the pur-
pose of paying probate duty, the security
should be good ; but “Father of Fifteen” an,d
“Constant Reader” like to make sure of
everything before they decide who is to
administer affairs when they are too dead
to do much administering themselves. And,
especially as the co. is up against a Govern-
ment Trustee, it would remove _ a doubt
which can’t be good for it if this item were
either reduced considerably or a fuller ex-
planation than “sundry debtors, £16,637”
given. To finance these loans the co. has
liabilities (thrown in one heap and labelled
“sundry creditors”) of £7307. The balance
of the funds is in a deposit with the State
Treasurer £5OOO. mortgages £5525, bank £731,
and property £3258. As usual, the business
shows a handsome profit, though the direc-
tors are evidently indisposed to suggest that
the figures will last. Anyway, 5 p.c. of the
present 15 p.c. div. is handed out in the
shape of a bonus.

The capital is now £106,250, and that and
the reserves provide enough for trading.
The liabilities amount to only £14,171 ; but
against that there is freehold and leasehold
property valued at £28.244. The financial
position is therefore very easy: if the co.
takes risks it any rate does it with its own
money, and doesn’t endorse anybody else’s
bills. How it would fare under specially
adverse Australian conditions isn’t too clear.
In the first half of 1904, when things were
bad in Sydney, it made a profit at the rate
of 12 p.c. p.a. on its then capital and
reserves; last half-year, under conditions of
general prosperity, it made just 10 p.e. p.a.
That looks as though the co. is liable to make
better profits when times are bad than when
they are good. Still, if bad conditions were
to last, a lot of the gilt might have to come
off the gingerbread. It may be that the co.
made no big losses in the bad times because
they were quickly followed by good times.
Anyway, in tliose bad times the co. set aside
some of its profits to build up a contingency
fund to allow of shrinkage in securities.
Luck stood to it —prosperity returned, and
the securities didn’t want protecting. If
any such provision has been made during the
recent long period of high prices for every-
thing, there is no trace of it in the accounts.
Perhaps there is a secret stocking some-
where. If not, it looks as though a time like
this might see the profits reduced
through a bad shrinkage in the value of
the co.’s pledges, etc. However, the writer con-
fesses with shame that he doesn’t know as
much about the innards of this business as
lie would like to. He never ran a Monty
I’eet. He has tried to get a treatise on the
Art and Mysteries of Pawnbroking, and
can’t. He appealed to an American college
that teaches everything by post. It replied
offering him special terms for a course of
card games—apparently the nearest thing
these univei’sal instructors, had in stock. In
desperation he annealed to Napthali, who is
in the business. “Learn?” exclaimed Napthali.
“Here’s noddings to learn. You lose und you
lose, und you go on doen id. Ain’dt id,
Rachel?”

Tasmania had a dry time early in the year,
and it looks as though there was a run on
the brewery to moisten things a bit. Any-
way, net revenue from beer, etc., was £14.495
as agaiust £10,171 for the first half of 1913.
There was no corresponding increase in
operating expenses (salaries, travelling,
“trade,” etc.) ; so this meant a large in-
crease in profits. In addition, £670 has been
dropped into the “Property Reserve Fund,”
evidently profit on property sales —portion
of the cumbersome Cascade Estate, perhaps.
But whether the extra demand for beer is
to be a regular thing, or whether it is merely
a passing burst, the accounts don’t so much
as whisper. One satisfying result of the
exti'a profits and the sale of assets is that
the co. has been able to make an attack on
its overdraft, which stood six months ago at
£24,451. Now it has been carved down to
£19,412. Unfortunately a large amount of
the co.’s capital is in land which isn’t
earning its keep, and the co. appears to be
doing its best to replace it with something
more lucrative. The directors are finding it
a long job, though. Other liabilities, mostly
mortgages, are heavy, and the debt is
£113,124—a big weight for a co. with a capi-
tal of £lOB,OOO, and all the heavier because
much of it is unproductive.
The next table shows how various items
have moved :—-

The fact that so much interest is being paid
on unproductive assets explains the smallness
of the profits. However, this dead-weight
should be got rid of by degrees, and, maybe,
at prices in excess of book values. Mean-
time the company is building up a very fair
Australian trade, and, locally, is in the for-
tunate position of having no Cold Tea party
of any magnitude against it, though there is
no saying when Tasmania may be
afflicted with that just as it is with potato
blight and King O’Malley.
* * * *
Half the mine’s at Broken Ilill are either
closed or closing down, and the others
are for the present working half time;
copper production at the big Cloncurry (Q.)
mines lias ceased, and there probably isn’t
one small copper producer at work in Aus-
tralia, the buyers having shut absolutely.
The resurrection of Cobar has been post-
poned indefinitely; and tin-mining has prac-
tically ceased throughout the Common-
wealth. the buyers of this metal also having
put their books away in the safe.
Yet with men and mines available there
is no reason whatever why Australia should
stop producing the industrial metals which
the world will want to buy as soon as the
war is over. The only question is one of
finance. And that is' not overwhelmingly
difficult. Three years or so ago, when the
markets were not propitious, many tens or
thousands of tons of Broken Hill zinc-lead
concentrates were stacked at Burns, on the
N.S.AY.-S.A. border, and they were there
for 12 months at least. If they could be
financed then, the same thing can be done
now. In that case the buyers, mostly Ger-
man, leaned up against Australian banks.
The latter presumably made advances
against metal certificates, which is the usual
practice. The same thing could be done
now, with a few modifications to meet the
circumstances.
The first question is: Who should do the
financing? It might be done most comfort-
ably through the credit of the Common-

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow lOin. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2
