Federal Treasury fl in gold, and the State
Governments may draw £4 in notes for £1
in gold. The Commonwealth, that is to say,
lends the banks in the one case £2, and in
rhe other case £3, and gets 4 p.c. for it. Now
the banks might possibly advance out of the
money thus borrowed enough to finance the
producing mines, taking the product as
security. Whether they will feel justified in
doing this business is another matter. The
metal prices at which the advances are to
be made would, of course, be a matter of
arrangement. Whoever made the advance
would not make it to the full present value
of the metals. But copper at £4B, lead at
£l4, zinc at £l7 10s.. silver a.t 2s. and tin
at £lOO ought to be perfectly safe proposi-
tions.
On advances at such prices it would be
impossible in most cases to pay the present
rates of wages. But that difficulty could be
got over by paying such a reduced rate of
wages as an accountant might certify to be
necessary, issuing at the same time a coupon
entitling the holder to an additional payment
on the sale of the output if the price then
warranted it—so much extra per day for a
realisation of (say) £1 in excess of £4B for
copper, and so on. (In practice it would
probably be best to pay the excess on the
basis of the average metal prices 12 months
after peace is proclaimed.) Though the
banks might handle this business it would
be an advantage in many ways if the Com-
monwealth worked the whole of it through
the Commonwealth Bank. There would,
for one thing, be le.ss difficulty in persuad-
ing the men to take a reasonable view of
the matter of reduced payments, and there
would be far less doubt about the value of
the excess coupons. Also the business would
come cheaper to the mining companies and
the miners, etc., for if the banks make the
advances and take the risks they will natu-
rally want to be paid for it; and this is a
case where no profits ought to be looked for,
and where the risk (if any) should be borne
by the Commonwealth. Besides, the banks
will have plenty to do in financing the pas-
toral, agricultural, manufacturing and ordi-
nary trading concerns.
If the Commonwealth finances the pro-
ductive mines, making advances against
metal certificates, there would no doubt have
to be a special issue of Commonwealth notes.
Ordinarily a Commonwealth note must have
25 p.c. of gold lodged and maintained against
it. A productive mining co. may have 1000
tons of copper, or 10,000 tons of zinc-lead
concentrates, but it may not have a single
golden sovereign to lodge with the Treasury.
But that should be no insuperable difficulty;
for if it is safe to issue £4B of Treasury
notes against £.12 of gold, surely in a time
like this, in order to continue the production
of wealth, of which the country is capable,
it is not only perfectly safe but highly desir-
able to issue £4B of Treasury notes against
a quantity of copper which, at the present
moment, is worth over £6O, has not been
worth less than £5O for many years, and,
on account of the vast consumption in this
war, will probably be worth £65 as soon as
peace is declared! The Commonwealth
Bank or any other authority might be
authorised to undertake this special issue
so as to keep it distinct from the ordinary
issue of Commonwealth notes. They would
of course carry interest : that is to say, the
company lifting them against a metal certi-
ficate would pay (say) 4 p.c. until January
1, 1916, when the note would be payable in
gold. This would mean revenue for the
Commonwealth ; and, much more important,
it would mean continuing the wealth-pro-
duction of the country. And this is of enor-
mous importance, because, war or peace,
rain or fine, our vast interest bill has to be
paid.. We shall want all the help we can
in that direction next year, for wheat and
flour shipments will be small on account of
the now-certain failure or partial failure of
the harvest in S. Aus., Victoria and Riverinh.;
and for the same reason the wool clip will
be deficient.
The first move must be taken by the Com-
monwealth Government. If the Prime Mini-
ster will call a conference, inviting Fisher
and Hughes. (Labor), BaiHieu and Colin
Templeton (mine-owners). Delprat or
Courntney (managers), Miller (Common-
wealth Bank) and F. H. Snow (metal
buyer, who knows all about metal account-
ancy), details could be arranged in a day.
* * * *
“Praeeo” : It must make a few men
thoughtful to observe that the six Australian
States have to renew loans next year amount-
ing to £16.714,320, by far .the largest lot to
be arranged until 1924, when £81,682,214
will have to be settled, chiefly by N.S.W.
and Q. The 1915 list runs thus': N.S.W.,
£3,881.081; Vic., £525.000; Q.. £11,722.800;
S.A.. £311,975; W.A., £85,840; and Tas.,
£181.624. The inherited curse of that fixed
date for repayment of our public loans will
ifiall heavily on Australia in the near years.
British Consols are interminable. Why not
Australian debentures? Why should the
terms of our loans range from 15 to 50
years? Why should there be so many vari-
ous forms of bonds, inscribed stock, deben-
tures and Treasury bills, with a very few
interminable securities? If for nothing else
but to simplify and make permanent our
debt obligations, their conversion by the
Commonwealth Government is imperative.
Not, let me add quickly, that an interminable
debt cannot be paid off. The great point is
that they can be liquidated in good times, as
British Consols are, while there is no danger
of a renewal having to be negotiated in bad
times when money is scarce and interest
rates are cruel.

Daniel Grove’s contribution to the discus-
sion of Australian diamonds: It is generous
of Mr. Havelock Smith to admit (B.
30/7/T4) that the Mount Boss co. has done
some useful work, and I daresay the mining
community at Copeton will be astonished to
learn, from such an impartial authority,
that the property was proved, by its earlier
owners, to yield seven carats of diamonds
per 100 loads of washdirt treated. No men-
tion has been made of the quality of the
tailings from. the old gear, nor has any
credit been given to the present owners for
making such an alleged low-grade proposi-
tion yield a good livelihood. It is difficult
to imagine that a man of Mr. . Havelock
Smith’s wide experience on the South
African and Australian diamond-fields would
allow new, virgin ground to remain un-
prospected ; yet it is said by men on the
field that for some years he has modestly
declined to. place his knowledge and capital
at the service of the mining community in a
search for large diamonds. I personally
take full responsibility for inducing my
Holland-Belgium principals to have the
field examined, and several propositions
tested, believing, as I did, and do. in the
reports made to me by Messrs. A. R. Pike,
Walter Hill, Peter Rooney and William
Cross, which have since been corroborated
by independent experts, whose credentials I
have reason to believe will compare favor-
ably with those of Mr. Havelock Smith. I
state publicly that Dr. Fuldaner, on whose
good name I allowed his group credit for
£BOOO cash expended by me, and whose in-
terests I have since upheld because I be-
lieve they mean to open the Australian fields
when the conditions are more favorable, has
done more for Australian diamonds than has
Mr. Havelock Smith, who now sits in judg-
ment on the entire industry.

Resolution of a meeting of diamond miners
held at Copeton (N.S.W.) on August 8:
“That we, representing the various diamond
mines of Copeton, express indignation con-
cerning Mr. Havelock Smith’s statements in
The Bulletin of July 80, regarding the in-
dustry and its prospects in this district, the
same being misleading in many points, and
in opposition to facts.”

The Mabel mine, at Wiseman’s Creek,
Oberon district (N.S.W.), has put up the
shutters after a brief career under the all-
solution system. Ore operated on was the
gossan capping of the copper lode, on which
the once fairly-payable Phoenix mine is situ-
ated. The gold is fine, derived from the
decomposition of the sulphides, and acid and
iron salts are present in appreciable
amounts. Underfoot the carbonates come
in, and working at any great depth is thus
precluded so far as cyaniding ore is con-
cerned. Still there is a pretty large quan-
tity to be obtained above the carbonates, as
the shoots (it may be one shoot) of gold
extend a considerable distance; and if the
gold could be saved, there are many worse
properties than little Mabel. But as only a
very poor extraction can be made by amal-
gamation, and as present proceedings indi-
cate that the all-solution process is not satis-
factory, the circumstances are not favorable.
WiTfc acid copper and iron salts to interfere
chemically with cyaniding, and with baryta
to give trouble mechanically, Mabel can
supply many obstacles in the race for wealth..
Low values and high difficulties are a bad
combination; but they were tackled plucltily,
and all honor to those who courageously
failed.
Hawkins Hill Reward (N.S.W.) is going
slow with development in order to put out
a good crushing. In July the mill crushed
90 tons for 89oz. A new reef has been cut
at the 200 ft. level from the Exhibition shaft,
and prospects so far have been about loz.
per ton for a width of 12in. The deepest
level at 320 ft. vertical has failed to pick up
the rich stone so long talked about, but
quartz leaders in favorable slate country
may lead to something better. There is
18in. of loz. stone at No. 1 winze, so calls
should not be necessary for some time.

Because it took £3849 to win tin worth
£3300 in six months at the Melbourne
Cope’s Creek claim at Tingha (N.S.W.),
the two plants working are to go on single
shift. No. 1 is on lb. tin but with costs
at over Bsd. per cubic yard, and the metal
where it is a bigger yardage will need to
be sluiced before there is a decent margin.
No. 2 plant costs, 10M. per cubic yard, are
even worse, but the ground is tight and
labor dear.
* * * *
Elsmore is another Tingha tin co. in
Jimmy Mackay’s office that feels the pinch
of hard times through the flopping of the
metal market, and it took £3765 to get tin
worth £3460. That isn’t payable, so one
shift is ordered. The consolation held
out to the shareholders is that the two
plants have at least 12 months ahead in
their present paddocks, and that the
machinery is in better order than ever
before.
Work at Cock’s Pioneer Eldorado. Vic.,
is on behalf of a mixed London-Melhonrne
crowd, with Y-Water Moline finding the
brains. Co. is to have a slap-up plant to
replace Jack Dane’s rattle-traps, as Bull
put up plenty of cash for the show, believ-
ing that, he had little to risk. From next
month on Moline will discover the truth
about the three-layer ground at Eldorado.
-Die co.s balance-sheet shows that he will
start with about £lO,OOO in hand and so
miss the financial gruelling got by the
Adelong Estates because of cutting into
heavy clay. Cock’s Pioneer’s new barge
will work up stream, and, as the old plant
cut out the paddock before Bull bought in.
Moline’s will get into the pay-dirt from the
start. He is wise to leave behind him the
big block of untested drift extending up to
the boundary of Langford’s claim. Dane's
old co. averaged per cubic yard about Is. sd.
from tin and gold, and paid divs. with a
patched-up plant. Tf Moline gets the same
grade working to where Dane’s plant
stopped, he will soon add to- the Collins
House gold reserves.

Outtrim-llowitt, having been elbowed out
of the Powlett Basin by the Victorian Gov-
ernment, has lit out for Kilcunda. The
old mine is a mere shadow of the past,
unable to bear State charges and working
costs and then give Private Enterprise a
bite. A whole lot of faith will have to be
packed behind Kilcunda, for it only counts
on a 24ft. seam near the shaft. A bit east,
bores show the coal thickening out to Sift.,
but with faults and high costs. The new
mine seems to have been got on job-lot
terms and is now at work. As the Outtrim
pit gave only 8000 tons of coal for the half-
year, the move-on clause had to be enforced.

Melbourne office of Lode Hill, Cooktown
(N.Q.), hands out its second half-
year’s accounts. The surplus is £2625,
of which £1283 is cash, and the
balance a valuation on 13 tons 2cwt. of
oxide on hand. On the same basis, work-
ing profit on an output of 47 tons 17cwt. of
tin oxide is £2099. The oxide therefore
goes into the accounts at practically £lOO
per ton. Gaps in the co.’s report are the
grade of the tin concentrates and working
costs, which, roughed out, appear to run to
about £54 10s. per ton of tin oxide. Stiff'
going this for a mine having plenty purling
water and an easy run for slum. First
return was 214 tons from 30,000 cubic yards;
the third 11} tons from 55,000 cubic yards.
On top of this the co. got into a cyclone in
the metal market, and, last of all, ran into
a belt of stones 24 chains wide left by the
original owners. Reading between the* lines
it looks as if Manager Northey had only a
toe of drift to sluice before he butted against
this. People down south await chairman
Turner’s reading of the lesson over this
little surprise packet.
Mount Lyell’s four-weekly output of blister
copper was 673 tons, the best for two years.
The amount smelted was 27,949 tons com-
pared with 26,434 tons for the previous four
weeks. Fine copper contained in blister
(665 tons) is 91 tons better than the pre-
vious return, and the average extraction has
improved to 2.41 p.c. copper per ton of ore,
although some of this improvement may be
due to the rich ore being obtained at the
lowest level, 1100 ft. The entire return was
665 tons copper, 34,2060 z. silver and 8760 z.
gold. Costs run to about £30,000 per month.

surviving.

TEN YEARS OLD.
APPENDICITIS FREQUENT.
NUMBERLESS SOCIETY PATIENTS.
[From Our Special Representative.]
London, Wednesday, 11.10 p.m.
Operations for appendicitis are becoming common
amongst society folk. The latest case is that of Sir
Charles Oust, equerry to the King, who accompanied
him on his Australian tour when Duke of York.
Appendicitis during recent years has become very
ordinary throughout the civilised world, and it is
held in many professional centres that before appen-
dicitis was correctly diagnosed numberless deaths
resulted, the physicians being at that time under the
impression the cause of death was peritonitis or in-
flammation of the bowels. Medical men, with the
educated finger-touch that comes with active prac-
tice, readily diagnose the complaint to-day, aided by
symptoms only too easily recognised.
Medical science of to-day has reduced this opera-
tion to an almost painless nature, but prolonged
weakness often ensues, as is shown in the case of Mr.
P. R. Miles, who was operated on in Melbourne last
year. In an appendicitis sense, his case was similar
to that of thousands of others, but the weakness that
ensued baffled all attempts to cure, and as he repre-
sented one of the largest commercial firms resident
in Australasia, his life was a very valuable one.
Prescriptions and medicines were alike useless to him
until he, as a last resource, took Clements Tonic,
the great nerve and brain medicine, and in a mirac-
ulously short time his strength and health were re-
stored. His nerves are now as sound as steel; he says
he is completely cured. As all chemists and stores
sell this medicine, it would be wise for all wevik-
nerved people to get it and get well.*

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 17, 1914.
My Dear Akini, —
There has been wrinkling of fore-
heads over the Mayoral hop in honor of the
British Blue Socks. This is timed —war
permitting—for August 24. The notion of
substituting a heavyweight form of enter-
tainment is in favor with the ’Varsity set
which is up to its eyebrows in Berlin wool —■
beg pardon, Mr. Censor, I meant knitting
yarn. As only 10 per cent, of the company
at these huge official crushes ever attempts
to dance under any circumstances, no one
need be afraid of too much frivolity on this
occasion. The spectacle of the right wing
of British Science being dragged into a
Lancers’ skirmish, and looking over its spec-
tacles for help from its absent-minded main
body, is likely to be the most frisky event of
the evening.
Professor Morgan, who hails from Dublin,
is going to shelter under the Tom Watsons’
roof at Double Bay, while he chats about
Science in this city. The Watsons are also
Irish by persuasion, and are descended from
King Nial of the Showers, or Nial of the
Hostages, or Flan of the Shannon, or some
other grand old warrior.
Mrs. W. B. Ma cart bur is putting up Pro-
fessor Wheeler at Kingsclerc, the huge, sky-
scraping flats that look like a brick gridiron
from our blue-water front.
Dr. W. M. Tattersall, of the learned,
spectacled brigade, will have every induce-
ment to cultivate a knowledge of Our
Harbor while he is in Sydney. He unpacks
his valuable ideas in the Christian house-
hold at Tudor, Elizabeth Bay. This beauti-
ful site, with terraced gardens falling into
the waters of the bay, which splash against
n stone rampart, used to be occupied by the
.T. C. Williamson menage. The view that
Tattersall’s eye will sweep—this is no allu-
sion to any Melbourne Cup sweep of the
same name- —suggests the blue and green
effects of an Italian lake after a spring
cleaning.
Staff-Surgeon Robinson, R.N., who mar-
ried pretty Grace Aclieson, of this city,
packed up his medical kit for the North
Sea a week or two ago. Mamma
Acheson gets a carefully-worded cable re
her only chick now and then. Just now
Grace is in the South of England.
Battle pictures in our National Gallery
are drawing the casual public like honey-
pots. The great canvas that shows the
famous charge of French Hussars at
Friedland, in 1807, was surrounded the
other afternoon bv mild city men and young
children, drinking in Detaille’s brush
work. Fighting, in the days when French
troops yelled “Vive l’Empereur,” was a
hand-to-hand affair, with such a dense cloud
of dust round a cavalry rush that one
wonders how many victims on eacli side
were hurriedly slaughtered by their own
friends before they gripped their enemies
by the throat.
Queensland is said to be searching the
British horizon for a spare Admiral as its
next Governor. There has been a whisper
of the nopular name of KingTlall —the best
naval barracker in the matter of an Aus-
tralian fleet this country has had, barring
Admiral Henderson.
The A. B. Piddingtons’ domicile in
Woollahra will roof Professor Jungerseu
and his wife during the invasion of Know-
ledge in the gross. Jungersen has a peace-
ful Danish sound, or possibly it is Swedish.
(No allusion to the Sound which Separates
these two countries.) Anyhow, it is war-
ranted not to go off suddenly if someone
sits on the Kaiser’s topknot for five
minutes.
Sydney’s well-known water-waggoner,
Archdeacon Boyce, .of St. Paul’s, Redfern,
who is going to and fro upon the earth seek-
ing health, accepted a chaplaincy under the
Colonial and Continental Church Society,
and was stationed at a place in Germany,
the name of which sounds remarkably like
Badlanguage-walbach fthe spelling being
Bad-Langenschwalbach). He expected to
remain until July 25. He was in Germany
on July 8, since when his family have had
no tidings. His Sydney congregation has
cabled to London in the hope of receiving
news re the text of his last week’s sermon.
The Archdeacon is 70 years of age, and has
been 46 years pursuing the horned Enemy
with the barbed tail.
Our tennis champion, Norman Brookes,
and his wife were week-ending under the
haughty Marlborousrh roof when a late mail
left London. Charles Richard, etc.,
Spencer-Churchill has various roofs, of
course—not including those humble ones
he recently sold “in person”—but the most
interesting ought to be Blenheim House,
stuffed with loot of the famous Churchill’s
swashbuckling career.
Lady Patey opened a sale of all sorts —
with a multitude of kitchen saucepans and
able-bodied pans in the rear —for the Ladies’
Home Mission Fund, the other afternoon. In
consequence of the address of this show hav-
ing; been changed from the Concordia Hall at
the last minute, the Chapter House, a dismal
kind of crypt off St. Andrew’s Cathedral, was
hung with doyleys and similar domestic
gauds. There was no air worth men-
tioning in this stone sarcophagus. So
the company panted round the stalls,
purple in the face, but firmly re-
solved to buy something it didn’t
want, or asphyxiate in the struggle. Mrs.
Brady’s pretty youngster, a vision in flaxen
curls and orchid mauve, passed the Admi-
ralty lady the usual floral tribute. His
Anglican Legs pronounced a blessing on the
lingerie present, and Mrs. Archbishop’s
commanding presence was in front of events.
Mrs. Baronet Cooper’s best blue feathers
tossed aloft in the buying arena, and the
whole gathering saved its individual lives
by dashing into the open air as soon as it
dared leave the doyleys by themselves. The
Ladies’ Home Mission aims at giving the
small clerical fry a scrape of butter, instead
of dripping, when times are hard.
Tenor Dufault inclined his ear to the
youthful warble of Madeline Collins and Ella
Goodman. He has issued the following ulti-
matum: The former girl is a fine dramatic
soprano, and the latter has a pretty chirp
suitable for light opera. The maidens will
now pursue their vocal gymnastics with
renewed energy.
Mrs. Mayoress Richards handed over
£290 to the Mater Misericordiae Hospital
authorities one glad day last week. This
prize money was the result of the July ball
in our Town Hay-loft, or Hollow Municipal
Void. The function was organised by
honorary organiser Mrs. Leahy, of Mosman.
She was generalissimo, and the way she led
her troops to victory suggests that she
should have inherited Huerta’s job when he
found it too big for him. There seems to
be no reason why the 1915 ball for this
haven for sick women and children shouldn’t
collect a £5OO cheque.
The Nurses’ Club ball committee still
hopes to pull its forthcoming revel, in aid
of a building fund, to a successful climax on
August 26. The Salon de Luxurious and
Romaine’s orchestra —which is It in dance
music—contribute to the gaiety of this hop.
Glad Rags, as displayed at various spring
shows, have been pushed into a retired
corner during the rush of politics. Almost
everybody has postponed the purchase of a
new blouse until hearing whether a Servian
general, with a name like chronic bronchitis,
has, or has not, shoved his army across a
river that sounds like a bird-cage in fits.
If these names were easier to pronounce
there might be more business doing,
but when a magnificent warrior named
I’zzkfptbszzrxxxxbj is fighting the good
fight in our cause on the banks of the river
L>jkh and everything- hangs in the balance
it is difficult to fix our thoughts on a yard
or two of ninon. Still, here goes! Bustles
predominate as the last whoops of Fashion.
The busy housewife who wears a camel’s
hump behind might use it to stow away
provisions on the march to the drawing-
room front. Black and white, a safe blend
in these uncertain days, rejuvenates the
shiny black glace of long ago. Hips are
built up under formidable outworks of puffs,
and you have to be Dolly Yarden below the
waist, and fish-tail round your heels.
Colors are like the tints in tropic orchids.
Hats are pancakes folded down, or spiky
towers that toss their plumes like the tail
of Chanticleer in a head wind.
Dorothy Holmes, daughter of Mr. Holmes
of the Water and Sewerage Board, was mar-
ried the other day to Reginald Travers.
Holmes, senior, is Colonel in a local
militia regiment, which is gnashing its
sword to be up and at it. Grandpa
Holmes was an old Imperial fight-
ing man, latterly chief clerk to the N.S.W.
khaki forces, when Dawes Point battery
was in working order and a matter of inter-
est to adjacent nursemaids.
Sydney Grammar School boys have shown
their common sense by saving the cash usu-
ally spent in trophies at their yearly sports.
Victors at these 1914 local Olympian games
may be crowned with anything handy—a few
sprigs of parsley or a cauliflower leaf—and
will go away with a certificate instead of a
silver mug. Half a hundred pounds—the
saving mentioned —has been hurried off to
Mayor Richards’s patriotic nest-egg.
The Cedric Bowkers, who belong to this
city of blue waters and blue skies and
islands and old traditions, are in London
just now, under grey skies and beside
muddy waters. After skimming over Eng-
lish roads in a swift petrol chariot, they
retired in good order to the arena of Russell
square.
Professor Benjamin Moore, who has been
dealing out physiology to Melbourne in the
gathering of Blue Socks, is going to camp
with Dr. H. G. Chapman—a local pillar of
the welcome committee —during his stay in
Sydney.
The October Dolls’ Carnival m aid of the
Alexandra Hospital has had all the sawdust
knocked out of it. Young men who were
going- to prance round as elephants, kanga
roos and peacocks in the Noahs Ark se
now absorbed in the Weltpolitik. (I think
that is how it is spelled, and 1 don t care it
it isn’t.) One of them who was going to he
a light and airy kangaroo stopped me yester-
day to ask my opinion about getting up an
expedition to nrevent the Arabs seizing
Siberia. The Dolls’ frivol is packed up till
further notice.
Dr. Emma Buckley, a graduate of Syd-
ney Uni., puts aside her stethoscope every
Monday evening to lecture on lurst Aid at
the ambulance classes under t,lie Girls Realm
wing in Vickery’s Chambers. Talks on the
right way to bandage continue till JU per
cent, of our girls begin to tie up the furni-
ture 011 sight. Even piano legs are not sate
in this rush for knowledge. A juvenile
enthusiast who smashed her parents best
gamp, the other day, so that she could ban-
dage its broken ribs, is not held up as a
domestic example of Force applied to the
best purpose.
Barrister Hanbury Davies got a firm giip
of a local Red Cross meeting which was
beginning to fall over itself in avoirdupois
weight last week. Miss Gould, one of our
star nightingales —with South African ex-
perienee—brushed aside a few cobwebs irorn
the lorgnettes of those present. Dr. Stone
Dixson, who preaches First Aid all the year
round, also handed the company some valu-
able advice.
The Gentleman Help service has been
hastily reorganised, since so many recent
importations have dashed off. Lord I reddie
Blackwood, Captain Anstruther and little
Help Henry Talbot, a loo’tenant in the 11th
Hussars, also A.D.C. Gale (on the Vic-
torian Governor’s staff) are now bumping
about in the Egypt after a frantic rush to
stuff their gorgeous uniforms into cabin-
trunks and put their hair-oil in the hold.
Lady Freddie went off from Sydney with
the Egypt contingent, and hopes to wait in
England while her tall soldier waves his
sword on the battlements of Jerusalem.
About the most dashing-looking aide who
fled from Sydney was Captain Hopwood, of
the South Australian sub-regal humpy.
Ilopwood, whose male parent is Under Sec-
retary of State for the Colonies, amongst
many other things, departed in the Will*
ochra.
The N.S.W. Patriotic Fund, which wants
a violent shaking up to bring it into line
with Victoria’s big financial whack, is going
to have an immediate prod from various
local mummers. The Little Theatre, bustles
into action with a matinee before this meets
your ruminating glance. Hugh Ward is acting
commander-in-chief of another huge matinee
for the Fund. It is bringing such a rush
of stage volunteers who want to do their
bit, that the J.C.W. tetrarch will arrange
the forthcoming campaign with a wet towel
firmly affixed to his massive brow.
Librarian Hugh Wright, of our Mitchell
Library, is gathering musty MSS. at the
present moment. The Sydney man left Lon-
don in July for France and Spain, to peer
into moth-eaten records of early Austral-
asian story. There are said to be many
odds and ends of our ancient narrative to
be found in Spain, for the old dons were
wonderful navigators, and it isn’t quite cer-
tain even yet that they didn’t land on the
Queensland coast before the Dutch and
British and Portuguese were around, and
say mass in the neighborhood of Mary-
borough.
Dr. and Mrs. Chenhall, of this township,
with small son Freddie in tow, mean to do
some more European sights before rushing
up the gangway of an Australian-bound
craft at old picturesque Genoa. The date
of their return is uncertain.
“Yesterday,” writes Kate Rooney from
London, “the newest thing from Sydney in
baby sopranos came to sing to me. I mean
lima Elliot. Her voice is deliciously pure
and sweet. Very wisely she is going first
to Italy to complete her studies... .When
she returns to London engagements will be
waiting for her.... 1 think this is a big-
feather in the cap of my dear old teacher,
Mrs. Christian, who has given lima her
entire training.” Concerning herself. Kate
writes in high spirits after a strenuous 12
months in America. Part of the Sydney
contralto’s English holiday is laid out in
studying the leading contralto roles in
“Aida,” “Trovatore” etc., for a late autumn
tour in Robyn’s Opera Co.
Mrs. Gleeson is bustling round a concert
in St. James’s Hall, to help the Sydney Day
Nursery funds. This event will march on
to a suburban victory, for the kiddies’
nursery at Forest Lodge, on August 28.
Our First Australian Infantry celebrated
its Diamond Jubilee with the loud popping
of chamnagne corks at the Australia. A
guard of honor that flashed cold steel under
the glittering arc-lights in Castlereagh-
street flung out its chest and tramped for-
ward to meet Governor Strickland. For
10 minutes or so this comparatively peace-
ful city street—where the roaring tram-
cars roll onward to the quay —was full of
warlike feathers and bayonets.
Professor Pope, a big British gun >r
howitzer in chemistry, who was handed a
Doctor of Science degree in Melbourne, will
wear his new frill at the Medical Lightoller
lireside, while he unfurls his umbrella in
this settlement.
The Austral String Quartet, with fiddler
Cyril Monk in command, recently gave, as a
new offering to the hungry maw of local
music fiends, a quintet for piano and strings
by Fritz Volbach.
The Kindergarten Union put on its best
bonnets to sally forth to a meeting at State
Government House on a recent fine after-
noon. The Strickland ladyship presided.
General-secretary Ellen Desailly, in her
spring millinery; Mrs. Francis Anderson
(one of the founders of the Free Kiddygarten
schools here) ; Miss Harriet Duinolo, the
youthful principal of the training college for
teachers of the cult; and Mrs. Dane orated
round the long list of things done, and
money spent, in the cause. Pastoralist
John Mackay, president of the Cult of the
Unsmacked Child, and a bulwark of the
Free Kindergarten schools--where babes are
taught nice manners and (he correct use of
hankies —made a few non-combative remarks.
The big American, Mr. Boorman, an old
friend of the Cause, rounded up the speeches
with the hope of good times to come. The
brand-new A.D.C., Wakeman looked as
cheerful as circumstances permitted. Before
stamping down the red gravel path to its
blustering trams, those present fell upon
the sub-regal refreshments with great
slaughter. The bun had no more chance
than the Egyptians in the Red Sea.
The final Dufault warble in the Town
Hall repeated the success of the tenor’s
previous concerts here. Encore fiends,
ravenous for more —some day these Oliver
Twists may be tamed down to more reason-
able demands —kept Paul’s evening slippers
on the trot from the artist’s den under the
growling organ to the municipal dais.
Legions of damsels in the galleries waved
a frantic farewell, after the tenor had con-
ducted the National Anthem to a full stop.
Premier Holman and his wife wandered
in to hear Dufault say good-bye to Sydney.
The N.S.W. politician looked a very weary
man, with the coal dust of the journey from
Melbourne hastily brushed off his top-coat
and the cinders hardly removed from his
back hair.
Much sympathy is offered to the well-likec
literary man. Dowell O’Reilly, whose wife —
formerly a Miss Piddington of this city—
passed over the shadowy border the other day.
Dowell, by the way, is only literary and
poetic in his spare time. He usually drives
his work-a-day quill in the Civil Service.
His booklet. “Tears and Triumph,” which
places Woman in the animal kingdom as a
useful maternal beast of somewhat limited
intelligence—except in the cow-like finality
of carefully tending her young—is a queer,
clever outbreak against modern feminism.
A well-known Australian journalist, who
put in 15 years solid work on the London
press, was not impressed with the type of
male pussy who writes Society notes on one
or two papers in John Bull’s antheap. “They
have been known for years as ‘mealers,’ and
frankly use their position to fare sumptu-
ously—-on the nod.” They are noted “walk-
ing stomachs,” and their impartial (?)
news is regarded by the people who feed
them as the direct reward for services ren-
dered. Women are not lured by food in the
same way. The average presswoman, if she
has a thin skin, holds aloof from the Society
crowd which is willing to pay its parasites
in kind —expensive food, seats in its innumer-
able cars (when no one more important is
about) and other social perquisites. The
returned Australian opines that the few male
lackeys on the press count for little against
the herd of women scribes who munch their
humble lodging-house hash seven days a
week.
Paling’s people publish a new song with
the title, “Good-bye, Dear Heart.” The
words are by Henry de Witt. The melody,
by Percy Dixon, flows softly round the
crooning verse. The title is absolutely
original. No one previously struck the idea
of “good-bye” as a song theme.
Our ex-G.-G. Denman recently told Lon-
doners that they might do more for visiting
Australians than was their wont. The pre-
sent visit of the British spectacles bears out
the Denman argument, that the Common-
wealth exudes hospitality to the strangers
within its gates.
That oddment in dukes. His Grace of
Manchester, who may be described as an
outsize in peers, has now added to his
accomplishments the r6le of “exploration
and geographical secretary” to a moving-
picture corporation. This body proposes. to
elevate the plain citizen by supplying
churches and institutions with “educational”
films. Possibly the average cleric isn’t look-
ing for more Light in his parish from that
will-o’-the-wisp of Manchester.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 17, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie,—
A young Australian doctor, Judah Jona,
has lately tacked up his brass-plate in the
practitioners’ paradise of Malvern, where
patients are both prosperous and numerous
enough to provide for those who help them
to die comfortably. Dr. .Tona commenced
collecting degrees in Adelaide. Continuing
the hobby in Europe he happened to he in
the Balkans when trouble arose there, and
kept on arising. Offers were made him by
two of the a risers; but Jona thought of
another Land of Promise, and pleaded an
Australian engagement. Asa private citi-
zen. however, young Jona saw a good deal
of the gory doings which ensued, and finds
Malvern quite a ladylike suburb after the
blood-and-tliunder happenings of the corpse-
strewn regions around Boastchook. Dr.
.Tona recently married into the family of
Austral Hat-Miller Kozminsky.
Sir Edward Schafer and his wife were
the guests of honor on Wednesday _ after-
noon last week, when Dr. Jona and his wife
received their friends at the Oriental. The
.Tona medico, of Malvern, struck up a. friend-
ship with the Schafer knight in England last
year, when lie was scientifically researching
at the Lyster Institute. In fact, Ihe
Schafers had arranged to stay with their
Australian friends ere the entertainment
committee insisted on taking them in charge.
The gathering at the Oriental Hotel was a
great success. Imported scientists rubbed
shoulders with local doctors and University
profs., and Mrs. Jona, as hostess, had the
afternoon <>f her life.
Lord Frederick Blackwood has gone
away, which is rough on poor little Lady
Frederick, hardly convalescent after her
operation. lie fought in South Africa and
was shot so hard or so often, or something,
that his distinguished Marquis parent rushed
to his bedside to say farewell. However,
nothing very dreadful is likely to happen to
him this time, as he has merely to lie low
and defend England.
Victor 1 food, who has stood in the social
firing-line with so many Governors, is also
off to the front —1 say front advisedly, since
in this war Ihe whole of Europe appears to
be the front, and the only rear that can be
railed a rear is Snitzbergen. My old friend
Humor avers that he intends to ask Kitch-
ener for a job. lie met Kitchener
when he was Private-Secretary to some State
Governor or other, and ex-vice-regal helps
arc well known to be useful War Office
officials.
And, finally, the vast and splendid
Anstruther aide is off as well. He was not
only an aide, but an eligible—a rare com-
bination. The local girls who frequent Gov-
ernment House are desolate.
The Antarctic Mawsons are on their way
to Melbourne. He proposes to lecture there
on the icy district where the chilblains come
from.
Captain Davis, by the way, is being
housed by the Registrar at the University.
For'all his experiences in Antarctica he says
he was never so cold in his life as
on the trip from Adelaide in the wee sraa’
hours of the morning. ITe is very tall, with
a Viking brand of bright gold hair and
beard.
One of the visiting scientists is Roseufeld,
some time of Melbourne, and an authority
on metals. He was chosen months ago to
speak in the Town llall. Melbourne, on
Saturday, August 15, on the “Making of a
Big Gun.” If that lecture isn’t the most
popular of the series it won’t be for lack of
a huge, free advt.
Captain I.andale, who married Miss Vera
Clarke, is rejoining the cavalry regiment
which he left half a dozen years ago. His
sword has been hammered into a shearing-
machine for some time now, but it is being
straightened out, and is expected to look as
good as new when finished. Not so his uni-
form. The sheep industry is one that in-
duces Mesh in many of its votaries, and the
tall, fair pastoralist will never take the
same size in waists again. Also he will need
a stout charger to carry him. When he
went through the Boer war a mere pony
would have sufficed.
Mr. Richmond, who had been soldiering
with the Black Watch before he returned to
the family sheep-run (Haddon Rig. N.SAV.).
is also going back in quest of trouble.
The cause of the hanging fire of the great
battle in Belgium was the fact that the
British force was waiting for Mr. Rich-
mond’s arrival. But if the Germans meanly
attack before he reaches the scene there is
another Richmond son on the spot, likewise
a son-in-law called Gordon Finlayson.
An interesting military wedding of the
hurried order was that of Miss Dolly Clegg
and young Harold Hall, who lately added
many Walter Hall thousands to what his
late parent had already put to his credit.
A couple of army corps of unnatu-
lalised Germans were grabbed one n’ght
last week by the police (mostly the
quarry was run to earth at the turn
Yerein) and requested either to take
i heir oaths that they would not play the
Commonwealth false in any way. or submit
to being- interned during the progress of the
war —“interned” being the international
periphrasis for gaoled or qitodded. *' uum-
her of them decided incontinently to become
British. One who dresses ladies’ hair in a
Coll ins-street shop was locked up for hours
without a drop of lager, while SassicU
women sat furiously waiting for switches,
fronts, bangs and so on. They were just
meditating a reconnaisance in force, with
the idea of retrieving him. when he arrived
parched, and in floods of tears, owing to the
loss of his nationality. It appears the
authorities naturalised him during the night,
lie has not been the same since. . e , 5 -
minds me of Samson after he had liis hair
cut. of Ernest Terah llooley after he tiled
his schedule, and tragic ruins like that.
I was pained in the tenderest part of
my Rule Britannia feelings the other night,
at Spencer’s pictures, to see Queen Mary in
the act of putting up an umbrella. Queens
and gamps don’t go together, somehow.
Many of our own social monarchs, scions
of 50-year-ohl importing houses and 40-year-
old sheep families, beast that they do not
own so much as one personal brolly. Vi hen
it rains they either make the chauffeur shield
them from the elements with his. or they
borrow the gamp of a friend. A passion for the
umbrella appears, by the wav. to he pretty
general in the Royal Family. Princess Mary
is usually depicted carrying one of tlic same
brand as her mother uses, and wielding it.
moreover, in the same resolute and uprigoi
manner. The Medicis loved rubies —the
Guelplis umbrellas, which latter are both
cheaper and more respectable.
May Vale Gilfillan had a, goodly crowd to
see the pictures in her Collins-street studio
last week. Most of May’s sitters appear
to be men. Doctor Alexander Dan-nay was
one of them, and State-Secretary Callaway
—the man who is so interested in gaols—
made another forceful portrait.
The week lias been divided between Science
and War. If by this time I do not know it
all. from the Genesis of Diprodotoiis to the
Campaigns of Alexander, it is not, believe me,
for lack of verbal hints. First in import-
ance, among the gatherings given to discuss
the burning topics of the hour, in the inter-
vals of eating, was the G.-G.’s immense party
on Thursday night. About 2000 people surged
into the ballroom, and another thousand
crowded the drawing-room and picture-
gallery. It was democratic, but not
altogether pleasant. The traffic confu-
sion at the outer gates was awful.
Motor-cars went mad, and cabbies be-
came insane- in the square arranged
for them. Generals in the police force yelled
and danced in their efforts to prevent humble
people who had arrived in trams from being
run over and put out of social action. Arrived
in the ballroom, everyone who was anyone
was promptly lost. In this respect it was
really more like the infernal regions than a
respectable Government House. There were
exceptions, of course. For example, Sir
Oliver Lodge, who is too large and conspicu-
ous to he mislaid anywhere. He stood head
and shoulders above the crowd, and his long
hair obscured the back of his neck like a
silver cascade. However, Sir Edward and
Lady Schiifer were lost almost at once by
Mrs'. Burston, and her cries of despair on the
subject would have moved to pity the heart
of a Uhlan—-one of the sort that commits the
newspaper atrocities when nothing more
authentic is doing. Also, Mrs. Geoffrey
S.vme —conspicuous though she was in an
adorable dress of blue and silver brocade-
got completely off the scent of the Luptons.
One of the A.D.C.’s and I were just hurry-
ing off to the stable to requisition the ser-
vices of the household bloodhound, when a
view-lialloa from the neighborhood of the dais
intimated to us that the quarry had been
run to earth. It was, indeed, a stirring
evening—though, as I said before, somewhat
congested. What it would have been had all
the invited turned up, I tremble to think.
As it was, many of them stayed at home for
various reasons. Mrs. Review of Reviews
Stead had some overseas geniuses staying
with her who lost every fragment of their
luggage. Diana, I fancy it was. who killed
Actseon for seeing her undress. In a similar
spirit Mrs. Stead’s guests announced that
they would see Sir Ronald shot before he
viewed them in undress. Most of the guests
were anxious to see the famous Ferrrr-
gusssson pictures, and the ones that didn’t
care two straws for the pictures felt called
on to. pretend that they did. and so there was
a tremendous rush on the gallery. I got a
splendid view of back-hair, osprey, jewelled
comb and bald head. Also, I saw sonic lovely
tapestries on the walls and a number of
electric lights, which were designed to illumi-
nate the canvases. Then I decided that Life
was sweeter than Art, and got out. It would
he a sound move, in my opinion, to put a
placard at the entrance to the gallery on
future occasions, inscrib’d “No Food Here.”
lending a helping hand to Art -the Art oh War. The
proceeds of lTeinnecke’s concert are to go to the War
Fund, and, a branch of the. Red Cross Society has
been formed. Madame Del mar Hull is arranging for
a big patriotic concert on August 29, and as bands
and orchestras and singers are giving their sendees
free, there should be money in it.
A big University audience did itself honor when it
greeted with specially .warm applause such of the
visiting scientists as were Austrian or German—two
of them are members of the Kaiser's Privy Council.
The crowd simply remembered that these men, its
invited guests, were in a dashed awkward situa-
tion, and they were helped through it generously ; m
return for which the “alien enemies’’ listened to a
lot of “God Save the King” (of England) with
(apparently) profound respect.

From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Science Congress has come and gone. The
opening engagement of the campaign was at the
Town Hall on Saturday evening, when the delegates
encountered the local Ministry, Both sides com-
ported themselves with courage, though tiie speechify-
ing—which included addresses by the Governor, the
Premier and Sir Oliver Lodge—was deadly in its
precision. Amongst those in the forefront of +he
firing-line were Lady Galway, in gold and blue
brocade; Lady Lodge, in ivory brocade; and Lady-
Butler in a serviceable field dress of grey, with
cloudy drapings of pink ninon. On Monday a
special armored train took the visitors to Aligns on,
where the commissariat arrangements were in the
hands of the Charlie VngaseS. and later on they
motored to the Lindsay Park garden. Meanwhile
scouting expeditions were being flung out in the
direction of the Hills and Milang. Also there was
a two days’ geological foray for some of the party.
On Tuesday Mount Lofty and Mount Pleasant were
carried by assault, the invaders returning to Adelaide
the same day, in time for the Mayor’s reception in
the evening. And on Wednesday a hurried mobilisa-
tion occurred and tiie entire force was transported
to Melbourne. It was brisk work and a credit to
all concerned.
The Mayoral reception was in the Exhibition Build-
ing, transformed for the occasion into an Egyptian
Hall. The supports of the gallery were clothed in
cardboard stencilled with Egyptian designs ; and hang-
ing hessian, also stencilled, hid as much of the walls
and the ordinary paint and gilding as possible. And
then there were electric lights and garlands of
oranges and bunt lies of grapes and a pond fringed with
water lilies, and many other things that one is accus-
tomed to find in the typical Egyptian flail. It only
needed a few unclothed savages howling for back-
sheesh, and about a ton of dust and some (leas and
four or five camels to make it absolutely perfect.
With the Government House party were Major Connor
and Private Secretary Mulholland : Captain Hopwood
having left in nucst of Teuton gore. Moss Cheek,
Mr. Walter W»od, -Mr. Cooper and Mr. Robyns pro-
vided the musical programme, with Mr. Prims as
accompanist; after which there was supper in the
western annex. Everyone was carefully dressed
.eeerdii.g to their lights; so 1 won’t invidiously pick
any me out for special mention, and about 3000 pev-
- ns who have not been definitely discovered in any
really serious offence participated in- the proceedings.
When not assisting Science we hate mostly been
Yours sincerely.
JOHNNA.

work with some beef tied on to it.

“^Esculapius”: In ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular dietv. We moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water —only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to the
Earl of Ilopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
“Figaro”: Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
but close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Hclidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of “Helidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, “Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom is always to
SPLIT IT WITH
HELIDON.

“De Oriel”: Had a quaint experience in
the sleepy little town of Wentworth (N.S.W.). Was
in'advance of a company (Howard Bros.). Made a
big show of paper on the old walls and fences that
night. Woke next morning and was amazed to find
that all vestige of my evening’s exertion Had
disappeared. Goats are legion there. I solved the
problem by adding bluestone to the boiled flour. It
killed a few goats but kept the town well and truly
billed.

“Billy T.” : They were discussing the
accident to poor Jock Mackintosh, who had just been

“Rufus”: Re the yarn (B. 16/7/T4)
regarding Binglii’s habit of mummifying his defunct
relations. When Dr. Mjoberg, the Swedish scientist,
was in North Queensland last year, he was very
anxious to secure a mummy, but, so far as I can
learn, his efforts were unsuccessful. On one occasion
I accompanied him to a camp where it was known
there was a corpse in process of curing, but we were
not permitted a look inside the preserving room.
The doctor tried to bribe a member of the tribe to
secure the departed, but the said departed's dele-
gates objected. A few days later I happened on a
camp some miles away, and walked unobserved to the
entrance hole of the largest gunyah. I just had a
glimpse of a swathed form suspended over a smoky
fire when I was roughly pushed aside by an excited
buck, and narrowly escaped a crack on the head.
The local tribes say that if a black dies away from
his country, i.e., his birthplace, the body is dried
and carried about by the tribes-folk in their wander-
ings till they arrive at the birthplace. Then the
body is secured in the fork of a big tree, but with-
out its internal machinery, same having been
removed immediately after death. There is no hurry
about the funeral, and the gone-before may be
humped for months before he is finally treed. As
far as I know, the gone-before have never objected to
this.

“Gouger”: A few days ago the Empress of
China took from Cooktown 97 tons of sandal-
wood, worth well over £3OOO. A good deal of
the wood came from the Peninsula and the back
country around Cooktown. Most of it was brought in
during these last two months. The Chinese are
almost the sole buyers of the wood, which they ship
to China. When polished, the Chinese turn it into
beautiful furniture, also into glove and hanky and
jewel cases for the expensive white missy. Yellow
storekeepers at Cooktown are now paying £45 per
ton for the timber, an enormous rise on prices they
have been giving for years. All through the Penin-
sula the miners are dropping the pick and the dyna-
mite and the wild cat, and taking the axe after the
more profitable sandalwood. Teams of every descrip-
tion, from the one-horse waggonette to the big-string
waggons, from the mob of carrying niggers to the
300-horse packs, are scouring the Peninsula
in search of the dirty-looking article for which John
Chinaman is giving his good gold.
“Y.Z.” : It was about 12 o’clock at night
when we found him lying on the Brighton electric
tram track. He was about 30 pints in, and all out.
Henry shook him up. “Le’ me Tone,” he growled.
“You must get out of this, old chap,” said Henry
kindly; “there’s a tram due. You’ll be killed.”
“Don’ be damfool; le’ me Tone. I’m all ri’,” said
the drunk. “But you’re not all right. You are on
the tram rails, and there’s a tram coming.” “Rot!”
snorted the inebriate. “I’m not on tram rails. I’m
perfecly all ri’ ’n’ comfo’ble—l’m in the gutter!”
Argument was useless. lie insisted he was safely,
perfec’ly all ri’ ’n’ comfo’ble—in the gutter!
So we took him by the left leg, and dragged him to
the side of the road, lie was very indignant. lie
expressed the righteous contempt he felt for our
outrageous interference with his liberty, then pawed
round till he found the gutter, and rolling into it
settled down once more while the stream purled
round him. “There,” he said. “Now I’m qui’ safe.
Man’s alwiz qui’ safe in gutter. Wha’ else is gutter
for?” He raised his head and his voice to be more
impressive. “Take away man’s gutter again,” he
said, “sue you for trespass.” So we left him.
The Maoriland system of hooking a dozen
farmhouses on to one telephone wire is a doubtful
sort of blessing. If one subscriber
rings up anyone the whole of the
other subscribers can walk to their
instruments and listen to the conver-
sation if they are inquisitively
inclined. Usually they are. If the
vife rings up the nearest store for
a new hat all the other cats know
it; and if the boss rings up the pub
for a two-gallon keg everybody calls
and helps to drink it. On one com-
munistic line there has been a lot
of trouble lately. A frantic- indi-
vidual has quarrelled with all the
other subscribers, and whenever one
subscriber rings up another he
dashes to his ’phone and delivers
such an assortment of cat-calls, dog
barks and monkey language into
the trembling wire that no one can
use the thing. He seems to sit up
beside the instrument all night, for
no matter when an attempt is made
to use the service the menagerie
breaks loose. Just at present the circuit
is down and out> bl]t the officials are saJd to
be going for the frenzied party with a summons.
f' or an individual like that a summons is not nearly

“T. 0.1.” : I’ve come on a new explanation
of the lately-discussed phrase, “Mad as a hatter.”
“Phrases and Names, their Origins and Meanings”
by T. 11. Johnson, 1906, has this: “ ‘Mad as a hatter.’
a corruption of ‘Mad as an after.’ ‘After’ expressed
the Saxon for viper or adder. The word ‘mad’ was
anciently used in the sense of venomous; hence this
expression really meant ‘Venomous as a Viper.’ ”

“Nedboc”: Re John Reid’s (B. 80/7/T4)
remark that “Australia has no organised [military]
transport of any kind.” What about this suggestion,
thrown out by the farmers’ official educator in
Bananaland ? ,
Couldn’t the Defence Department do something in
the way of subsidising a motor-truck or waggon?
The War Office does so in England. Indeed, if a
man spends £6OO in buying a motor waggon that
comes up to the specifications of the War Office, he
gets a grant of £llO towards the cost. Why shouldn’t
our Defence Department do something similar? Our
transport corps is notoriously dependent on the
farmers for vehicles. Why not help the farmer to
provide the best possihl. vehicle?
It can’t be done this week, or next, or the week
after; but it seems to me there’s something in the
idea. It would have to be stipulated, of course, that
the vehicle didn’t habitually honk in • the remote
places beyond Birdsville. If it was there it probably
wouldn’t know it was wanted until it was too late
to matter, anyhow.

“Lagani-Namo”: The most convincing
warning to trespassers that I’ve happened on to was
at a trading station in the New Hebrides. On each
of the four gate-posts to the compound was a human
skull and thigh bones—the device so popular with
the buccaneers of old. Over these grim ornaments
was written: “Any dogs, pigs or niggers found in-
side this fence will be shot. By order, Jut,ns.” One
day some strange boys ignored the warning and were
promptly given a passage into the Great Beyond.
Jules quietly explained to their angry relatives how
the departed had disregarded his taboo marks; and
the relatives admitted that the punishment was
deserved. Then they bade him a friendly farewell and
scattered for home. At break-o’-day, a week later,
they called on Jules again, murdered him in his
sleep, cooked him with wood from his own fence,
digested him, and finally tied his bones alongside
those at the entrance-way. Incidentally the sign of
the skull and crossbones is used by the Common-
wealth Government at Pennant Hills, N.S.W., above
the warning to the public to keep clear of the wireless
station in certain weather conditions. Some day an
infuriated citizen will call there and eat Joseph Cook.

“Boronia”: How far inland has the boom
of a. rough sea raging along the coast been heard?
On clear, calm nights when all the atmospheric con-
ditions were favorable I have heard the hammering
of billows on Cape Leeuwin, a distance of 50 miles.
Let Baron Munchausen or Dictator Joseph Cook stand
forth. [The reference to brothers Munchausen and
Cook is by me, Hermann von Plugge, a patron of
the arts and other liquids.]
“Moana”: It isn’t all Garbage and Nat
Gould in the backblox. Went into a little shanty
in Maoriland miles from .anywhere the other day, and
in 'a rough bookcase in one corner found a small
collection of books that made me stare. Here is the
little list: Keats, Omar, “King Lear,” Green’s “Short
History,” Carlyle’s “French Revolution.” “Marcus
Aurelius,” “The Treasure of the Humble” (Maeter-
linck), “Candida” (Shaw), “Captain Brassbound’s
Conversion” (Shaw), “Life of Gladstone” (Paul),
“Life of Parnell” (O’Brien), Scott’s “Voyage of The
Discovery” (2 vols), “Scott’s Last Expedition”
(2 vols.), “Marie Claire,” “The Contagion of Charac-
ter” (Hillis), “Personal Power” (Thomas), “From
the Cape to Cairo,” four small books by Arnold
Bennett (“How to Live on 24 Hours a Day,” “The
Human Machine,” Literary Taste” and “Mental Effici-
ency”), and one novel, “The Way of All Flesh,” by
Samuel Butler. Not at all a bad little collection of
reading matter to be left alone with. Of course, the
man that owned them was an exceptional sort of
bloke, as blokes go, but still he was a bloke.

A WAR DIRGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Three cheers for the Glorious Game of War,
So ripe with sorrow and red with gore,
And pretty with bright shivoo.
The sides are picked, and the pipers play,
The crowds are crying Hip-hip-hooray,
And the teams look trim as they march away,
Their mission to die or do ;
Whilst many an eye that sees them go
Is shedding a tear of silent woe,
With life to lose, and naught to win
The “Tommy’s” lucky to save his skin
In the Glorious Game. Hooroo!
The umpire’s name it is Gruesome Death:
He blows his whistle with all his breath,
And barraekers get their fill
Of the “sport” the players must now provide:
For the Lord of Hosts (on the stronger side)
In the sacred cause of a Nation’s Pride
Is working His holy will.
The game goes on till call of Time,
When Peace is made and joybells chime.
The side that won, the side that lost,
Must mourn their dead and count the cost —
And Poverty pays the bill.
So wear your colors and spread your chest,
For the play is hot and the match a Test —
Oh ! a glorious game is War.
On land and sea men fight and die,
And bombshells drop from the frowning sky
Like thunder-bolts from the Lord on High
Whom emperors all adore.
The players of every race and creed
Are true-born boys of the Jingo breed,
And a citizen barracker’s chief delight
Is to wave a flag on a noisy night—
The Lord only knows what for.
Australian blood, on the word of Cook.
Shall flow as free as a babbling brook
Ere Britain is brought to shame.
Joe blinks the truth though he speaks aright,
Whilst Britain is fighting a far-off fight
We’ll hold our own, with a grip that’s tight,
In the Empire’s ancient name.
Let Britain’s strength be a British boast:
This land of ours has a wider coast;
With never a man or a ship to spare.
On behalf of our kinsmen over there,
We sing “Save the King” as we now prepare
To play a defensive game.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

OVER THE CORDUROY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are some who travel on well-made roads
And sit in a motor-car,
AVith rugs and cushions to keep them warm
As they spin to a town afar,
And some pay well for the rides they take,
While others steal rides of joy ;
But we of the bushlands jar and jolt
Over the corduroy ;
AA r e thump and jump with many a bump
Over the corduroy.
There are some who drive their mettlesome steeds
And sit in their landaus smart;
But we have only a tired old nag
And ride in a creaking cart
Along the gullies and round the hills,
While the swarming flies annoy;
It's a weary trip for man and nag
Over the corduroy,
And it’s oh for the rack of an aching back,
Over the corduroy.
There are some who stick to the railway tracks,
A “first return” they buy,
AVhile others go up in an aeroplane,
And wing their way to the sky ;
But we who live in the bush afar
Must humbler means employ,
So we bump along in a worn old cart,
Over the corduroy,
With a jar and a jolt that loosens each bolt,
Over the corduroy;
AVith a jump and a thump and a bump, bump, bump,
Over the corduroy.
TASMANIA.
O’PIIIMERTY.
“Y.Z.”: I had been invited to spend a
pleasant Sunday evening at Flohm’s. The Cohens
were there, and the Blooms, and the Tleinsteins;
also Mr. and Mrs. Yaeobs, and four persons named
Moses. 1 think they were Jewish, too. We played
cards mostly, but my interest was centred in old
Mr. Flohm, the father of our host, a patriarchal
gent who looked like Moses out of his bed quilt, and
who had a nose that snuggled down in his whiskers
like a bird in its nest. Flohm, senior, did not play
cards. He had a piece of pencil, and employed him-
self making marks on anything and everything. When
Flohm took the paper from him he made marks on
Bloom’s collar. Every now and again Mrs. Flohm
would whisper: “Issatore, dere is fadder ad id again.
Sdop him, vill you ?” Flohm would stop his ancient
sire ; but presently the old man was as busy as ever,
making marks on what cards he could sneak, on the
wallpaper, on the woodwork—anywhere. - I investi-
gated later. The mark Flohm the elder was making
was this “£.” I found the walls literally covered
with it to a height of seven feet. Flohm’s. outhouses,
his asphalt and his back fences are thickly orna-
mented with the magic sign. “Vot, aind’t de poor
olt shendleman right in hiss mindt ?” asked Bloom.
“Righd in hiss mindt?” squealed Issadore indignantly.
“Of gourse, he’s righd in hiss mindt. Can’d der
fadder haff some innocend amusemends? Vot he
tinks is dot he is sdill making money hand-oafer-fist.
Righd in hiss mindt! Righd in hiss mindt! Veil, I
neffer see such tarn impudence!”
THE TARIFF RAIL-SITTER—
IN TOPHET.
“Moana” : Although the fact is not gener-
ally known, the Maoriland tui, or parson bird, is
one of the best talkers in feathers on this planet.
The average parrot is an also-started alongside of
him. Its wowser name was given it because it
is quite black save for a white plume underneath the
throat that sticks out and looks like a parson’s little
white tie. I know of a tui that an Auckland man
had, which learnt lengthy sentences and picked up
many scraps of stray talk not intended for its ears.
Its enunciation was remarkably clear, and it invari-
ably used the exact tone of voice employed by the
person whose words it was repeating. But it never
learned to write and its cheque was no good at the
Bank of Ohinemutu.

DOBBS: “Far from it, dear boy , far from it. I seem to meet the brutes everywhere .
“0.H.5.”: Arrived at Port Moresby last
Saturday, and one of the first objects that met my
eye was a white nigger. In formation his features
are exactly the same as any other native, but his
skin is quite white and his hair quite fair. The
oldest inhabitant told me that he (the bleached nig)
was born of parents as black as the outlook in
Europe, and was simply a freak of Nature.

“F.H.R.”: Was asked by an old battler
t’other day if I knew of any work. I told him of a
bit of fencing on the tops that should suit him ; good
sinking and soft, and plenty of timber handy. “Any
bees-nests he asked. I told him that it was rather
cold for bees up there. “No good ter me,” he says.
“Why, what’s a man going to eat with his damper?”
'these old battlers never dream of buying butter or
jam.
* * * *-
“Wyangarie” : Surely it is about lime that
our Forestry Department began to take reafforestation
seriously. At present it’s only policy seems to be
the collecting of royalty. Writer knows one forest
reserve, no great exception either, on the North Coast
(N.S.W.). three miles from a deep-sea port, with
hardly a suitable tree for milling purposes on it.
If the reserves of this description were felled and
burnt, thousands of seedlings would take the place
of the present useless and ant-ridden timber. With
a little discriminate thinning out in the early stages
of growth, Nature would accomplish the rest; and in
30 or 40 years the State would have a fine forest and,
incidentally, a big commercial asset. Anyone familiar
with the hardwood forests of the North Coast can
have ocular proofs of this in the areas felled by the
early selectors. Some of these were abandoned, and
are now covered by healthy trees, which if pro-
tected for a few years from the timber-destroying
mania of the settlers would be fit for the sawmill.

“Mrs. Lance Itawson”; His Gaiters of
Papua can have his native “converts” for- me. I
know them —have known them since the ’Bo’s. As
far back as that time there was a mission station
on Fraser’s Island, and the “converts” were its
joy and pride. One time the rations gave out and
the blacks deserted and came over to us on the
mainland, to steal and fight, loaf and drink (when
they could). They were given to standing at the
street corners in Maryborough and repeating the
Lord’s Prayer and the Ten Commandments for
sickapence: “You gib it sickapence, I say Lord's
Player, I say it Cleed [Creed], I say it Command-
ments,” was their way of drawing a crowd and
taking up a collection. When asked why they didn’t
return to the Mission, the reply generally was: “Him
got no more beef, no more phflour—me no wantem
Jesus Chlist.” Black Brother is not a Mission hoy
from inclination. He’d be anything while the tucker
lasted.
David Umti'iHiii, the of a new
sheep-shearing machine, and a full-blooded aborigine
of the Narrinyeri tribe, who formerly hunted the fero-
cious ’possum on the Lower Murray, yarns about his
brother
I don’t believe that because aborigines become
civilised and live ill houses they will die rapidly.
It is the chopping and changing' about that reduces
the numbers. The native lives, in a cottage for a
while, but suddenly hears-the call of the wild, and
goes out and makes a camp in the bush. If he
stuck to either the wurlie or the cottage he would
be all right, but shifting from one to the other brings
on pneumonia. It Would be far better for the full--
bloods if they Were working in a city, They Would
have so many things to see find think about that their
hereditary instincts would Weaken. To put them in
a mission station that is surrounded by the bush is
like placing a chicken in reach of a tame fox. A
black will Want to camp in the bush at some time
or other, and the chopping and changing in this
condition of life will bring on lung trouble. The
common ailments at the mission station are colds,
bronchitis, pneumonia and indigestion.

Mr. Public House is the latest person to
be threatened with industrial trouble in N.
S. Wales. The position is about as tangled
as a serpent in a fit. The Ma .State’s pub
servitors were originally organised by the
Hotel, Club and Restaurant Employees’
Union. This body obtained a regulation of
conditions through the Industrial Court.
There are, however, rival organisations, one
of which is the Federated Liquor Trades
Employees’ Union. As a preliminary to
taking matters before the Commonwealth
Arbitration Court, the Federated Beer Union
secured a compulsory conference with the
employers in the various States; but Mr.
Public House of N.S.W. refused to come to
any agreement, holding that the award ob-
tained in the State by the Hotel, Club and
Restaurant Employees’ organisation made
further action unnecessary. Now the fede-
rated union has a considerable membership,
and is. threatening industrial battle. A
strike is not feasible; so the Federal Union
threatens to take its agreement to the hotel
licensees individually, and ask them to sign.
Those that refuse it proposes to put on a
black list, and it hopes to get the Labor
Council to declare a boycott against their
beer-pumps. If Mr. Public House in
general is obdurate, the Union will try an
agitation to have all beer pumps brought
under the early closing law. The Union is
taking . a lot for granted, for its success
with either the State Government or the
Labor Council is doubtful; nevertheless Syd-
ney may yet see rival Messrs. Pub waving
different badges of “whiteness,” given by
rival . organisations, while the puzzled
unionist will try to worry out which beer
really is black and bad. He will probably
wind up by deciding that all beer is amber,
and there is none that isn’t good on a hot
day. Between drinks, if he is wise, he will
decide that, since the Commonwealth con-
trols the tariff, and the tariff and wages are
inextricable, the sooner the whole business
of fixing wages, etc., is passed over to the
Commonwealth the better.
Au ancient Maori woman died the other
day at Orakei (M.L.). She was declared
to be one of the tribe that, in 1840, sold to
Captain Hobson the land on which the city
of Auckland now stands. Presumably there
was a person named Hobson. Her name
was Katene Rewiti and she was alleged to
be about 101 years old. The alleging party
is at large, but his address is obscure and
fleeting.
“B. J.”: Are there any other bishness
men who treat their clients in the habitu-
ally arrogant manner of some of the chiefs
of the amusement industry hereabouts? Not
even patent-medicine vendors more con-
sistently misrepresent the quality of their
goods. Even if the theatrical man carries
out his contract, more or less, in regard to
the seat which he has sold, he may still, if
he. feels that way, give an entertainment
quite different from the one his clients have
paid to see. For instance, a change of
programme was announced for the Thurs-
day of Lauder’s last week in Melbourne,
but only one of the seven new items adver-
tised came to pass; so that those who had
come to listen to new stuff, having paid
their money, took the management’s choice.
What would be the position of a publisher
who put the letterpress of a July magazine
in an August cover and sold the thing as
the latest issue?
Condensed yarn of the Genteel Landlady:
She said she was just having her afternoon nap.
Or was it nip?
Anyway, her house wasn’t like some she could
name. She wasn’t a Colonial herself. She took
only the best class of boarders, and took them for a
pastime, her husband being the head of a State
department, and having considerable means. Never
any unpleasantness here, no irregular payments, no
late hours, no drink, and none of this familiarity.
“And don’t you let them get familiar, either.
There’s a young girl here—but you’ll find out for
yourself.
“Now, see this room here, Kid. A young actor
has it. It’s a bit untidy now. He says to me the
other day, ‘When are you going to clean my room
out, Nell?’ I says, ‘When are you going to pay
me?’ Ah, you don’t know them.
“Wait till I tell you about the two sports in
the top room. I won’t have no bad language or
carrying on in my house, and I told them so one
night last week. Two o’clock it was; and, look, Kid,
they oidy laughed and says, ‘Come on and have a
drink, missis.’ And me that doesn’t touch it, and
neither does my husband.
“I set my teeth, and I says, ‘By the living Gawd,
you’ll clear out of this damn house to-night, or
I’ll get the traps in.’ I came down, got on my
clothes, got the revolver, and went into the young-
actor’s room. He was just getting into bed, and I
says, ‘Will you stand by me as a man?’ Well, I
went upstairs again, and I says, ‘Now, then, out of
this, you rotten mongrels, or, by the living Cheddar,
I’ll shoot you dead where you stand.’
“And just then if the boss didn’t come home, and
he’d had a fair drop, and between the mob of
them things was lively. But they didn’t know me,
son. When my blood’s up, and I have a drop of
drink in, I’ll fight-t-t. I let off that revolver; and
when they took it from me —the d—n cowards—l
up with a beer bottle and smashed the lamp to
smithereens. And the d—n curtains blazed up
and ■
“Oh, I see; too far from the city. Well, you
know best, Kid.”
