[F. C. Jones., Elgar-road, Burwood, Vic.]
Great are the Fishers among men. It
was one, Andrew, who gave us that Aus-
tralian fleet for which now we sleep more
soundly o’ nights; it was another, Johu,
who, in the face of other fierce opposition,
insisted on the concentration of Britain’s
naval strength in the North Sea.
“Westerner” : Attorney-General Tommy
Walker, of Westralia, who has been in the
limelight lately, was for some years a familiar
figure on the Inky Way. lie edited Perth
Sunday Times (which paper is now fiercely
attacking him), and afterwards moved on to
Kalgoorlie to take John Drayton’s place as
chief charioteer of the Sun. Between whiles
he launched a sensational journal of his own
(Perth Sunday Press), but piled it up on
the rocks. Tommy is a brilliant speaker,
but a dull and diffuse writer, with a style
as stodgy as cold porridge. His journalistic
recipe was to mix an ounce of fact with a
ton of verbiage. If he had to record the fact
that Bill Jones was before the beak charged
with being drunk and disorderly, he would
start out by recounting the glorious “burst”
with which Noah celebrated the grounding
of the Ark ; quote the story of Dot and his
daughters ; look in at the marriage at Cana
in Galilee; glance at the “banquet” of Plato,
the solitary repasts of Lucullus and the orgies
of Nero and Heliogabalus ; and after filling
a column with the names of famous “tanks,”
ancient and modern, would flop down to the
lame conclusion that Bill Jones was fined
10s. The outstanding feature of his editor-
ship of the Sunday Times was a libel action
brought against the paper by a parson named
Price, whom Tommy had accused of illicit
housemaid. The information came from the
girl herself; bfit on examination her story
resolved itself into the same old imaginative
yarn, and the narson collected damages.
Among many other things in his varied
career, Walker was for years a Freethought
lecturer. He once showed the present writer
his library of controversial works —Tom
Paine, Bradlaugh, Ingersoll, G. W. Foote
and the rest. On the writer remarking how
dead these old controversies were, Tommy
threw out his chest and exclaimed: “Do
you want to know why? It’s because we
have won the day!” And from the pride
with which he spoke, it was easy to see that
he believed he was entitled to no small share
in the honor and glory of the victory.
In these days the big Broken Hill Prop.
Co. is a mixed grill. The chairman, Hugo
Carl Emil Muecke. has long been the Ger-
man Consul for South Aus. The general
manager. Guillaume Daniel Delprat, is Con-
sul for Holland in Adelaide. If these two
settle their international differences in the
usual manner there will be a Dutch victory
in the first round. David Baker, the man-
ager of the iron and steel works at New-
castle, is ’Murikan, of course; and
equally, of course, the chief engineer.
Lindsay, is Scotch. The Broken Hill
manager, Horwood, is Australian —a Ben-
digo boy 30 years ago, trained for
a start at the Melbourne Uni.’s Engi-
neering School. Apart from Muecke,
the Board almost without exception takes
parritch for its breakfast, and has been like
that for 25 years.
C. 11. Bertie, the Sydney Municipal
Librarian, is advocating the establishment
of a Museum, distinct from the general
Museum, for the preservation of curios con-
nected with the city. He suggests that Vau-
cluse House, the old home of the Wentworths,
now public property, would be a suitable
place for the glorified waxworks show. A
hundred years ago this Vaucluse was the
home of Henry Brown Hayes, the titled
gentleman who had abducted a Cork quaker-
ess, Miss Pike, and got a life sentence for it.
He, by the way, after receiving his speedy
pardon, established the first Masonic Lodge
in Sydney.
From “Rosna,” in London: —-
AVe have just erected a statue of Captain Cook;
and as it is only 144 years since the useful sailor
acquired the best country on earth for us, this seems
precipitate. However, we made up our minds to it
with the celerity of lightning in exactly six years,
on the strength of a letter from Joe Carruthers, of
N.S.W., who pointed out what would have hap-
pened if Captain Cook had never landed there, etc.
The statue is in bronze, and is on the wrong side
of the Admiralty Arch, where no one will be in
danger of meeting it unawares. Prince Arthur of
Connaught was employed to disrobe it —the Con-
naughts being much in demand for Dominion jobs
of this kind. The usual busy people looked on,
guarded by the party of Australian cadets who are
here, to study the ways of the army. A great grand-
nephew of the captain, one F. W. Kealey, was also
present to show his gratitude at this swift appre-
ciation of the services of his ancestor.
How to be Beautiful.
By RITA MOYA.
It Is not every woman’s good fortune to be endowed by Nature with fine features and
a flawless complexion, but nowadays it is possible to make the plainest fade attractive, and
that too in the privacy of one’s home, without recourse to beauty specialists, which usually
involve expensive treatments and much loss of time. I shall endeavor to show you in the
following lines how much may be done in this direction bv any woman of ordinary intelli-
gence. When facial applications are necessary, use only the pure ingredients just as they
come to the chemist himself. Do not allow yourself to be persuaded into buying some
cheap, ready-made preparation instead. Any chemist will gladly obtain the original con-
centrated ingredients for you, if you insist, and although he may not have them in stock,
you will be well advised to wait while be orders them for you. The improvement in your
appearance will be ample compensation for any trouble taken to obtain these simple and
harmless beautifiers.
Beauty in Breathing.—A great deal can be
done towards brightening the eyes by system-
atic breathing for 10 minutes each morning
and evening. Breathe slowly and deeply to
the fullest capacity of the lungs. You should
stand erect by an onon window. The corsets
should not be worn during this exercise.
About Shampooing.—Even the best shampoo
is somewhat drying, and if the hair is not
naturally oily I suggest that just before the
shampoo you apply olive oil to the scalp, rub-
bing it into the hair roots vigorously. Then
use pure stallax for the shampoo. Dissolve a
leaspoonful in a cup of hot water. This will
leave the hair very clean, soft and fluffy.
An Instantaneous Beautifler. —Instead of face
powder, use a simple lotion made from one
ounce of cleminite and four teaspoonfuls of
water. This lotion will tone and clear the
skin, and acts as a protection against sun and
wind. A little applied with the finger-tips
instantly gives the skin a delightful “bloomy”
appearance. No powder is necessary, and the
result lasts ail day long under the most try-
ing conditions.
To Permanently Remove Superfluous Hair.—
It is a simple matter to remove a downy
growth of hair temporarily, but to remove it
permanently is quite another matter. It is a
pity that it is not more generally known that
powdered pheminol may be used for this pur-
pose. Apply it directly to the hair. The
''’'•’"ended treatment is designed not merely
to instantly remove the hair, but also to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Any chemist
should be able to- supply you with an ounce
of pheminol, which quantity should be suffi-
cient.
The Real Cause of Most Bad Complexions.—
It is an accepted fact that no truly beautiful
•oinplexion ever came out of jars and bottles,
and the longer one uses cosmetics the worse
the complexion becomes. Skin, to be healthy,
must breathe. It also must expel, through the
pores, its share of the body’s effete material.
Creams and powders clog the pores, in-
terfering both with elimination and breathing.
If more women understood this there would
be fewer self-ruined complexions. If they
would use ordinary mercolised wax instead of
cosmetics they would have natural, healthy
complexions. This remarkable substance is
not absorbed by the skin; its action is just
the opposite. The skin repels mercolised wax,
and at the same time throws off all imperfec-
tions. An exquisite new complexion peeps
out. quite free from any appearance of artifi-
ciality. Apply nightly, like cold cream, for a
week or two, washing it off in the morning.
About Hair Tonics. —Each week almost one
hears of some wonderful discovery for im-
proving the hair, and although this paragraph
may seem a little superfluous, an old-fashioned
recipe may coim as a welcome change. One
thing about it is that it will grow hair, and
also prevent it falling out. From your chem-
ist get an original package of boranium, to
this add I-pint of bay rum, allow it to stand
SO minutes, then add sufficient water to make
half a pint. Rub briskly into the scalp with
the flnsrer-tips and you will immediately ex-
perience that clean, tingling sensation which
is a sure sign of healthy action. [2
BOYS OF THE BULL-BEER BRAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Echuca '’Vic.) the loyal multitude has virtu-
ously refused to drink German beer.
Ah, brethren, this is war indeed,
Insatiable hate!
There many are who’ll gladly bleed
For home and country and for creed,
But few, I dare to state,
Who nobly for their country dear
Will turn their backs on any beer.
In fair Echuca now-a-night
It is not safe to go
Around the town extremely tight,
Unless prepared to make it right,
And definitely show
The drink that put you on your ear
Is French or Dutch or British beer!
In this emergency a pi .n
Echuca’s hit upon.
There every upright British man
Drinks all the English beer he can,
And when the booze is gone
His loyalty to manifest
He sticks the labels on his chest!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
LITTLE VERSES OF WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Red glows the forge with the fires of hate ;
Loud rings the anvil ’neath the hammer-strokes
of War,
Ho! for the blacksmith welding fast a State 1
Bui the bright sparks perish on the cold, black
floor !
The Kaiser’s Promise:
He said the Belgian lands would smile—
Or' so the story runs;
But every dreadful shattered mile
They looked in vain to see it smile.
It only grinned with guns!
Song of the German Soldier Besieging Liege:
Why should I march to Death
When Death is close behind?
The frontier guns’ hot breath
Has our own trenches lined
With shattered comrades who
Will nevermore draw breath.
Now Russia’s breaking through,
Why should I march to Death?
Why should I march to Death
When Death behind me comes?
I feel his charger’s breath -
I hear his rolling drums!
For those behind I pray
With every sobbing breath!
My Emperor ! Oh, say,
Why must I march to Death?
N.s.w.
E.FRANCIS.

PRACTICAL PRESCRIPTION AGAINST
STOMACH ACIDITY.
BY A SPECIALIST.
“Nine-tenths of all eases of stomach
trouble nowadays,” says a leading specialist,
“are caused by too much acid. In the begin-
ning the stomach itself is not diseased, but if
this acid condition is allowed to continue the
acid is very likely to eat into the stomach
walls and produce stomach ulcer or cancer,
either of which may render a radical surgical;
operation necessary, even to prolong life..
Therefore an ‘acid stomach’ is really a dan- 1
•gerous condition, and should be treated seri-
ously. It is utterly useless to take pepsin'
and ordinary stomach tablets. The excess
acid must be neutralised by the administra-;
tion of an efficient antacid. For this pur-
pose physicians nearly always recommend'
taking half a teaspoonful of bisurated mag-
nesia in a littld water after each meal.
Larger quantities may be used if necessary, 1
as it is absolutely harmless.. But be sure to:
get the bisurated magnesia, as other forms
of magnesia have not the same action in the
stomach as the bisurated, and frequently do]
more‘harm than good.”*
A Simple Recipe for Clearing
the Complexion.
Exposure to hot winds and sun makes the
nerves and muscles of the face tired and life-
less, and the skin shrivelled, wrinkled and
unhealthy.
If anyone with a sallow, blotchy, wrinkled
or pimply complexion will only take a little
trouble in following the directions here given
they will find a marvellous improvement in
their complexion. Get some Puritol Wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply at
night, washing off in the morning with warm
water and soap. Then lap a little cold
water 10 times upon the face with the hands,
allowing it to get thoroughly round the
closed eyes. The treatment should be fol-
lowed out in every detail to ensure success,
and undoubtedly accounts for many of the
clear complexions so much admired by those
who do not yet know the secret. See that
you get the right kind, it will not injure the
most delicate complexion.*
THE HOWARD: Exact.
T n 1829 Edward Howard was apprenticed to
Aaron Willard, Jun., the descendant of a long
line of English Watchmakers, who settled in the
Massachusetts Colony about the middle of the
eighteenth century.
In 1840 he started in business for himself. Up
to that time watches had always been made by
handwork exclusively. Howard’s plan was to
make automatic tools that should perfect the
parts of a watch, leaving to the hand the task of
assembling and finishing the parts.
In 1842 the first Howard Watch was produced
—a thoroughly practical watch. The Howard of
to-day is the finest practical timepiece in the
world for a man to carry in his pocket.
It has a patented hard-tempered steel balance,
which will not knock out of true with the jolt
and jar of the severest service.
Peary carried a Howard on his trip to the
Pole.
Orville Wright relied on a Howard in his Aero-
plane Tests.
Admiral Chas. D. Sigsbee carried a Howard for
forty years.
Send your address for the Illustrated Watch
catalogue.
PROUDS LTD.
Jewellers and
Watchmakers,
187 PITT STREET, SYDNEY.

[Edward Dyson, St. Kilda, Vic.]
“Liberalism” is again waving the Rural
Workers’ log in the face of the gentle agri-
culturist, and urging him to save himself
by voting the Fusion ticket. The Argus
publishes the log and asks the farmer how
lie will like that, although it knows very
well that, if the log goes before the Arbitra-
tion Court, reductions are bound to be made
—just as they were made in the journalists'
log, otherwise the Argus would be paying
senior reporters £9 a week, instead of £7.
Moreover, in this matter, it will make no
difference to the farmer which party is
returned, since neither has offered to amend
the Arbitration Act so as to prevent the
rural workers going before the Court.
[B. J. H. Owen, 108 Albert-street, East Melbourne.]
Defence Minister Milieu :
One of the greatest difficulties in equipping the
expeditionary force is that of providing the large
number of horses required. The difficulty arises, not
from any shortage of horses in Australia, but from
the necessity of procuring them in the very limited
time that is available. The kind most urgent] v
needed are gunners’ and draught horses, which should
all be broken.
With stud farms in various parts of this
continent, the defence authorities would
have been able to supply their needs simply
by wiring the farm-managers, instructing
them to send the required number of trained
animals of various classes to port without
delay—quite a different proceeding from
scouring the country for more or less un-
trained animals, with a time-limit which
may mean the acceptance of animals that
would not be passed otherwise. Presumably,
as the Minister now knows the “difficulty,”
Australia will, make a start at. stud-farm-
ing forthwith. A second “difficulty” may
not be as successfully climbed over as the
present one has been.
[Frank llenty, Glen Iris, Victoria.]
One of the few interesting election fights
is taking place in Wentworth (N.S.W.),
where Labor-man Robert Cruickshank is
giving Kelly some anxious moments. Cruick-
shank is a. journalist, who also knows the
advertising side of the business, a, good
speaker and an experienced campaigner. He
long fought with Drake, the Federal Attor-
ney-General, on the White Australia side in
Queensland. At the last State elections
Labor won on the second ballot in Waverley,
and only missed Woollahra by a whisker or
so;, and since then the Laborites reckon that
their vote has been so augmented in Padding-
ton, Woollahra and Waverley that Kelly's
margin will be wiped clean out.
[Harold Mercer, Katoomba, N.S.W.]
If by chance the big armies—German on
one side and Belgian, French and British
on the other—fight one stupendous battle
instead of a number of small engagements,
that shock will be the biggest thing of its
kind in history, so far as concerns the num-
bers engaged. The record battle up to date
is Liao Yang. Chalons, with Aetius, the
Roman, in command, comes next. Certainly
there, are wild and awful conflicts in the
Book of Mormon, showing how God went
to America in the time before Moses, and
24,000,000 Americans —more or less —were
induced to do battle, and everybody was
killed save one, and his name was Jones, or
Briggs or something of the sort. But the
best authorities on fortification have doubts
about that battle which, according to the
Prophet _ Smith,- happened when God went
to America.
An appeal that this country should keep
on. buying British goods, and thus
help the Ma Country, reached here
last week. About the same moment a
number of the smaller Australian news-
papers, which carry limited supplies of raw
material, applied to British agents in this
country for paper. They tyere told that
supplies were low. They cjabled to Eng-
land, and learned that Belgian and French
and German paper mills being practically
closed, English supplies were also low. The
Ma Country, it seems, is well off for grind-
stones and dried cod. but that doesn’t greatly
help the newspaper man.
The State Governments, whose foreign
borrowings have been a scapdal. are now
putting a brave face on things and declaring
that, though foreign loans must necessarily
cease for a while, the finances couldn’t be
better. So the inference is that the borrow-
ing was quite needless, and that Holman
has been pawning the country for a jest,
and Scaddan lias been selling posterity into
financial bondage out of pure wickedness.
Australia is beginning to got a proper
view of the value of the bygone Fisher
Government. In the days when the Oppo-
... . ~ , , , , ~ “Hnnnt
sition talked at random about a tmpot
Australian fleet and a conscript army,
Andrew Fisher and his party pushed on
with the details of the national defence
scheme. Had the Liberals been on deck
and put their own alleged principles into
practice we would have liad no army, no
great battle cruiser, no small-arms factory,
no Commonwealth notes to ease the strain
on the gold reserves. Truly, the gold reserves
were never strained, hut they might have
been had that German army rolled through
Belgium like a wave, and the High Seas
Fleet swept the British First Battle Squad-
ron off the top of the North Sea. Fisher
is a man who lias done a heap of things,
Cook is a politician who merely sits on
that heap and asks Australia how it thinks
it could possibly manage without him. He
is the noisy fowl that cackles after another
lien lias laid the egg.

IN THE COUNTRY OR MARY WARS.
PROUD GERMAN SON : “Fader, I am ordered to der front.”
PROUD GERMAN FATHER: “Yah, but which front is dot?”
A timber company which had applied to
the Tariff Commission for an increase of
duty on dressed timbers came along the
other day and wanted to withdraw the appli-
cation. It foolishly explained why: It
thought of closing down its mils in Aus-
tralia, and having its timber cut in Siberia,
where labor was cheaper. Which means, of
course, that the present duty is not sufficient
to protect the high-wage Australian workman
from the.cheap labor of the Russian ; where-
fore what was an Australian industry is
likely to become a Siberiap business. The
change will make little difference to the
co., which will still get its profits; but it
will mean a lot to the workmen who will lose
their jobs. Apparently the co. forgot that
the Commission might be more or less in-
terested in the workman.
[Harold Mercer, Katoomba, N.S.W.]

From “Croson”: The war has at least
taught our Tory-minded State Premiers the
practicability of regulating prices. Faced
with the cornering of necessaries 'by ultra-
loyal wholesalers, the said Premiers at once
discovered that the alleged ‘'impossible in-
terference with trade” was quite feasible,
All they had to do was to follow the lead
0 f Westralia, which penalises any person
who sells food-stuffs over and above any
required for his owu personal consumption
—at a price higher than that fixed by a
commission. And they promptly did so.
It will be handy by-and-bye to remember
that the “hasty legislation” thus initiated
may not only be continued hut extended,
so that the prices, not of food-stuffs only,
but of other commodities, may be fixed by
law.

BRITAIN’S CHAMPION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Because the very air would tell
And, maybe, sway the fight,
The battle-fleet went out to Hell
Across the tides of night.
Silent and black, their foes to quell,
The great ships took their flight.
The sweeping shadows, line on line,
Stretched out across the tide-
/. tested cable, tough and fine —-
A thousand years of pride:
'.fink after link, without a sign,
It went —and Britain sighed.
Fair Britain. Mistress of the North,
She closed the casement then.
As in the years when knights rode forth,
She looked not out again,
But prayed that they with fire and wrath
Might free the sons of men.
Though Rumor creep with tales of dread
Arid conjure sights of fear,
Fair Britain will not turn her head
Or lend an eager ear,
For ancient pride with honor wed
Holds human life not dear.
His sword was keen, his arm was strong
And glorious was his state ;
He held the Right against the Wrong
And Freedom was his mate.
Who finds the-hours too dark —too 10ng?....
His trumpet at the gate!
N.s.W.
E. O’F.
INDIGNANT PROTEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Germany is being doped with fake news.— Cable.
I sympathise with those poor folk
: (Though enemies they be),
Who daily have to sadly soak
In vague uncertainty.
It makes my indignation fuse;
In fact, I cannot bear,
To think the Germans fake the news—
Thank God, we don’t live there!
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Reginald (commonly known as "Snowy )
Baker is organising a great military tattoo at the
Agricultural Ground for the afternoon, and _an
assault at arms at the Stadium in the evening. The
date is September 7, and all loyal Australians are
notified to keep that day quite clear. The object is
patriotic—to wit, the raising of funds in connection
with the families of the men who are now on active
service. Further particulars will appear in the next
issue.
Ivennington Oval (London), where the
cricket matches used to be, is now in use as a
military drill ground. And still C. B. Fry is look-
ing forward to the day when “Cricket shall be the
bond of union between the nations; the basis of
understanding; the game of games."
A “sporting” cricket match was that
played this English season between old boys of Eton
and Harrow. Ilawke was asked to captain the one,
and A. J. Webbe the other ; but they preferred to let
younger men play. Jackson and Maclaren, both
Harrow, were the veterans of the 22 ; and they
scored 55 and 73. In the Eton team were Bosanquet,
young Tennyson and Johnson, who was here long ago
with Warner’s amateur lot, and now made top score,
102. Harrow led on the first innings, but the game
was left drawn.
* * * *
The war lias knocked to shreds and frag-
ments a goodly array of atmospheric castles in the
sporting world. Three club football teams and a
N.S.W. lacrosse tribe were in readiness for a visit
to Maoriland, but the war has sent the tours to the
dustbin. An amateur expedition to Panama Exhibi-
tion is off. The Australian XI which proposed to
trek to South Africa, will stay in its ain countree
to defend its hearthstone in ease things go tempo-
rarily wrong in the North Sea. The importation of the
army of hike pushers from Europe and America is
suspended till this cruel war is o’er—when a fair
percentage of the best of them will have gone out
in a blaze of glory. It is quite a record hold-up.
* * * *
Another contest that has gone overboard
owing to the war is the race for the America Cup.
The general mind is too occupied by graver thing's
1o be interested in a yacht race—so the event is
postponed for one year.
“Bilrock” (from London, .Tuly 10) :
Merrie England suffered some sad shocks as a result
of the last seven days’ doings in the sporting world.
At Henley, in the greatest rowing carnival of the
year, two American crews, one Canadian and one
German, outed the opposing English rowers in the
Grand Challenge Cup. the Harvard University wiping
the floor of old Father Thames with the famous
Leander Club. Thus J. Bull found himself in the
mortifying position of being without a representative
in the semi-finals, and the British lion dragged his
tail mournfully in the mud. The field narrowed
flown to a final collision between the two Harvard
crews. The Yanks use a short stroke, and their
style is very similar to that of the Australian crew
which swept all behind it at Henley in 1912. Eng-
lish amateur scullers were also left. In the final of
the Diamond Sculls the Cambridge expert. Stuart,
met Sinigaglia, the beefy Italian, who, rowing in
anything but a finished style, pushed his craft in bv
brute strength two or three lengths to the good.
Never in the history of rowing have English clubs
received such a set-hack. It was the triumph of a
modern stroke with devil in it over an antique and
bewhiskered style, said to have been originated by
Charon on the River Styx. Nothing short of a
cyclone, however, will induce John Bull to wake up
and rearrange his stvle. Then the Australian tennis
learn fell upon AVimbledon and smote it hip and
thigh, bestowing the very father of a hiding upon
the English crack players. Spectators were treated
to the astonishing spectacle of a semi-final for the
All England Men's Doubles Championship being- con-
tested by three Australasians (Brookes. Wilding and
Doust) and a Greek (Mavrogordato). For the lasi-
three years tlm Lawn Tennis Singles Championship
of England (which is tantamount to the champion-
ship of the world) has been held by a Maorilander.
who last week lost the title to an Australian. In
the same week Australian cricketer Tarrant, who
had temporarily given place to -T. W. TTearne, jumped
to the front- again in the list of batting averages.
But there are signs of a great awakening. A well-
known sporting writer in one of Uoandon’s big dailies
has come to light with the remark that “the supre-
macy of England in the realm of sport is being
seriously threatened.” #
The Y.R.C. suggestion that, all race-
meetings should he suspended was an essentially fool-
ish development of war scare. Regarded as an
expensive show for the grandstand public, and a
cheap excitement for the flatites, a race meeting has
the recommendation that all the money it causes to
change hands is kept in the country, whereas the
receipts of Harry bander or a foreign musical artist
go mostly out of the country. As to whether it is
less “seenily’-’ to gamble on a racecourse than to
laugh at the performance of an imported comedy co.
when guns are going off in Europe, the V.R.C. is
no licensed censor of the people’s pleasures. If the
people don't want amusement, race meetings will
stop soon enough, without being “suspended on
account of the war,” for the takings will drop to
freezing-point. The one practical patriotic thing'
the racing clubs in Vie. and N S.W. can do is. to
agitate for the legislation of the Tote, which pays
a percentage of its receipts to the Government, and
forces gamblers who live on the game to devote
themselves to other business. Some of them are
even driven to work, and this country needs workers.
The punter who nurses a hope of growing
rich on the losses of benevolent bookmakers had an
object-lesson in the first batch of N. S. Wales Income
Tax prosecutions. Thirty-seven people liable to tax
were sued : and of the lot twenty-nine were book-
makers, who had, it was said, never supplied returns,
on the beautiful plea that, as gambling was illegal,
the Commissioners had no claim. The magistrate,
of course, brushed aside the cheeky inference that
the man who acquires affluence by an illegal occupa-
tion need not pay the taxes which the legitimate
business man has to meet, and imposed tines and
costs total line' £4Ol, Nothing was said about the
taxes that had been dodged for years past; but the
axe of the Commissioners is sharp, and their arm
has a long reach, so McNoses may yet wish he had
paid up by instalments instead of waiting for the
expensive law to pursue him for the lot in a lump.
“Tote”: On May 80 the Wagga (X.S.W.)
Amateur Picnic Race Club held a horse meeting on
the course of the Murrumbidgee Turf Club. Book-
makers were tabooed, but a makeshift totalisator was
run. Its transactions, it was understood, were limited
to members and honorary members. The Crown Law
authorities prosecuted the club officials for betting on
horseraces contrary to the Gaming Act. The defence
was that all the club was doing was to assist its
members to run a series of sweepstakes such a> arc
held by the hundred at every race-meeting, and that
defendants were not personally making a profit out
of the scheme. The magistrate held that the club,
bv allowing its members (hon. or otherwise) to use
the club room for tote purposes, was not guilty of
betting .within the meaning of the Act. That being
so, what’s to prevent any club inaugurating the tote
and making its patrons hon. members on race days?
Uncle Sam, the brilliant gee of two years
ago, had an airing in a six-furlongs sprint at Flern-
ington last Saturday, and ran at the wrong end of
the field all the time. Uncle will do better than
this presently, of course, when he has been put to
strong work again, but as a possible winner of a
big race over a distance the old reprobate is not
worth considering. He seems to be a hopeless case
of nervous dobilitv.

The easy victory of Londonderry in the
Adelaide G. N. Hurdle Race was gall to a good
many punters who backed him for the maiden
hurdle race at Flemington last month. London-
derry then was talked about as a “rod in pickle,”
but his stable companion. Lady Pistol, carried the
money and did the trick. At Adelaide the gelding
met the mare on 111 b. better terms, and both were
placed. In the management of jumping horses the
Adelaide men—Jas. McCann trains Londonderry—
are at the head of their profession.
Judas MePalestine writes bitterly: When
the owners of racing nags hit upon such names as
Whakarewarewa, Algabohnvah, Ootacamund, Mosque-
terie, Mnesarahus, Spegliesen, Laissey, Waukenphast,
and so on, it is not surprising that decent Asiatics
of my tribe get our tongues twisted into knots. Our
errors are excusable in a measure, but a glance
at the Turf Register suggests that owners send the
stable boys to enter their horses on its classic
pages. Anacrion was, presumably, called after the
Greek poet who warbled of wine and women, and
signed his verses Anacreon when he carted them
round to The Bulletin of the day. The brute is a
four-vear-old gelding by Tom Moore, and as that
excellent bard published some translations of his
Greek predecessor, and was occasionally known as
the “Irish Anacreon,” the reason for the horse’s
name is obvious —but why spell it wrongly? Nudity
was the name chosen for one promising filly, and the
animal managed to get loose in the racing world as
New Ditty. There are a few more samples of the
same thing, but these are enough to go on with.
Ali [?] Snylor [?] the scalp of [?]
to his collection.
A pleasant record fell to a ticket-holder in
a Tatt.’s sweep the other day, when one particular
piece of paper drew £5O, £4O and £2O cash
prizes. Writer has sunk some hundreds of pounds in
Tatt.’s dining liis misspent life and never drawn a
copper. The person who can scoop three prizes
on one investment is evidently an abnormality, a
caitiff who has rubbed shoulders with a cross : eyed
Chinaman, a brownie or a Bulgarian general.
How the motor does flourish in Australia!
For the half-year ended June 30 the value of motor
vehicles imported, either whole or in parts, amounted
to £1,032,041 —a bound of £201,632 on the figures
of the previous year. If only half this enormous
mass of manufacturing had been done in Australia
we should not be so hard hit to-day at the mere
rumor of a war in far-away Europe, which war has
not vet properly begun.

,T. It. Hooper, of the Ma State, made small
spuds of the opposition in the Australian billiard
championship at Sydney last week. In the first
round S. 11. Wilson, of South Australia, put up a fine
struggle against the Ma State champion, but
went down when the scores were 1200 to 936 against
him. L. Beauchamp, of Victoria/ outed Artley, of
Queensland, by 1200 to 563. The final proved a
good thing for Hooper over Beauchamp, and the Ma
Stater ran out when the scores stood at 2000 to
1003. Hooper hails from Newcastle, and his mastery
with the cue threatens big things. Those who ought
to know opine that Hooper is the best amateur
N.S.W. has yet produced.

Bill Anderson passed out the other day -
not the modern AA’illiam who runs the bellowdramas and
gee-gees, but an old-time Victorian bookmaker of the
same name. J. J. Miller writes reminiscently re this
Anderson of yore: “He was one of the promoters ot
the fund, for sending Arthur Orton, the ‘Ticlibornc
Claimant,’ to England, and another of his unlucky
ventures was a scheme to break the bank at Monte
Carlo. Bill Anderson had the late Bill Branch with
him in the bank-breaking job. They prepared an
infallible ‘system,’ and went to Monte Carlo with a
lot of money. They came back convinced. The
bank repulsed the attacking party with heavy loss.”
* * * *
Sydney and Petersham, in the lacrosse
final at Hampden Oval (Sydney), on Saturday, pro-
vided a tussle worth roaring at. In the first quarter,
Sydney bounded off and led by four goals to one. At
half-time it still had the sniff of victory in its
nostrils, and showed its heels by six goals to four.
The third quarter, however, saw Petersham shake
thing’s up, and go into the lead by seven goals to
six:. The Anal quarter was a hurricane affair, and
Petersham —which probably didn’t fail to have a
dope in the interval—lasted just long enough to win
by 10 goals to nine. The peculiarity about Sydney
lacrosse premiership has been the success of Peter-
sham in gathering the title five times in the past
six years; while Sydney lays claim to filling- the
runner-up position in a like number of seasons.
* * * *
Parramatta to Sydney “semi-Marathon”
on Saturday proved the weak joints in the physical
armor of the competitors. AVarm weather and clouds
of rolling dust played havoc with the bold spirits
who started in the 15-mile jog. Before half-way was
reached distress signals were frequent, and demands
for liquid refreshment were hung out emphatically.
Choked runners reeled into the bars all along the
way and gasped for beer. Some of them couldn’t
even gasp, and merely beckoned for beer. The
roadway was in a shocking state. It always is, but
on this occasion it seemed worse than usual, with
long stretches of broken and unbound blue metal at
frequent intervals. Before a similar race is held
the bloques or coves who choose the track should
be made to walk over the chosen highway. The
result was a win for F. Hourigan, who started from
the 15-minute line; while AV. E. Corben, from
scratch, put up the fastest time by throwing the
broken and decrepit distance behind in llir. 39min.
lOsec.—about four minutes slower than his own
record for the same journey. That dust would
break up anything.
* * * *
Otago (M.L.) cross-country champion-
ship, decided at Dunedin recently, was won by M.
Mickclson, who beat J. Beatson, the holder and the
present Australasian three-mile champion, bv 100
yards. This is the first time in six years that Beat-
son has been defeated in this event, he having won
it from 1909 to 1913 inclusive.

tialia, and with difficulty won through with four

The tour of the Maoriland All Blacks
grows monotonous. On Saturday last at Sydney
Sports Ground the team scored its tenth successive
victory. It was not a battle but a battue, the scores
at the sound of the whistle standing at 22 to seven.
This is not war—that is to say, sport. However, .this
was the last smash, and on Saturday evening, after
bread had been broken and wine luid been spilt, the
ever victorious NA' left by the AVarrhnoo for the land
of the Maori. It scored 246 points against 47-. It
won three'matches, two of thorn consecutive struggles,
in which it scored 41 to nil. It never lost a game,
and it did as it liked.

«• * * *-
South Sydney looks fairly safe to bag the
football premiership. It still maintains its lead of
one point from Newtown, and as only one more after-
noon of fighting remains, South's chances look cos.'. .
On Saturday, South bumped into North Sydney, and
it was expected that a Homeric scrap would result.
Nothing of the sort happened. North just crumpled
up, blew out and went bung, and South triumphed by
19 to three. Which was plain Banagher. Newtown
kept its end of the log up by falling on the
depleted ranks of Glebe. Something had deprived
Glebe of its best and bravest, and second-grade men
were drafted into the ranks to fill the waste of war.
They were not seasoned to be trodden on by first-
grade men, and though they struggled gamely enough
they had not the faculty of controlling luck —which
is a. fine accomplishment in any walk of luck. Glebe's
name was Sewage, and Newtown leaped delighted to
victory with 18 points to five. Balmain gave West-
ern Suburbs an awful hiding, the figures being f>9 to
eight. This sort of happening is too painful to dwell
upon. Further comment is censored. But this Tittle
list was not in it with the upheaval of Annandale,
which walloped Eastern Suburbs, the premier teajn,
by 16 to 14. Think of it! Writer has always pro-
phesied good things from the plucky, hard-struggling
Annandale crowd, and now that particular mob Has
done it.
* * *
In the British Association’s wild and com-
plicated rumpus, which no man who is not a Soccer
enthusiast can follow, Granville is easily ahead in
the First League premiership. It is six points in -
front of the next best, and has only two games to
plav. So it can lose with a light heart. On Satur-
day last Sydney, declining to face the music, passed
in a forfeit. Balmain, which is stronger and more
reliable at Soccer than at any other branch of the
toeball game, downed Annandale by four to nil.
Glebe overthrew Newtown by four to one, and North-
ern Suburbs played a scoreless draw with Canterbury.
* * * *
Melbourne was without senior football on
Saturday, League and Ass. having agreed to arrange
a vacant date on which the visiting English team
might tackle the N.S.W. Rugby Leaguers, for the edifi-
cation of Victorian innocents —and for the “gate.”
Here it may be remarked that the said “gate” was
hardly worth collecting, a poor 5000 or 0000, largely
M C.C. deadheads, being the measure thereof. The
game was cheerful and willing, but the tremendous
English forwards super-imposed themselves on the
proceedings, and none of their opponents seeijred over-
enthusiastic about tackling the elephants. At half-
time the scores were nine points to eight in Eng-
land’s favor ; but soon after resuming a penalty goal
put the Cornstalks in the lead. After that, Longstaff
was ordered off for rough play, but the teams were
equalised through Leggo being knocked out and having
to retire. The visitors ultimately prevailed, with
three tries (two converted) and four penalty goals to
N.S.W.’s three tries (two converted) and one penalty
goal—2l points to 15. The inexperienced spectators
didn’t seem enamored of the disturbance, many opin-
ing that if the local first-class teams bogged in on
(he reckless lines of the Britishers there would he a
call for more hospitals and ambulances and an extra
cemetery or two.
# *
The Davis Cup comes back to Australasia
after having spent two seasons over the water —
England in 1912 and America in 1913. The new
holders, Brookes and Wilding, with Doust and Dunlop
to fall hack on, had beaten Canada, Germany and
England. Last week they fell on America, the
holders. But it wasn’t a very desperate beating.
U.S.A. relied on McLoughlin and Williams for the
singles, with Bundy to give McLoughlin a hand in the
doubles. McLoughlin was the only one who caused
much damage, and he beat both Brookes and Wilding
in the singles—beat Brookes three straight sets (one
going to 17-15, a Davis Cup long-distance recordl
and Wilding three out of four. Williams lost each of
his singles,’ and the Australasian pair collared the
doubles without much bother. And once more The
Bulletin asks: Who in Australia is going to stand
up against McLoughlin, who is a colt, when Brookes
and Wilding are done? The habit of sending the
same two to play each year may win the Cup, hut it
is mud for the younger nlayers. Australasia lias now
had six wins ; British Isles five : and U.S.A. four.

The sudden come-back of David Smith at
Brisbane is explainable in a dozen different wavs.
Smith may have pulled himself together and made a
supreme effort. On the other hand, flushed by ins
former easy victory, Clabby may have grown careless.
Smith is a stronger man mentallv than the average
boxer, and if he had the moral fibre to pull himself
together after the crushing defeats he had endured,
anything was possible. He might have started on
another career of conquest, for the man who can out-
point Jimmy Clabbv for 20 rounds is obviously up
among the first flighters. That is, of course, it
•Tames were battling at his best. Anyhow, eveiy-
body is glad to hear that our Smith did overcome
that valiant Yankee, and he seems to have done it
in first-class style.

A walloping match that has been looked
forward to for “years and rears,” as the parson said,
eventuates at Sydney Stadium next Saturday night
when the pride of Australian lightweights, Hughie
Mehegan and Herbert McCov, will battle for the
championship of Australia. Mehegan’s luck has been
out for some time now, c.ud in meeting McCoy he is
up against the battle of his career. If he loses this
ho will fall from his high estate, and he no moiC’
ohamnion. If he wins, he will have demonstrated
that he is no back-number, and. in spite of his recent
defeats by foreigners, is still the best Australian
lightweight. It should be a sterling battle, as both
youths are, hard hitters and belong to the class of
fighters that hits all the time. They are always
good to watch.

Goodlet and Smith, Ltd. (Sydney), who
manufacture and deal in builders’ goods: —
To Re- Re-

Written off leaseholds 2,500 48,756
1913 .. 14,151 8 p.c. = 13,200 951 49,737
Freeholds written up 10,000 59,737
1914 . . 15,123 8 p.c. - 13,200 1,923 61,660
The co., which owned many freeholds, and
wrote them down pretty severely some years
ago, decided recently that it was a good
time to sell and then centralise its opera-
tions. In 1911-12 considerable business was
done in that way, and again last year. As
at June, 1912, freeholds were in the books at
£lB.OOO and leaseholds at £37,500. Last
year, after the further sales, the freeholds
were revalued and £lO,OOO was added to their
book value. Obviously, though it was pro-
posed to make other sales, there isn’t very
much left to do in that direction; and on the
present look of things it is just as well,
seeing that the property market isn’t likely
to get back for many a day to where it was
a few months ago.
The sale of property, along with a certain
amount of machinery and plant, has put a
different face on the accounts in recent
years:—■
1906. 1910. 1912. 1913. 1914.
£ £ £ £ £
Liabilities .. 90,170 93,552 15,180 16,855 7,902
Properties . . 100,670 93,670 55,500 44,000 i
Machinery and ; 100,274
plant, etc. 89,867 89,837 89,421 78,715 )
Ships.. .. 2,200 500 450 400
Stock.. .. 02,585 60,497 31,788 33,430 18,514
Cash and book
debts, etc. 32,177 55,251 60,907 81,647 122,433
Thus in the last 12 months cash and book
debts, including shares in other cos., have
increased by £40,786, while liabilities have
been reduced by £8893. Total £49,679. This
amount has been made up by reducing stocks
(£14.916), selling assets or writing them
down out of profits (£32,441), and accumu-
lating visible profits (about £2000). The
big item, “Cash and book debts, etc.,” cannot
be split up: but assuming that they are a
good lot, then the co.’s position is extra-
ordinarily good. And it is incredible that a
eo. like Goodlet and Smith, with an enviable
reputation behind it and a splendid name to
carry on with, would gild a doubtful item
with its cash. Together this item and the
properties represent practically the full
amount of the capital (£165,000), leaving
the stock and the plant and machinery—
which has been severely written down during
the last few years—to represent the reserves
and the handful of liabilities. As things
have turned out the reduction of the stocks
is greatly in the co.’s favor. There is going
to he a severe and probably long slump in
such materials as Goodlet and Smith mostly
handle; and when money in stocks that you
can’t sell is earning nothing but insurance
bills, it is a fine thing to have it in some-
thing else. Profits from trading will pro-
bably shrink a good deal: still, if the big
composite item before mentioned is as good
as the cash which forms part of it. the co.
may reckon itself extremely fortunate in
disposing of property and fixed assets and
in reducing its stocks just when it did.

The Castlemai.xe Brewery, Ltd. (Fre-
mantle, W.A.) :

Dividend ten To Re- Re-

Dec.. T 3. . 6224 10 „ „ 3150 904 2170 23,659
June, ’14.. 4954 10 „ „ 3675 873 406 24,065
That is an uncomfortable shrinkage in
profits; and of course the directors’ report
doesn’t explain it. Directors’ reports very
rarely do. In nine cases out of 10 you have
to he a friend of somebody’s to find out
whether a shrinkage is just a bit of passing
had luck or the result of a disease which is
likely to be chronic. All the light that can
be thrown upon the trouble in this case is
derived from this comparison:—
Gross Profit Operating Expenses, p.c.
June, from Sales. Expenses, of Profit on Sales.
1912 ~ £11,234 £6050 53.9 p.c.
1913 . . 12,888 7532 58.5 „
1914 .. 11,807 7814 66.2 „
Operating exes, include wages, fuel, taxes,
maintenauce (but not allowance for depre-
ciation) and salaries; and it looks as though
some of these items are moving along fast.
If isn't all wages, either: “salaries” have
moved like this-—£623, £745, £B7O. The
interest bill is also moving up : hut the new
capital may arrest it. Altogether, then, it
doesn’t seem as if a mere passing stroke of
bad luck has cut at the profit. Costs gener-
ally have gone up; and it’s usually a tough
job to pull them down again. it
is worth noting that, after allowing for
depreciation, there isn’t now any large mar-
gin of profit beyond the amount required to
pay the 10 p.c. dividend. Certainly the new
£lO,OOO of capital lately got in may not
have been earning its best throughout the
half-year, though it has been favored with a
full half-year’s dividend. As for the security of
the capital in the business nothing much
can be said, because five-sixths of the assets
are shovelled into one untidy heap—“ Fre-
ehold and leasehold properties, plant and
machinery, rolling stock, _ goodwill, water
supply, etc.” —and there is no telling liow
much of this is good, honest beer and how
much is goodwill froth. On the whole, the
position doesn’t look as good as the dividend.

until it can do flic necessary financing.
Mount Morgan is reaping the reward of
sending its blister to an Australian refinery.
Last month’s return shows *74760z. of gold,
with 618 tons copper, so approximately
£31.000 to £32.000 worth of gold is made
available by refining the* copper locally.
£31,000 per month will hardly pay costs, for
last month (four weeks) more than 28,000
tons were treated. Still, with economy, it
will go a long way.

The Inky Way.
“Nixpenee”: War pays-—tile newsboys.
During last week, when editions were pouring
out of the Sydney offices as fast as news and
alleged news concerning' the trouble in
■Europe would admit, the sellers were coining
money. One of them cleared £5 Ins. before
knocking off for tea.
A new English half-crown quarterly
announces itself as “The manifesto of the
Vortieists,” who seek to aim a “death-blow
to Impressionism and Futurism and all the
refuse of naif science.” But if its purpose
is vague its title, at any rate, is expressive:
it is the one beautifully simple word. Blast !
-Jf * # *
“.T.F.11.”: I have my doubts about the hustle
which has followed the alleged modernisa-
tion of London’s inky way. Chamberlain
passed out at 10 o’clock on a Thursday
night, in his London mansion in the South
Kensington quarter, and not a single Friday
morning paper had a line about it. Even
the Daily .\Jail. which is nearly always on
the spot, was napping. Immediately after
the death the two doctors posted a notice
to that effect on the porch, but either nobody
bothered to read it, or, if anybody did read
it. he did not know that it was
news worth money. Of course, if the
morning papers had known that Joseph
was sicker than usual (he had been
helpless for eight years) they would
have been on the alert, but the family was
reticent and gave no public intimation of
danger. So the Friday evening papers got
a scoop, and the Saturday morning ones
seemed ashamed of themselves in their stale
editorial appreciations and pages of belated
biography.
C. A. Pearson, the English newspaper
man. having gone blind himself, is battling in
London for a big fund to provide blind
readers with Braille literature. Just bow
much money is needed Pearson indicated in
a note tbe other day: “Ivanhoe” cost £4o
for plates to start with, and then every copy
cost 10s. fid. to produce.
"J.F.H.”: T. P. O’Connor, one of tbe
Irish M’s.r.. has built up a record in the
Inky Way. He started the Star. London’s
halfpenny evening paper, quarrelled with
partner Prof. Stuart (nephew of a one-time
Premier of N.S.WO, and retired. After
that be founded another evening paper, the
Sun. and passed it on to Horatio Bot-
tomle.v. Next it was a gossiping weekly—-
M.A.P. Here be quarrelled with partner
Iharson, and retired. T.P.’s Weekly fol-
lowed: and now he is quitting that “in order
to establish a new journal in the autumn.”
In a spare moment he also founded T.P's.
Monthly, hut that perished before he bad
time to quarrel with anybody. “T.P.” turns
out a tremendous amount of copy every week
for London and American papers, and is
constantly clicking his typewriter in the
House of Commons.
* * * » *
“Poetaster”: I recently visited Adam
Lindsay Gordon’s old home. Dingley Dell,
about a mile from the ocean at Port Mac-
Donnell (S.A.). and not far front Cape
Northumberland lighthouse. The cottage
nests in a pretty nook surrounded by a wood
of typical Australian trees. Of course, the
house shows signs of age and neglect, and
like the Pyramids of Ghizeh. the Wall of
China and the Eiffel Tower, there are dis-
tinguished names scribbled over if. such as
Potts. Watts, Clegg, Higgins, Ham and Wjn-
terbotham. The pronerty is owned by a
Mount Gambicr publican, and is carefully
locked up to keep out the nine Muses and
other vagrants.
GOING GENTLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(War poem made by a nervous bard with a censor
in the next room.)
I do not know a single thing,
But —/ am, not to tell!
So, in an undertone, 1 sing
That war is always Hell.
(Indeed it is—it's Hell!)
There’s just one thing to write about;
But nothing’s coming through,
And ITE sits ready to knock out
The little things 1 do —
The songs and pars. I do.
Down in the street news-liars ydl
The “news’’ of yesterday,
And eagerly to asses sell
The same old truss of hay-—
The stale, familiar hay.
Vague rumors hurtle round the town;
None can their authors catch ;
So many up—so many down.
It’s like a cricket match- -
Some distant cricket match.
I hear no gun —1 see no smoke;
The only foe / see
Is that perambulating- bloke
Who sells stale “news” to me —-
Poor, unimportant me!
And yet—-and yet I'm not to tell!
I Vho says that modern war ain't Hell?
x.s.w.
BLUE PENCIL.
CENSORED
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
[ wrote u title poem oti the European War;
1 used a lot of tricky rhymes no poet used before;
1 filled it up with slaughter and 1 sprinkled it with
gore—
To heights of verse my pen soared !
1 wrote in patriotic mood of England and of France;
I urged the Empire onward, and I yelled to it,
“Advance!”
1 smote the German heavily: my pen became a
lance —
But it was censored!
My Pegasus he hobbled, and he even clipped its
wings;
I wrote of and . and of many other tiling-.
lie passed a phrase or two, like “hitter mnrmur-
ings"
And even “War’s immense sword!”
All. this'was censored!
n.—(The s.w. was censored).

LIGHTING EXPERTS AGREE
that the qualities of Economy, Brilliancy and
Durability are essential in a satisfactory lamp.
Tests of various lamps give the following results :
1. CARBON LAMPS. This type proved durable
but deficient in economy and brilliancy —
therefore satisfactory only in one feature.
2. METAL LAMPS (pressed filament). Although
economical and brilliant, found lacking in
durability—satisfactory in two qualities.
3. MAZDA DRAWN WIRE LAMPS stood up
excellently on test, and possessed the three
qualities essential to complete satisfaction.

IN BANANA LAND
Eight men employed by the Brisbane
Water Board recently struck for higher
wages, on account of having to work in mud
Extra wages for working in mud? Nonsense!
Queensland politicians do it at the usual
pay, and you never hear a grumble from
them !
“Rangan”: Time is proving to Banana-
land the value of her scrub lands. The
largest and richest of them all, the Atherton
tableland, is receiving some of the attention
that has been owing to it for years. This
land consists of a patch of cedar scrub 130
miles long by 30 miles wide, and cedar scrub
soil is the richest of all scrub soils. The place
is about 3500 ft. above sea-level, with the
bracing climate that makes the dairyman’s
hehrt rejoice. Richmond River and South-
ern Queensland dairymen have gone there
in dozens in the last year or two,
and the results obtained have exceeded the
highest hopes. The soil grows any quantity
of paspalum, Rhodes grass, panicum and
clover, and one farmer’s cream returns for
June averaged 30s. per cow. This year a
butter-factory has been erected, and is ex-
pected to turn out, before the end of the
year, over 25 tons of butter a week.
NOBLESSE OBLIGE.
JOHNNIE : “Say, Chollie,
did you take my knife? ”
CHOLLIE : “What do you
mean? Sutfnly not!”
JOI.NNIE : “Suah?”
CHOLLIE : “ Take me dying oath!"
(A pause.)
JOHNNIE: “Say, Chollie, on yaw word of honnak?”
CHOLLIE: “Oh, dash it all, if you put it that way — beak's yaw knife."
JOHNNIE: “Thanks, Chollie; always said you weak man of honnak! ”
“Rangan” : Bananaland is preparing legis-
lation to increase the power of its stunted
District Courts. These institutions haven’t
been revised for 20 years, and badly need
repair. The chief objects of the alterations
are to give them power to take some of the
work off the hands of the Supreme Courts,
and thPs'save the time, trouble, expense and
delay of litigants. To make up for the addi-
tional toil D.C. judges are to have their
salaries increased from £lOOO to £l2OO,
which, considering the importance of the
work they do and the wage George Reid
draws, is not exactly munificent.- If now
Police Courts were revised and their
jurisdiction extended, affairs in Bananaland
would be about balanced. I don’t propose,
of course, that the Bobby Courts should pass
capital sentences on drunks. And whereas,
in .the old crude style of melodrama the
w/ongfully-convicted man who had escaped
from Dartmoor, with the fragments of his
life-sentence trailing behind him, used to
have his conviction cancelled and his estate
restored to him by the cop at the street
corner on production of the lost document,
I hold that it is better to stick to the old
stolid order of things. The cop at the-scorner
might be a cheap court, but he was unreli-
able. Still the idea of shifting a certain
amount of work to the inferior tribunals is
none the less meritorious.
The Wages.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Women do better work than men. They make
better dairymaids. Any member would sooner feel
the soft, white hand of a woman tonsorial artist
on his face than the rough grip of a man. A
woman was the best typist in the world, and he felt
sure that members would rather have a smart, dainty
woman to do their typewriting than a man. — M.L.A.
recently advocating equal pay for the sexes.
He was a legislator bold,
Who made a thrilling speech,
Wherein he pled with tears —and told
How dainty Dolly Peach,
Who toiled for half man’s daily wage
Could come in competition
With any males who till the age
With anger and sedition.
Could make the trousered tyrant yield,
Could win her way without him,
Could face the foe in open field,
Or turn his flank —and rout him.
“For instance,” said that orator —
“My chin-growth here, arboreal
This morn a buxom maiden shore
With lily hand tonsorial;
“And as she smoothed the tender face
Of Me —the Politician,
I found her all compact of grace
A shapely shearing vision.
“There is no man here would prefer
A masculine typewriter
With bristling beard in place of her
Who makes his office brighter.
“And how could man drop pen or plow
To supersede the fairy
Who pats the sow, or milks the cow
In well-conducted dairy?
“Where’er in this industrial mill
I meet the female toiler
I find her high in zeal and skill
Above the whiskered moiler;
“And in the lines where gentle touch
And kindly glance are needed
No man could ever give you such
A treat as charming She did!”
* * * *
That night that same Demosthenes
Was found abroad and raving—
“My missis turned me out to freeze
Because of Dolly’s shaving.
“She says she’ll pull my office down
On gentle typist Mary,
And show me up about that brown
Young hussy in the dairy;
“And when I plead : ‘That speech, my dear,
Was only ideality,’
She grips again my handiest ear
And cries : 'This is Reality!’
“No more I’ll champion ladies fair
Against the Wrongs of Ages,
I guess the sex that raised my hair
Can also raise its wages!”
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 15, 1914.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
This has been a most exciting week; a
jumble of patriotic meetings, races, fat
cattle, dust, noise and children. The last
four were banded together at the Show, of
which they seemed to be the greater part.
Mayoress Jenkinson has been making her
gentle voice heard at various patriotic
gatherings this week. On Thursday 257
women surged round the door of the Town
Hall and endeavored to squeeze themselves
into the deliberative room of our City
Gran’fers. Eventually, the Mayor, with
perspiration in his voice, announced that
the Albert Hall had been hurriedly borrowed
to meet the rush of business, whereupon
there was a great sound of swishing skirts
and a clatter of shoes making for the ren-
dezvous. Colonel Lee, the indefatigable
Mayor, Colonel Sutton, P.M.0., Colonel
Harris and Major Butler all made speeches,
and the meeting formed a Red Cross Society.
Also, it dropped £212 into the neat military
cap of Colonel Sutton. This, together with
as much more as the Society can gather in,
will be blewed in medical stores and com-
forts for the British Army, or for our defend-
ers in case of invasion. If any other Queens-
land women want to help, they should write
to the hon. sec., Mrs. L. M. Bond, Crescent-
road, Hamilton, Brisbane.
Mrs. William Collins, the pretty little
squatteress, has given £lOO to the society,
and, with Mesdames Ross-Munro, Cecil
Palmer and E. K. Tully, is arranging a big
ball for next Tuesday night.
Brisbane theatricals have, as usual,
generously given their time and money to
the good cause, and a big matinee, at which
every show in the city will be represented,
is to be held in H. M. Theatre on Tuesday
week. The proceeds go to the Mayor’s
patriotic fund.
The mgin social item of the week was the
QueensTSjud Club Ball on Tuesday night.
The whole top floor of Winchcombe, Car-
son’s big'wool store at Bulimba was used,
and for once in the social history of Bris-
bane several hundreds of people were able
to dance in comfort. The little hall at the
South Brisbane Technical College is about
the only prancing pavilion in town, and it
is far too small for requirements. The big
wool store looked like a botanical garden.
A small forest of palm trees must have been
slaughtered to line the walls and make the
pretty little sitting-out places. Sweet peas
were the floral decorations on the supper-
tables, and these were enhanced by white
chicken, pink candles, green and purple
salads and other delicacies. The band was
from Toowoomba. A. 11. Wittingham and
wife did the necessary with the official wel-
coming hand.
The races on Wednesday and again this
afternoon were largely attended. A run of
outsiders romped home to-day, and one
quadruped snared £3O for the lucky few who
backed him on the tote. If .they had been
doing business with a bookmaker they might
have snared as much as 30s. or else got a
back view of the bookie as he fled over the
horizon.
Dolores gave two concerts here this week,
and collected some truck-loads of flowers on
each occasion. On ( Tuesday afternoon Mrs.
Crase gave a tea-party in her honor, andi
on Friday the Brisbane Women’s Club per-
suaded her to a reception. Pianist Elsie-
Hall should have been there too, but she-
couldn’t come. So she was absent. Dolores
shed smiles on all the company and compli-
mented a few local amateurs who tickled'
the piano with their fingers or hit the old!
welkin with their voices. ■
Yours faithfully,
AUSTRLIA JANE.
Labor-leader Ryan: “We want to do some-
tiling.” Minister Blair: “You want to do*
somebody.” That’s the crux of Party Gov-
ernment —Bill trying to “do” Jim out of'
his job, and Jim defending it tooth and!
nail.
“Rangan” : They rather overdo tbe depu-
tation business in Bananaland. Recently
three people came 400 miles to Brisbane to
try to borrow £l7 from Treasurer Barnes..
They wanted the money to patch up a broken
road. The road certainly needed patching
(most Bananaland roads do), but the shire-
council which they represented could have
done a deal towards it, if not the lot, by
bogging in with the money wasted on the
jaunt to Brisbane. Incidentally the deputa-
tion arrived just when the city had on its
glad rags and top hat to attend the annual
Show. This may or may not have had
something to do with the case. Anyhow,
Barnes hit the request to the boundary with-
out unnecessary apology.
“Lex”: Cabled to-day (17/8,/’l4) that
Japan has delivered an ultimatum to Ger-
many to get out of Kiao-Chau. This may be
taken to mean that Nippon is in the present
conflict for keeps. A declaration of war is
not an essential nowadays. An ultimatum
may take its place. Hostilities commence on
the hour fixed in the ultimatum, and the
subsequent declaration, if one is issued,
amounts merely to an intimation to neutrals
that a state of war exists.
“The Old Soldier”: How little we have
heard in the present scrap about the new
weapons—the “cheap submarine,” the mis-
sile torpedo, the Zeppelin and the aeroplane.
A submarine has been sunk, a few ’planes
have been used for reconnoitring—and that
is about all. Some of us were not quite
unprepared to find the torpedo rather less
effective than it is supposed to be. In the
Jap-Russ war, Togo’s flagship, the Mikasa,
is said to have been torpedoed by mistake,
but it was not much the worse. In the
Balkan war the Turkish cruiser Hamidieli.
was torpedoed in the bow by the Bulgarians..
Repaired by tbe Turks, it was making his-
tory in the Mediterranean within three-
weeks. Science doesn’t seem to be in it,.
after all, with a steady, reliable supply of'
human meat.
“Hamer”: A peculiar situation lias been-
created by the death of Gregor McGregor
in regard to the Senate elections in S.A..
The passing of McGregor automatically
cancels his nomination. Every Labor voter
will have to give his sixth vote to one or
the other of the Liberal six. In such a case
it is likely that one of the least promising
Liberals will be selected, because he will
bring tbe least intelligence into the enemy’s
camp. Many Laborites believe that they
are still entitled to cast a vote for the dead
hero, but even if they elected him there
would be a vacancy, which will be filled by
the South Australian Parliament nominat-
ing the losing Liberal with the highest tally
of votes. Apparently each party should
have a dummy candidate or understudy
whose votes don’t count unless one of the
real candidates passes out while the election
is in progress. That is to say, the under-
study candidate should vote for himself, but
no one else should be allowed to vote for
him unless he is wanted.
[Harold Mercer, Katoomba, N.S.W.]

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken 0/ communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
W. Ingers: In this paper’s opinion the
purest English isn’t spoken anywhere in par-
ticular. Pure English is largely a matter of
individual, opinion. Thus Alderman Thomas Henley,
of Sydney, considers that his method of employing
the language is correct, while Arthur Balfour, M.P.,
of London, would probably commit suicide rather
than talk like Henley. Boston (U.S.), which uses
phrases like “gotten,” is as satisfied that it is right
as "Oxford (England) ; and Shakspeare would need
an interpreter to understand either of them. ... J.R.:
Thafks. When things settle down, it might be
worth while going into the case with a view to
getting the youngster some relief. Let’s hear from
you then. .. .T. Tabby: It was a good yarn once. . ...
Archibald F. : Wind Billy: True, maybe; but
there’s no hope of saying so just n0w....Le0 B.:
When a yarn is returned in that way it may be
taken as a hint that the editor, though he didn’t
want it himself, thought it might be worth submit-
ting somewhere else.... Old Reader: (1) Yes; the
information was given to the captain of the Swan-
hilda. The papers that were handed him in mid-
ocean showed that the police knew Butler to be
aboard as a seaman. The captain and the mate kept
the news to themselves until the Swanhilda was
boarded at the Golden Gate; (2) Senior-detective
Roche (now Superintendent of Detectives), Detective
Mcllattie and Constable Conroyd. .. . Yank : Sir
William Johnson was born in Ireland, and went to
U.S.A. in 1738. As a fur trader and soldier he
acquired great influence amongst the Indians, whom
he often led against the French. It was he who
enlisted and equipped (under instructions) the Six
Nations (Iyoquois) against French Canada. After-
the death of his wife, Catherine Wisenberg, he had
many mistresses, amongst them Molly Brant, sister of
the famous chief, Joseph Brant. Can’t swear she was
styled Lady Molly, but it is probably correct....
G.: No interest in the bygone aviation prediction of
Kepler that Germans would yet inhabit the moon.
Perhaps he was right. They appear likely to go
sky-high, anyhow. ... D. 0.: Can't republish....
Henley: Not known to this office. Shouldn’t think
it was right in the sense your letter conveys....
Brumby: It’s a good story, spoiled by the fact that
there's a wages-board award in that industry, and the
young women get the benefit of it whether they are
unionists or merely loafers on the unionism of others.
. ...J.M.: The Peacocks who make jam and the
Peacocks who make strange noises are different birds
altogether. .. .Cowslip: The Commonwealth Govern-
ment has nothing to do with land laws ; but so far
as N. S. AVales is concerned there’s a provision more
liberal in most respects than the S’African land-
purchase law mentioned. .. .F.E.W.: No use to
us. Try the public hangman. .. .Isabel M. M.:
Sorry verses not strong enough. Verses that
can stand on their own feet are at present in
most demand. .. .Bayonet: It is rather late to start
shouting “Prepare!” Write something about “For-
ward!”... .William F. G.: In a time of excursions
and alarums, you arrive as an extra infliction.
Can’t you join something or go somewhere in a
tremendous hurry ? Go and annoy the enemy....

tion with violets and paddocks is pleasing in this

and your paddocks du11.... Sapper: “Where are my

There seems to be prevalent among hotel
managers an intense dislike to the guests
drinking water in their rooms; and to dis-
courage the practice usually only one glass is
placed in each bedroom. This, of course, is
generally used for laundrying the teeth, and
nobody but a person of the depraved tastes
m
of a German Emperor
would care to use for
a drinking vessel the
same utensil with
which he conducts his
teeth-scrubbing. And
even where there are
two glasses provided,
no effort is made to
distinguish which are
jaw-baths and which
are water glasses. All
good mouth washes
have flavors which
linger long and cling
hard, and even when
there is nothing to dis-
tinguish the molar bath from the drinking
glass they are bound to get used indis-
criminately. What is required is a glass for
mouth scrubbing which is obviously intended
for that only. Difference in shape is not
sufficient. There should be some easily-
recognised distinguishing mark, which would
be understanded of by all men. The Odol
Company, of London, is offering such a glass
—a specially-designed toilet tumbler, with
the word “Odol” plainly etched on the side—
and, as all civilised people connect “Odol”
and teeth, the use is obvious. The eo.
supplies these glasses at quite a nominal
price.*

EARPHONE
GIVES PERFECT HEARING
The object of the Danjhill
Earphone is to enable
deaf people to hear.
By simply wearing a Danjhill Earphone a deaf person can sit in a room and hear
all the conversation with perfect ease. It collects sounds from all directions without
losing any of their strength.
It transmits the voice clearly, and can be used in a Public Hall, Theatre or Church.
For years past Jerdans Ltd. have been successfully attending to the eye
troubles of the public.
If your eyes fail you naturally take to spectacles.
Why not let us treat your ears with the Danjhill Earphone? The result
will be perfect.

The Vic. Public Service offices had a
lovely joke last week. One or two conse-
quential blokes at the Treasury building had
drawn up a circular laden with questions as
to what kind of a patriot the recipient would
like to be —soldier, special constable or what
not. This was an attempt to get in front
of the military recruiting officers, and its
only apparent cause was the author’s desire
for advt. The circular was solemnly para-
graphed in the dailies, and the juniors in
the service sent in their answers without
questioning the authority of the bloke who
was worrying them. But one day a skit on
the uncalled-for document was posted up in
the Treasury, and before the authorities
could tear the thing down it had sufficed to
blow out the circular aforesaid. The skit
had a list of 19 questions to be answered,
beginning with “What is your fighting
weight?” and ending with “Can you state
the policy of Cook.” One of the 17 inter-
mediate questions was “Have you done any
barrow pushing?”
Moltke, the nephew of the great strategist,
who is now Chief of the German General
Staff, is one of the few who has had the
nerve to stand up to the Kaiser and tell him
when he was acting foolishly. One of Wil-
liam’s fads was to lead cavalry charges at
manoeuvres. Moltke stopped this, declaring
that charges by masses of cavalry were not
practical tactics, and were calculated to
cause losses out of all proportion to anything
that could be gained by them. He also in-
sisted on night operations and night marches
being constantly practised. The cable tells
both of night work and of charges made by
great masses of German cavalry resulting in
heavy loss. If these stories are true it would
seem that in at least one respect the Empe-
roar has overborne the judgment of his Chief
of Staff. But the war cable is notoriously
our most prominent liar nowadays.
Thus Melbourne Akgus :
Faced by war, Australians recognise that at the
very least they must submit to diminished revenues
and increased taxation. If need arises they are
prepared to see their country bled white by the
drain of its resources in order to preserve its integ-
rity and that of the Empire with which it stands or
falls.
Yet when Fisher proposed to spend coin on
unifying the railway gauges and doing other
important defence work, the Argus wailed
that unless the electors trod heavily on
such hair-brained proposals, there was mil-
lions of defence taxation looming ahead —-
and Australia was expected to shudder at
the very thought of it. The Argus is quite
willing that Australia should be bled white,
apparently; but with its big, broad views it
insists that its own handy-man shall have
the job of sticking the pig.
[B. J. H. Owen, 108 Albert-street, East Melbourne.]
The Ardath Tobacco Company, the pro-
prietors of the well-known State Express,
Winfred and Splendo Cigarettes, and the
famous Ardath Smoking Mixtures, has taken
the lead in harnessing scientific methods to
the needs of Commerce. To the State Ex-
press Motor Delivery Van it has,' after
much experimenting, added a complete
wireless installation by means of which the
traveller on his daily round is enabled t©>
communicate at any time of th» day with
the head offices in Finsbury. In this way
the urgent requirements of the Company’s
customers are dealt with more speedily than
is possible even by the use of the telephone,
and the facilities so afforded have already
been taken extensive advantage of. The State
express car is fitted with a complete trans-
mitting set. capable of getting into communi-
cation with the head office within a radius
of 20 miles. The Company has experimented
for a considerable time in this direction, and
many difficulties have had to be overcome
before satisfactory connections could be ob-
tained. An ingenious feature of the installa-
tion is the method adopted for completing the
circuit and making “an earth” by dropping
a metal chain on to metallic manholes or
drains, or by trailing same in water.*
