“Heave ho”: The 8.1.’s new tug Coringa
has just put up a fine performance—almost
a record for this part of the world. It.
passed a line - aboard the disabled Cairnhill
at Namatamai, in the German Archipelago,
on July 9, and pointed its nose for Australia.
What with heavy weather, the foul con-
dition of the Cairnhill (it had been lying
with a snapped tail-shaft since March) and
the SOOO tons of phosphates. and coal on
board, the first 500 miles glided past at the
rate of 2.S knots per hour. Finer weather
then fell out of the packet of samples and
the next 800 miles were done at four knots.
Townsville was reached on July 26, and
given reasonable conditions it was reckoned
that they would tie up to Sydney about
August 12. The only better tow that I re-
member was from Broome (W.A.) to Syd-
ney, via Torres Strait, in ’99. The Flinders,
an Adelaide co.’s tug, struggled with the
Serina, which had been bought from the
underwriters for a song. And it may not
have been a better tow, either; for though
the pull was longer there was no dead
weight aboard and the weather devils were
asleep.
“Ricardo”: Sydney and Melbourne, stand
up! The 173rd lesson is to begin. For the
past year London County Council’s Tram-
ways show a deficiency of £BB,OOO, after the
statutory charges have been paid. This is
the first year to end with any deficiency at
all! So much for the war which foolish
authority allowed Private Enterprise to wage
with the public through the motor-’bus. Of
the debt of £12,000,000 London has paid off
less than £8.500,000, and has been mad
enough to allow a tricky lot of private capi-
talists to play havoc with the assets repre-
senting the still-unpaid lOU for £8,500,000.
Sydney and Melbourne, go back to your
places— -and don't moke fools of yourselves.
* * * *
“Briner”: The German commercial navy
has the finest sailing vessels in the world,
trading - between Hamburg and South
America chiefly. They are manned by none
but Germans —crews such as used to be in
our Australasian clippers, like the Sovereign
of the Seas. Lightning. Red Jacket and
R'angatikei. Such of these vessels as hap-
pen to be on the ocean will be in grave
danger, for Britain lias a svvarm of cruisers
not required in the North Sea, where the
business is mainly for Dreadnoughts. The
commercial steam,er can trust much to her
heels. If there is smoke on the horizon she
is off, and the stern chase, with steam in
both, is long. With a sailer the case is very
different. What may happen to Germany,
once Britain masters the sea, may be
guessed at from the fact that the then
rapidly-growing United States commercial
navy was ruined by the Civil War. and in
nearly half a century has recovered but
little, the trade having gone to the British
and German flags. Now, the Americans
are out to grab it from the Germans. So
the whirligig of time brings its revenges.
“Carbon”: Sydney and Newcastle com-
mercial men were thrown into a state of
anti-hallelujah on August 7. when a notifica-
tion was issued by the Customs that coal
was not to be carried coastwise. The notice
posted up read : “Articles prohibited include
Sacks, Coal, Silk Thread, Silk Braid,” etc.
For three hours the Customs Houses in
Sydney and Newcastle were in a state of
siege. Arrangements were about to com-
mence to lay up the collier fleet and close
some of the mines, when a bright official
discovered that two small signs had been
omitted. The notice should have read:
“Sacks (coal), Silk Thread.” etc. When
the error was corrected the coal-dealing
community went out and had a drink and
holders of gas shares countermanded their
orders to sell. But it was a torrid three
hours while it lasted.
* * * *
“jVloana” : The West Coast of Maoriland
is inhaling great quantities of hope because
a company, with much Sydney cash behind
it, is about to put a powerful gold dredge on
the Aliaura River. The vessel, which has
been working on the Macquarie River, is a
50-li.p. plant wfitli 7ft. buckets, and is capa-
ble of lifting and treating 16,000 cubic yards
per week. During the last M.L. dredging
boom a small and inefficient struggler worked
this claim, and in three years took
out £lO.OOO worth of metal. As this baby
mammoth was incapable of breaking the
ground in any quantity or doing anything
like justice to the claim, big things are ex-
pected of the new plant, which is a full-
grown mammoth. The Aliaura River cuts
a belt of gold-bearing country which, during
the boom, supported four dredges and yielded
over £400,000 worth of sovereigns. If the com-
pany succeeds, it contemplates building three
more dredges to scratch the ground. At pre-
sent it is brimming with confidence in the
future. The trouble is that the future has
such a horrible habit of turning out dark and
dismal and filled with cursing.
« * * *
“Knocks” : The world is turning to gall in
the mouth of the Colonial Sugar Co. The
taste was bad enough when the Australian
cast out the colored laborer from the north-
ern eanefields. Still, the gentle Fijian
remained to the co. as a sweet savor. But
now the sweet savor has turned sour and
told the horrified co. that it wants five bob
a shift for handling cargo by day, and ten
bob —ten bob —if the w T ork is done at night.
Ten bob for a nigger! Alas it is true: in
its agony the co. discovers that the Fijian is
a nigger.
* * * *
“Hamer”: Prison administration in
N.S.W. is a completely changed thing, and
it badly wanted to be changed. On the
North Coast an area has been set aside for
tree-planting by prisoners, and the experi-
ment has proved such a success that the
Government is extending the programme by
the purchase of an area at Emu Plains,
where prisoners are to be put to work grow-
ing fruit and vegetables for the public in-
stitutions. The gaol loafer is, in fact,
threatened with utter abolition. Already
the man who goes to prison on account of
his disregard for domestic obligations is not
suffered to waste his time in idleness: he is
put to work, and a portion of the money he
earns goes to the suonort of his neglected
family—the sanest thing in penal reform
ever attempted.

A Ballad of the Purple
Parramatta.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BN the purple Parramatta, where the
nascent morning dawns
Over dun-brown - boiling-down works
and grey wool-scouring lawns,
•Just beyond the classic bone-nulls built by
Alley, Pose & Co.,
Dwelt the hero of this poem, James Adolphus
Pummeloe.
James Adolphus was a simple, kindly, lanky
sort of chap,
)\ itli a vacuous expression and a nose shaped
like a tap ;
At the early age of twenty James Adolphus
got a job
A ith the Parramatta Council, under sub-
inspector Blobb.
Mister Blobb, the sub-inspector was a very
solemn man,
\N ho took life in deadly earnest on the
strictest Baptist plan ;
He was trusted by the Council —solemn per-
sons always are;
And he sub-inspected strongly in a dinky
motor-car.
Almost everything that cluttered Parramatta
Mister Blobb
Sub-inspected once fortnightly ; for, you see,
it was liis job;
And wherever it might happen Mister Blobb
might choose to go
He was always sure to carry James Adol-
phus Pummeloe.
In the long, hot, dusty summer of a recent
year of grace,
Parramatta, once historic, grew a most
hysteric place ;
For there came a Mighty Odor —an Objec-
tionable Smell —
Which persisted and insisted till the people
felt unwell.
'Twas a wonderful ha!it an —penetrating,
silent, strong,
Yet elusive and . diffusive like an operatic
song;
It was also highly complex, chromotropic,
yet obscure;
And it shared the gift old loans have —it
seemed able to endure.
When the Mayor and Council smelled it —as
they did in course of time —
They requested the police-force to investigate
the crime,
Which (they said) they felt had happened
somewhere in their happy town ;
And the Mayor said, with great feeling,
’twas an insult to the Crown!
For the supposititious corpse the force
looked all in vain ;
• Every morning Parramatta smelled the
Same Old Smell again ; /
And complaints poured in from Robinson.
and likewise Smith and Jones,
Sarcastically hinting that the Mayor should
shift his bones.
After frequent consultations, cogitations and
remarks —•
Also “lively” Council meetings—and the
searching of the parks,
Gardens, back-yards, drains and foreshores
of the municipal bound
It was solemnly decided that the Smell could
not be found.
Then the patience of the townsfolk, greatly
tried, at last gave way;
And they formed a deputation to approach
the Mayor and say
That the thing was gettiug boresome; some-
one had to do the job ;
And his Worship pronmtly answered: “Send
for sub-inspector Blobb !”
When that solemn sub-inspector, with his
Baptist face and air.
Stood before the troubled Council it was
promptly to declare
That the only course to follow was to show
a contrite heart.
And repent them of their sins, and vow to
make another start.
But, although some half-applauded, it was
very quickly guessed
By the Council that the problem that way
wouldn’t go to rest:
The position called for action —here an
alderman arose
And suggested the employment of a man
who had a nose.
“Ret him take a skiff and follow every wind-
ing of the creek”—
fSaicL that fertile-minded alderman) —“e’en
though it take a week.
If he rows about the river for a month or
two lie’s bound
To locate this horrid odor — and ire'll pro-
secute when found.”
Great applause met the suggestion; but.
which man should do the job?
Some said. “Leave it to flit- Mayor!” Others
mentioned Mister Blobb :
But that worthy sub-inspector under inspi-
ration's glow
1 Cried. “I know the man. yer Washup—James
Adolphus Pummeloe!”
Straight they sent for James Adolphus;
when the Council saw his nose
It was felt that here was one who could the
mystery disclose; '
And without a day's delay they fitted out a
skiff for James
And despatched him on his mission to dis-
cover smells and shames.
Past the bone-mills of his boyhood—past the
soap works paddled he;
Past the spntie tanks and gasworks, and the
new fell monger y :
Past the tannery owned by Flannery, and Ihe
glueworks. in tin 1 glow
Of the rosy-crested sunset paddled .T. A.
Pummeloe.
Gallant soul! lie never faltered in liis hard,
smellifluous task ;
No—not even when he reached the spot
where very dead dogs bask.
And the ancient fish lies sleeping in the
Parramatta ooze—
Alas! he failed; though now he'd find, and
now, again, he'd lose.
Sore spent and baffled oft he rowed, his tap-
shaped nose spread wide
To catch the quaint effluvia that meandered
o’er the tide ;
In vain! That complex and elusive Odor of
tile heat
Completely puzzled faithful James; in short,
it had him beat!
And so he gave it up? Not he! No, no!
Good, faithful lad ;
At duty's oar for evermore he rows and rows
like mad :
Where the purple Parramatta in the rosy
sunset glows
You’ll see Adolphus Pummeloc with elevated
nose.
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
When the War Started.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HENRY SEA BOTTOM had never
troubled himself about the fabled
world that was supposed by religious
mermen to exist in a dry fashion miles above
his head. As a prominent sandlord and
alderfish, Henry had a reputation to uphold,
lie did not want his fellow-mermen to get
the idea that his head was filled with vague
and unprofitable speculations about other
worlds. It was well enough for curate-fish
and sea-astronomers to go about with their
mouths open —that was their job. Bui
Henry classed himself as a practical fish, with
investments and sand to look after, and lie
never allowed his mind to wander from
those subjects, not even when he was swim-
ming to church with his belltopper gleaming
phosphoresccntly and his family wriggling
behind him.
Henry had a nice house in Sea Channel, a
suburb where the sea-serpent service was
good. If he missed the 8.45 serpent to town,
be simply sat on his tail, opened his Morn-
ing Cuttlefish, and waited for the 8.50.
In fact, there were so many serpents (some of
them swimming express) that his acquaint-
ance with the time-table was very limited.
His friends, too, were numerous, weighty
and of regular habits; and he found that, by
varying his serpents judicimisly, he could
manage to have a chat with everyone once a
fortnight, and thereby gain a good first-hand
knowledge of what was going forwaul. liis
whole life was oidered in this careful
manner, for Henry Seabottom was not the
sort of oyster to miss any chances, however
small. lie was generally regaided by his
business associates as being as keen as a
swordfish, and was revered accordingly; but
the curate-fish, though very grateful for his
numerous donations of shell to the church,
used to heave bubbling sighs of regret when-
ever Mrs. Seabottom spoke with regret of
her husband's preoccupation with the world
about him. The constant cry of the curate-
fish was, "I wish he could be given a Sign!
I cannot help fretting about him, Mrs. Sea-
bottom, because 1 have a sincere regard for
him. The sea would be a Better Place if we
had more fish like him!” Despite all this,
Henry continued to be preoccupied with his
sand sales and talked business with his pals
on the morning and evening sea-serpent.
Henry had one child, a daughter, who was
tin apple of his protruding eye. He wor-
shipped the very sand she swam over, and
worshipfully swallowed the bubbles that
marked her track. He gave her everything
she wanted —all but the useless young poet-
fish who used to call round in the evenings
and read the wild romantic nonsense he had
composed about fights in other worlds and
still, speechless, staring things with two
tails apiece who drifted down from the
water-sky and lay about until removed by the
ancient town council at considerable expense.
The poet-fish, himself, lialt'-believed in these
things, and, of course. Henry’s girl thought
they were simply lovely. But Henry him-
s< If thought the writing of such imaginative
nonsense a criminal waste of time. The
impudence of a fish of the kind aspir-
ing to his daughter sometimes moved him to
such a pitch of fury that he was unable to
sit quietly in the parlor, opening and shutting
his mouth, but had to swim quickly to and
fro. Henry would have been hard put to it
to conceal his delight if the evening poet-fish
had been taken by a shark, or run over and
killed by the sea-serpent. Once, indeed, he
nearlv ordered him to swim out of the house
and never lighten his door phosphorescent!? 7
again. Only a tearful appeal by bis wife
prevented him from taking this extreme
measure. She reminded him that, after all.
lie was only trying to secure bis daughter's
happiness. Why ruin it by turning out this
foolish young fish, who might possibly become
sensible in time? Henry, like a reasonable
fish, yielded, and even managed to contain
himself while the poet fish fastened
on to him and started to tell him
how the fabled drowned men with two
tails who had cqme down from tlx' water-sky
in the long ago had been bound about
the chests with polished steel. With a'dmir-
able self-restraint, he curtly told the foolish
young poet-fish to keep all such observations
for his daughter, who was young and could
be imposed on. For himself, he was just a
practical fish, and the less nonsense fish
talked the better he liked them. From that
time on. the young poet-fish, whenever he
encountered Henry Seabottom. talked sand-
sales exclusively: and. although he didn’t
talk verv brilliantly on that line. Henry met
him half-way and condescended to instruct
him and be very boring. But once in the
drawing-room (or drawing-pool) the poet-
fish would commence again with his
legends, and Henry’s daughter would fall
into a sort of trance and admire the poet-
fish's eyes.
One morning Henry Seabottom discovered
that the kitchen chimney was blocked up.
At first glance it doesn’t look the sort of thing
that shoubl change the whole course of a
merman’s life, upset all his carefully matured
views, and drive him out of his intellectual
trenches. But the fact remains. Tim block-
ing up of Henry’s chimney was the beginning
of the end of him. He investigated with the
longest household ladder, and. after much

A woman has more than her share of burdens in life.
The care of the house, the needs of the family, the making
and mending —and constant anxiety for everybody round —
except herself.
It is not surprising that women are subject to kidney
weakness —their kidneys cannot stand the strain that’s forced
upon them.
Backache from morn till night, worn and tired before
mid-day with headache, dizziness —to stoop is painful, and to
rise again is torture.
Sallowness, weak heart, sleeplessness, sciatica, rheuma-
tism, dropsy, gravel, stone are outcomes of diseased and tired
kidneys.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills act diredlly upon the

out a perfect stranger who tallied in nearly
every way with the descriptions supplied by
the mentally crippled poet-fish. Every scale
on Henry’s tail stood up on end with horror
and astonishment. He had acquired the very
thing he had always denounced as impossible
and absurd, and the fish who would probably
laugh loudest was the one for whom he had
the most profound contempt. Balanced pre-
cariously on his tail on the top rung of the
laddei’,. and with the dreadful exhibit waving
beside him, lie asked himself a series of
questions. The first question was: “Am I
mad?’’ That got a hearty negative, for
Henry Seabottom’s belief in his own sanity
was tremendous. The next question was:
“Is it real?” After furtively considering It
from all angles, Henry muttered, “I don’t
believe it is!” Then he drew himself up,
opened his mouth and bubbled, “Who put
this effigy in the chimney?” The answer at
once stx’eamed into his mind: “That d —d
poet!” Looking hurriedly around to make
certain that no fish had seen him, he
descended the laddei-, concealed the body in
the weedshed, and went off, bubbling ven-
geance, to look for his px-actical joker.
As Henry swam down the street to the
sea-serpent station, he promised himself that
he would do something serious to the accursed
poet-fish when he caught him. But his fury
cooled somewhat when he remembered that
it was just the sort of thing the poet-fish
wouldn't do. If the Thing at home in the
weedshed wasn’t i*eal, then it was a wondei’-
ful imitation, and the poet-fish must be either
rich or industrious —and Henry knew he was
neither. What if Mrs. Seabottom should
chance to swim out to the weedshed and
discover it! Henry had almost decided to
go back and stow it away when he recol-
lected that it was washing day at home. He
swam on in a hot perspiration, carefully
avoiding the eye of the local sergeant-fish,
who was waggling his fins in an idle manner
at the street cornei\ He pui'cliased his
Mobning Cuttlefish as usual, but, during
the journey into town, it lay unopened on
his lap. The Thing in the shed at home
was becoming more realistic every minute,
and, by the time the sea-serpent ran into the
central station the mild poet-fish he
had set out to discover and assail
was the very last fish he wanted to
meet. He decided to buy theatre
tickets for his entii-e family for that evening,
and have the Thing removed and buried
befoi-e they got home again. He sighed with
relief when he thought how near he had
been to unbosoming himself to the half-witted
and unreliable poet-fish. Suppose he had
rushed like a fool to that babbling idiot and
revealed bis dreadful secret? Why, the news
would have been all over town in an hour,
and he would have been humiliated and pro-
bably ruined ! .
Before Henry swam into his town office
he composed his face (Henry was no
musician, but he could compose his own face
twice a day if necessary), and waited till
his tail had slowed down to a dignified beat.
Then he swirled in and bumped into his
partner Ray, who had a paper and seemed
excited.
“See the special edition?” asked Ray.
Henry said he hadn’t.
“Here you are, then ! They’ve discovered
a merman with two tails! What's the
matter?”
Henry sank into his chair and buried his
face in his chest-fins : “I—l’m not well!.. ..
Where did they”
“Out here in the street! It’s the greatest
discovei-y of the age. Fish are fighting to
see it!”
Henry was a fish of great recupei-ative
powers. He was also a fish of iron will.
He had ironed out too many fish not to know
an opportunity when he saw it. He rose on
his tail-tip and struck an attitude.
“Feel better?” queried his partner.
Henry blew a few bubbles with his mouth
and made his scales sparkle. “Well,” he
said, “I don’t need to fight to see their
double-tailed merman. I’ve got one at home
in the weedshed!”
His partner wriggled forward and shot his
eyes out. “Where did you get it?”
But Henry did not give away secrets easily
like that. He liked to make other fish stand
off and say he was a Marvel. “Never you
mind ! I’ve not only got one of my own,
but I know a young fish who can tell you
more about these things than any other fish
in town !” Then, in order that some shark
should not get in ahead of him and secure
the young fish-poet, he took his hat and said
condescendingly to his partner: “Come along
and I’ll introduce you to him.” And his
partner, who was frightfully interested, and
loved a free treat, bubbled gratefully:
“Thanks, I’d love to!” They hurried out
together, only pausing at the Giant Clam
Safe Deposit to get some papers Ray had
left thei’e.
The young fish-poet wms found at last in
a library doing something particularly use-
less; but Henry Seabottom greeted him no
less warmly on that account. He had col-
lected a few of his best friends as he pro-
gressed and they made an impressive depu-
tation. all of them being fish of great bulk.
The young poet-fish drew back when he first
saw the mass of respectability advancing in
close formation; but when Henry had ex-
plained their mission, he wrung his fins with
pleasui-e and swam off at top speed with them
in close pursuit. They wei’e lucky enough
to just get a through sea-serpent and were
quickly landed on Henry’s home-station.
But, as they approached his home, they saw
that, the house was surrounded by a large
and glittering collection of excited fish.
They forced their way through the press to
the inner cordon of police-fish; and there
they found what all the trouble was about.
The merman had been found and was lying
of the verandah in full view of the street!
Several police-fish had it in charge and were
arguing as to what they should do with it.
Mrs. Seabottom was distractedly blowing
bubbles and wringing her fins with shame
and confusion, while her daughter was clap-
ping her fins with delight and proclaiming
that “Herbert must be told at once.” When
Herbert burst from the crowd, she swam to
his side like a flash and led him, wriggling
with pleasure, to the prostrate exhibit.
“There you are, dear! It’s all come true —
every word that you said! Father won’t
ever be rude to you again !”
They bent lovingly over the strange,
silent, pale thing with the strange red
opening in its chest. Henry took
chai-ge in his accustomed manner. As
an experienced aldei-fish, he knew how to
make impromptu speeches about things of
which he knew nothing; and this was too
good an opportunity to be missed. It was
a splendid speech. Henry took up the atti-
tude of a public benefactor who had just
made a valuable contribution to science and
wanted to remain anonymous—or as anony-
mous as possible under the circumstances.
He mentioned in a tremendous voice that he
didn’t want his name mentioned and that
wimt he had done had been done without
any thought of gain. (Loud applause.) He
handed over this unique specimen—this deni-
zen of another world —he handed it over
without reservation of any kind to the
State. (Tremendous cheering.) He consid-
ered that in doing so he was only doing his
duty. (“No, no!” and “Yes!”) lie con-
sidered that every fish should do his duty.
(Cheers.) And he knew that every fish
there would do his duty when the time came.
(Deafening applause.) But for himself—
(More cheering for himself) —for himself he
was quite satisfied to have the love and
esteem of his fellow-fish. (Hear, hear!)
And now he would call upon his young friend
Mr. Sofafish to give them the benefit of his
wide and unrivalled knowledge of the world
from which this wonderful —this uniquely
wonderful—specimen had come. (Great
cheering.) His dear friend and associate,
Mr. Sofafish, had devoted the best part of his
life to this fascinating study, and he was
sure they would listen to him with the
greatest attention. (Cheers.) He thanked
them once again for their touching expres-
sions of confidence and good-will, and assured
them that he would not accept the slightest
rewai’d from the State for the specimen—no
matter what its value might be. (Loud
cheers.) As reoresentatives of the State
there assembled, he presented them with the
specimen. (Roars of joy.)
Mr. Sofafish wriggled forward and faced
the cheering thousands. He was just about
to open his mouth when something drifted
slowly down from the sky and settled before
him on the sandy front garden. It was another
specimen exactly like the one on the veran-
dah ! The crowd roared again, and swayed
to and fro. Henry Seabottom rose up on
his tail and stared. Sofafish bubbled with
insane joy and claimed it. as being nearest
to it when it fell. And those were the last
words that were heard for some time, for at
that instant specimens in hundreds came
twisting and writhing down from the sky,
and it was a spry young fish that didn’t get
a few scales knocked off through being
bumped by the heavy, helpless things. The
temper of the fish ci’owd changed in an in-
stant, and instead of roailng applause it
screamed with anger and derision. Henry
Seabottom and Sofafish waved their fins on
the verandah and tried to explain ; but they
might as well have tried to explain to a herd
of mad whales. Old and furious fish could
be heard denouncing Henry as a humbug
for solemnly presenting rubbish to the State,
and young fish ui'ged him to go and get
netted. These remarks were screamed at
them as they watched from shelter the
frightened crowd dodging the bundles that
came hurtling down by twos and threes and
discoloring the water. Sofafish (a fool to
the last) was trying to tell old Henry some
old myth about legendary beings who lived
up in another world where all was dry and
hot and no mortal fish could exist, and how
these beings or gods sometimes fought one
another with iron and fire (fire was the
stuff they fried the moiming bacon with, in-
stead of drowning it in a pot fish-fashion)
and how, when these gods or devils were
killed at sea (sea wasn’t their element, but
they went on it for some silly reason), they
couldn’t swim, and that this shower showed
thex-e was a fight away up in the next world.
All this and much more the half-witted Sofa-
fish screamed into the gills of his beloved’s
father, instead of firing on the family cuttle-
fish and obscuring the issue sufficiently to
enable the Seabottom family and liimseif to
escape the frenzied mob. The consequence
was that Henry Seajbottom and he had to
stand at bay until an enormous shadow
scared away the mob in one glittering rush.
Sofafish and Henry, being two different sorts
of fools, didn’t take the cue, but instead
stayed to ask each other what the matter
was. And the delay was fatal, for next
instant the warship that had been sunk in
action settled down cx-ushingly on the house
and all in it. The others were killed
instantly. But Henry lingered long enough
to have a few words with the first rescue
party. He said he had never done any harm
to any fish, and that the sea was becoming
unfit to live in. And then he died in it.
Moral: Some of our present misfortunes may
be passed on.
N.s.w.
LEADING SEAMAN.
My Standpoint.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS gay old giddy world of ours
Is full of joy and beer and flowers;
And beer, or joy, or flowers I choose,
Uncaring who these things refuse ;
And yet a man who next door houses
Looks o’er my fence at me and wowses.
This wowser filled with holy wx-ath,
Abjures my pleasures. Life is froth;
I am Content to take a drop
Which bears its only froth on top,
But this man never yet I've chided
Because he did not do as I did.
I do not look across his fence
With piety as my pretence
For interference in his cheer,
And urge him to indulge in beer,
Or shun the dangers of the choir,
And fix his thoughts on something higher.
If I should slumber at his door,
And kill his rest with song or snoi’e,
Or drop a flounder in his milk,
Or any. way his pleasure bilk,
lie has his remedy in storming.
But I resent his d —d reforming.
Some day I’ll meet him in a lane,
And, holding him by might and main,
Will pour his holy gullet down
Some seven pints of liquor brown.
To show I know will be my labor
His sense of duty to a neighbor.
N.S.W.

The importance of efficient insulation, in many cases, is not realised. Great
care is shown in the selection of a steam plant to ensure the greatest economy,
yet the insulation which is of equal importance is in many cases partly neg-
lected. If you strive to make a saving in the steam consumption of your
engine, why not make certain that it is not wasted again through faulty in-
sulation? The claims we make as to the exceptional efficiency of Newall’s
Coverings are fully borne out by actual test, and, further, by the enormous
sales, including repeat orders, and we do not know of a single instance where
any other covering has been used once Newall’s have been tried.
The Ultimo and White Bay Power Houses are insulated throughout with
Newall’s Coverings, and this material has also been adopted exclusively by the
N.S.W. and Victorian Governments. These are facts which will tend to fur-
ther prove our claims. The innumerable advantages of this material over all
other forms of lagging are described in interesting booklet, which we will be
